Socialistic Verses
The Red Page
THE LAND SALE.
Behold, upon the wind-swept ways they rear
The tattered ensign of the hosts of greed,
Who batten on their brothers’ homing need.
Across the moorland echoes far and near
The jargon of the loud-tongued auctioneer,
Bewildering the birds that build and breed
Upon their Makers moor sans title-deed.
Ye timorous things! what is it that ye fear?
What though men sell this flower-filled land,
arid mar
Thy heather ed havens with unverdured
street,
And hide the gracious grass with sand and
tar
To pave a way for rent-collecting feet, *
Be comforted, ye freedom-nurtured things,
For God hath other moors, and ye have
wings.
RAIL AND ROAD.
Adventurous weeds upon the railroad stray
Among the sleepers, vagrants that reveal
How swiftly Nature such a hurt could heal,
Clothing again with bough, and bud, and
spray.
And fragrant flower, this hard metallic way,
Where thrust of crank and mad revolt of
wheel
Thrush out the wanderlust of steam and
steel-
Tivir ties that rule our world to-day.
Give nr a road that winds among the hills,
Some mountain myrtles in a cloudy slcy:
And be not niggardly with wayside rills,
And cool retreats for all things moist and
shy:
And let me hear the lyre-bird’s luscious
notes
Thieving the bullads from his neighbors’
throats.
ROOMS TO-LET FOR SINGLE MEN.
Kerb and pavement, street and wall,
And this hangs staring out at all.
O Woman ipith unrounded breast,
Poor little dream-child uncaressed.
O callous City, bending cruel,
With brutal breath, o’er phoenix fuel.
O smouldering rooms, blaze up, and then
Build on those ashes homes for men!
A WEALTH CENSUS CARD.
My meed, of Beauty—
'Though 1 but glean
Tardily, here and there ,
Just a faint gleam,
Only a chord or two,
i\ ! ot the full theme.
My meed of Poesy —
Though I belong
Not to the highways
That aspirants throng,
But stumble at times
On a sheep-walk of song.
My meed of Faith —
That ( when Self is laid bare,
Showing the evil
And good that is there),
In spite of the strife
Beckons Hope, not Despair.
My meed of Patience ■ —■
As daily I plod
Over the plough-lands
So many have trod;
My feet in the furrow,
My face towards God.
From “-Verses,”
by R. H. Long.
Happily, It. H. Long, whose Verses are
published by Stone, Adelaide, does not
always harness his Pegasus to his socialistic
cart: sometimes he allows the nag to graze
by the roadside. For the moke runs stiffly
between the socialistic shafts. There is a
sonnet to Adela Pankhurst, in which the
poet asks
What theme more fit for story or for song
Than fearless youth oppressing ancient
wrong ....?
But when he asks the world to twine laureate
wreaths around Adela’s brow, “wreaths for
the maid of Peace, this seer of ours,” the
sentiment may be all right, but the poetry
isn’t. And when he remarks: “I cannot see
how goodwill will arise by bashing out
brains'or by gouging out eyes,” this is sheer
polemics and sordid prose. And though we
agree with the author’s sentiment in refusing
to believe’ that “when Christ preached his
sermons he made reservations excluding the'
Germans”—though we are open to argue
that if there had been any Germans in those
days Christ would have felt inclined to
qualify his remarks—-yet the argument is
shallow and the manner of it uninspired.
But when R. H. Long forgets his politics
and gives his Pegasus its rein, when he
treads “the quiet country roads of God,” he
finds such treasures as. “The Skylark’s
Nest”: —
Here Nature holds as in a holloiced hand
For keen and loving eyes alone to see,
The larks and lyrics that are yet to he....
and learns that 1
From simplest nest may soar the sweetest
song ....
or sees
The flying-foxes swoop from tree to tree,
The ’possums scamper up the messmate’s
hark
In shadowy spirals, and. the wallaby
Goes thumping down the gully in the
dark. ..„
or finds the Acheron Valley
Gleaning the quiet afterglow of day.
There is genuine poetry here, the product of
a self-contained and original mind.
Fiction
William ,T. Locke can always be depended
upon. The'lied Planet (Lane, pec George
Robertson, Melb.) is, of'course, a war story,
but, wisely, the scene is not set on the
battlefield. “Thus over the sequestered vale,
far away from the sound of the shells, even
off the track 'of marauding Zeppelins, rode
the fiery planet Mars. There is not a
homestead in Great Britain that in one
form or another has not caught a reflection
of its blood-red ray. No matter how we may
seek distraction in work or amusement, the
angry glow is ever before our eyes, coloring
our vision, coloring our thoughts, coloring
our emotions for good or for ill. We cannot
escape it. Our personal destinies are inex-
tricably interwoven with • the fate directing
the death grapple of the thousand miles of
battle-line, and arbitrating on the doom of
colossal ships.”
It is a charming story, with heaps of plot.
The old Boer-war warrior, a cripple, who
tells the story, offers enough puzzles lor the
reader. “I felt myself being drawn into a
fog-filled labyrinth of intrigue in which
already groping were most of the people I
knew. What with the mysterious relations
between Betty and Boyce and between Boyce
and Gedge, what with young I lucre’s full
exoneration of Boyce, what with .young Ran-
dall’s split with Gedge and his impeccable
attitude towards Phyllis, things were com-
plicated enough; Sir Anthony’s revelations
regarding poor Althea and his dark surmises
concerning Randall complicated them still
more; arid now comes Airs. Holmes .to tell
me of Randall’s mysterldiis disappearance.”
B.ut it is more than a detective story ; it
is an incisive study of Fear—-the sort of
Fear that practically for the first time we
have heard of in this war, the Fear that
attacks even the bravest man, and by a tem-
porary paralysis of his brain leads either to
disgrace or. possibly, to the Y.C. And the
whole Story is so delightfully English, so
gentlemanly in attitude, so pleasantly suave !
Locke knows the public taste: it is to his
credit that he always gives the beast a little
better food than it needs.
When a novel opens gaily with a seasick
bishop, and when that bishop is the Bishop
of Bampopo, and when we learn that he is
bound for the Mediterranean island Nepen-
the, “famous for its girls and its. lobsters,
but also for its sirocco,” and we note the
genial wit of Norman Douglas, we feel that
South Wind (Seeker, per Dy mock’s
Library ) is going to do us as much good as
it is going to do the Bishop of Bampopo.
And it does. Indeed, Nepenthe is a delight-
ful Everybody goes there, and
everything happens there; but somehow
nothing seems to matter.. . .perhaps it is due
to the lobsters, or, maybe, the south wind,
which seems to have a steadily disintegrat-
ing influence upon the Bishop’s morals. You
see, he had just come from the Equatorial
Regions of Africa, and .lie was a little run-
down.
Nepenthe seems to be populated chiefly by
gossipers and scoundrels; both are charm-
ing. It is. an amazingly varied assortment
of characters to which Norman Douglas in-
troduces the Bishop. And life is so sunnily
pleasant on Nepenthe! Listen to the
Nepenthean philosophy; “We have only a
certain amount of energy at our disposal.
It i*fe not seemly to consume every ounce of
it in a contest with brute nature. Man is
made for better things. .. .Consider the- com-
plex conditions under which a Northern
European family is obliged to live. Think
of the labor expended upon that unnecessary
duel with the elements—the extra clothing
and footwear and mufflers and mantles, the
carpets, the rugs, the abundant and costly
food required to keep the body in sound
working condition, the plumbing, the gas,
the woodwork, the paintings and repaint-
ings,. the tons of fuel, the lighting in winter,
the contrivances against frost and rain, the
daily polishings and scrubbings, and those
thousand other impediments to the life of
the spirit! Half of these are non-existent
in these latitudes ; half the vitality expended
on them could therefore be directed to other
ends. At close of day, your Northerner is
pleased with himself. He has survived ; he
has even prospered. His family is adequately
housed and clothed. He feels, presen table, as
he calls it, in the eyes of those who share
his illusions What has he done? He
has performed certain social and political
duties—he knows nothing of duties to him-
self. . . .It is not enough for us. Living in
our lands, men would have leisure to culti-
vate nobler aspects of their nature. They
would be accessible to purer aspirations,
worthier delights.... In the Mediterranean
lies the hope of humanity.” Perhaps we
should restrict that sweeping assertion to
the island of Nepenthe.
In this delightful island once lived the
Good Duke Alfred. He built a mountain
stronghold—a symphony in emerald and
coral, since lie insisted mat every building in
it should be painted a uniform pink. He
also fixed by law the number of inhabitants,
thus solving for all time the congestion pro-
blem. 11 is method was simple: the town
must contain 500 souls, neither more nor
less. “If in any year the population ex-
ceeded that figure, the surplus was taken
away, from among the adult males, to work
as galley-slaves in his fleet; a deficiency in
the requisite number was met by giving new
husbands from the lower town, often three
or four at a time, ‘with a view to ensuring
good results,’ to those of the native women
who had hitherto failed to produce offspring.
The system worked well.” A historian
recorded that “never have the generative
capacities of mankind shaped themselves
with more success to the shape of an un-
necessary wall.”
In this charming vein of cynicism Norman
Douglas describes the evolution of a
Russian saint who. after triumphs and
vicissitudes that shame those of Rasputin,
came at last with his band of converts to
the haven of Nepenthe; and with a touch of
Anatole France he dilates upon the many
fountains of Nepenthe. Indeed his descrip-
tion of the various fountains makes an ideal
guide-book. “The Fountain known as ‘La
Salina’ of arsenical nature, was frequented
chiefly by women who found in its waters
an alleviation for troubles which Monsignor
Perellf does not specify. It was recom-
mended, moreover, as a sheep-dip. . . .The
Fountain of Saint Feto had, by virtue of its
smell alone, applied to her nose as she lay
in her coffin, raised from the dead a certain
Anna da Pasto. . .. The Fountain of . Saint
Elias, sulphurous and saponaceous, was re-
nowned for its calming influence upon all
who suffered from abuse of lechery or
alcohol, or from ingrowing toenails.”
The good old Duke was an ideal despot.
His taxation methods were a marvel of sim-
plicity. Each citizen paid what he liked.
If the sum proved insufficient he was
apprised of the fact next morning by having
his left hand amputated. In a period when
all Europe was convulsed in savage warfare
he remained immune. He conciliated his
more powerful neighbors by timely gifts of
local delicacies—the Nepenthe lobsters and
the Nepenthe girls, “both items of the finest
quality imaginable.” Even minor sovereigns
he supplied with periodical consignments of
pretty maidens and well-flavored crayfish,
only of somewhat inferior quality—the crus-
taceans often too young, and the damsels
occasionally over-ripe. His habit of cub-
ting off portions of the features of his sub-
jects gave rise to one of his blithest watch-
words : “Nose after ears!”
Exquisite fooling, this, delivered with a
mock-gravity that heightens the dry humor.
Rut Norman Douglas does not lose himself
i'n his quips. He provides a breathless plot
and a host of curious character studies. At
Nepenthe there is good , company for the
fastidious reader. The dear bishop is
affected by the prevailing south wind-—and
the ' prevailing morals. He found himself
condoning what elsewhere he would have
termed vice—but then he had just come from
Rampopo. He had to puli himself up.
“The whole question is plain as a pikestaff.
Am I becoming more of a Christian, or
less,?” In that fortnight on Nepenthe—a
fortnight including a murder, several scan-
dals, a miracle or two, a volcanic eruption
and a continuous torrent of conversation —
the Bishop wilted.
“A kind of merry nightmare. Things hap-
pened. . There was something bright and
diabolical in the tone of the place, something-
kaleidoscopic—a frolicsome perversity. Puri-
fying, at the same time. It swept away the
cobwebs. It gave you a measure, a stan-
dard, whereby to compute earthly affairs.
....His values had righted themselves. He
had carved out new and sound ones; a work-
able, up-to-date theory of life. He was in
fine trim. His liver -he forgot that he eyer
had one. Nepenthe had done him good all
round. And lie knew exactly what he
wanted.... And how would England com-
pare with the tingling realism of Nepenthe?
Rather parochial, rather dun. Everything
fireproof, seaworthy. Kindly thoughts ex-
pressed in safe, unvarying formulas....
Ships tossing at sea; minds firmly anchored
to the commonplace. Abundance for the
body; diet for tlie spirit. Horror of the
tangent, the extreme, the unconventional.
God save the King.”
And, characteristically, we take leave of
the Bishop when he is on his way home
from the tavern, and he notices that the
moon is behaving queerly, almost as if it
were drunk. And sometimes there were two
moons. ...
Perhaps you have to get into the
Nepenthe mood before you enjoy South
Wind. The serious reader would never dis-
cover the - way to Nepenthe—or if he did, he
would never realise that he was there. But
once you relapse into the Norman Douglas
mood, you will enjoy yourself as much as the
dear Bishop did.
Gone to Earth, by Mary Webb (Con-
stable), is another Gloom Novel. It is set
in one of those gloomy counties of gloomy
England, a fox-hunting county peopled by
dour religionists, stolid peasants and dull
fox-hunters. Miss Ciombers rode “with so
grim a mouth and such a bloodthirsty ex-
pression that she might have had a blood-
feud with all foxes. Perhaps, when she rode
down the anxious red-brown streak, she
thought she was riding down a cruel fate
that had somehow left her life vacant of
joy; perhaps,' when the little creature was
torn piecemeal, she imagined herself tearing
so the frail unconquerable powers of love
and beauty. Anyway, she never missed a
meet, and she and her sister never ceased
their long silent battle for Reddin, who re-
mained as unconscious of them as if they
were his aunts.”
Hazel, the ignorant daughter of a bee-
keeper, coffin-maker and harp-player, is, how-
ever. a niece of lush and pulsating life,
doomed, in that environment, to come to
grief. She is wanted by two dramatically-
contrasted men, Edward, the young neutral-
tinted minister, and Reddin, the crude horse-
breeder. Edward gets her,- simply because
Hazel told her father that she would marry
the- first who asked her. When Edward
proposes she asks, “Will you buy me a green
gown with yellow roses onV’’ and “Will you
gi’ me pear-drops every day V” But Edward
fatuously errs in making this vivid, wonder-
ful thing his wife only in name—a mistake
which husbands make more frequently in
novels than they do in real life. Still,
Edward was that sort of chap. Of course,
Reddin gets her —by methods simple and
direct, The reader cannot feel much sym-
pathy for Edward—Reddin was -obviously
the mate for this beautiful wild thing. After
the catastrophe Reddin and Hazel “regarded
each one another mistrustfully, like two
creatures taken in a snare. They both felt
as if they had been trapped by something
vast and intangible.... Reddin had imposed
a deeper change on her than the change from
maid to wife. He had robbed her of a thing
frailer and rarer than maidenhood —the sac-
ramental love of Nature. It is only the fair-
est, the highest and fullest matings that do
not rob the soul of this, even when it is an
old, tried joy. lie had wronged her as deeply
as one human being can wrong another. Ilis
theft was cruel as that of one who destroys
a man's God. And. the strange part of it
was that never, as long as he lived, would
he know that he had done so, or even guess
that there had been any treasury to rifle.
He would probably, as an old man, long past
desire, repent of the physical part of the
affair. Yet this was so much the lesser of
the two. Indeed, if he had been able to win
her. love, it would have been, not wrong-
doing, but righteousness. That a woman
should, in the evolution of life, cease to be
a virgin and become a mother is a thing so
natural and so purely physical as hardly
to need comment; but that the immortal
part of her should be robbed, that she should
cease to be an entity in a world where per-
sonality is the only rare and precious thing
—this is tragic.” i
Still, as the- author says, though Reddin
could not help his over-virility, it did seem
a pity that “after so many centuries, so
many matings and births', all his emblazoned
and crested ancestors should have produced
merely—Reddin, a person exactly like them-
selves.”
So each of these men attract one side of
this beautiful girl; and all she wanted was
herself. The final tragedy is in keeping with
the sustained sombre beauty of the tale, and
yet, though hinted at in the first chapter, it
comes as a shock to even the hardened
reader of Gloom Fiction. Mary Webb can
write.
Much is Life, by Tom Collins (Joseph
Furpliy), was published 14 years ago by
The Bulletin. A second edition, accord-
ing to Vance Palmer, is. now called for by
“weight of critical opinion rather than the
importunity of the booksellers.” Hence this
new edition, published bv The Specialty
Press, Melb. Vance Palmer, in a weighty
preface, claims that this work, apart from
its intrinsic literary quality, registers a
faithful picture of life in the Riverina a
generation ago. The author, born at Yering
in 1843, was a fitting chronicler for that
period; and Such is Life is essentially Aus-
tralian in outlook and atmosphere. “The
value of Such is Life lies in' the extraordi-
nary richness of its philosophy and charac-
terisation, in the vitality of its style, and in
the whimsical humor with which it is
saturated.” And Vance Palmer claims that
■ this work will become a classic for the next
generation.
Such is Life is the sort of book an Aus-
tralian should keep on his handiest shelf, to
dip into. Occasionally he’ll draw a blank;
but usually he’ll find something well worth
thinking over. A multifarious and stimulat-
ing mind, that of Tom Collins! Vance
Palmer might consider the question of a
concentrated extract of Tom Collins of the
essence of At dralianism in literary tabloid
form. Sacrilege, no doubt, to Vance
Palmer. but a been to the tired Australian.
The tenth part of Muirhead Bone’s note-
worthy drawings of The Western Front
( Country Life, London, 2s.) deals with
ship-building; without which there could
be for England no Western Front. It is a
remarkable series of pictures, conveying an
overwhelming impression of the vigur and
the magnitude of Englands desperate race
against the U-boat. The standard ships are
shown in every stage of their construction;
and the drawings are brilliantly done.

Self-Poisoning
IT is easy to get constipated. It is easy to let
yourself stay constipated. It may seem a matter
of little consequence to you.
Yet any doctor will tell you that the poisoning of
the system by the products of constipation is one of
the most dangerous conditions to which mankind
is subjected.
Dull headaches, listlessness, sallow complexion,
intestinal troubles —all of these are signs of self*
poisoning, which weakens the system and exposes
you to the inroads of disease.
You can prevent and relieve constipation by the
use of Nujol, the natural laxative which is now
recommended by leading physicians the world
over as the safe and sure treatment for constipation.
Nujol is not absorbed by the system but acts as
an internal lubricant.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An Old Love Story.
[When Shah Jehan was deposed his treatment was
kinder than that meted out to most Oriental despots
who throw the dice with Fate and lose. The victors
did not put out his eyes. Instead he was permitted
to sit on the balcony of his prison at Agra and regale
those mournful eyes with the vision of the Taj
Mahal, that truly Imperial tomb which he built for
Arjumand Banu, the wife of his youth.]
By the Jumna’s tide is 'the marble flower
That Jehan fashioned from love and tears
When Arjumand died in the Jasmine Tower
And joy lay slain for all after-years.
In the time-worn tower on the city wall
Her face like a star at the casement shone
When she heard the sound of his footstep
fall—
O Allah ! Allah ! The joys that have gone!
If the mouldering walls took voice and told
Of the passioned things in that dark room,
Of her flower-like breasts, as pale as gold.
Her dark eyes flaming through incense
gloom,
Of tense brown faces and scarlet lips,
The mating glory of life at its noon —
O Allah! Whatever the years eclipse,
This love outlasteth both sun and moon !
Now the lizard may bask on her window-sill.
And the night-bird hoot where the lady
laughed—
What does it matter? They had their fill,
And of life’s good measure they drained
their draught!
The silken couch where they laid their heads.
And drowsed to sleep in the Indian night.
May be dust of the desert or shameful shreds.
But their soul-fires flame in the years’
despite.
When Arjumand, called from the Jasmine
Tower,
Like a leaf went down on the Shadow
Stream,
In marble, moon colored, for tomb and dower,
Her Lord upbuilded his Dome of Dream.
Together they rest, the sheath and the sword
Sleep side by side, as they slept of old.
In the guarded chamber, the wife and her
lord,
When the counted hours dropt like beads
of gold.
When the judgment comes to the hosts of
the dead
It is written that love shall be more than
prayers;
When their flower-dome falls and their dust
is spread
O Allah ! what boon for such love as
theirs?
N.S.W.
P. E. QUINN.
The Journeying.
My words go by the railway, but my heart
goes by the sea.
For the mail van and the engine they are
all too slow for me !
My written word can loiter, packed away in
bag and crate,
But my gypsy heart goes singing, as a bird
sings, for its mate !
My words go by the railway, over Wallan-
garra’s fiats,
Up to ranges and the fern-growths, spread
like genii’s praying mats,
To the paddocks near Glen Innes, and to
little Loehinvar ;
To the great and dirty coalfields where the
long grey waters are.
And grumbling up the hillside, and shrieking
o’er the bridge.
With a sliding to the hollows and a puffing
up the ridge,
And into Sydney suburbs with their flower-
trimmed diadem.
My words can bide the pleasure of the train
that carries them !
My words go by the railway, but my heart
goes by the sea.
And it flings the fine foam from it in an opal
ecstasy.
And it drives beyond the shipping, and it
races steam and wind.
For there never was a funnel that could
leave my heart behind ;
And it treads forbidden places where the
waves curve clear and green,
But the searchlights cannot find it, for a
heart goes by unseen !
All in a wild rose morning it will come
between the Heads,
When the lazy mists are rising from the
sleepy garden beds;
And though the words come later, coyly
fashioned to deceive.
You will smile and never heed them —-tis
my heart you will believe !--
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Buttercups.
Once you held my childish kisses, happy
kisses, warm and red;
In your golden bowls I dropped them that
the fairies, dainty fairies,
When at dawn they climbed dance-weary to
their satin-petalled bed
There might find my friendly offering, take
my kisses, childish kisses,
And. perhaps, leave theirs instead.
Now you hold my woman’s tears, bitter
tears, stinging tears —
No fit gift for dancing fairy, should one
venture down the years.
But one day my love may see you, o’er your
beauty bend his head,
Find my tears, woman’s tears, and leave
perhaps his own instead.
N.S.W.
MARGARET FANE.

Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and.
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
irords, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 31, 1918.
NOW WHAT ABOUT WINNING THE
WAR?
Though this paper willingly admits that
the proposal for compulsory military service
has been defeated, it does not admit that
the poll of either last year or the year
before inflicted any serious damage on the
Germans. Nor does it believe that the
war can be won by Australia frequently
registering majorities for No. A preponder-
ance of negatives leaves unaltered two grim
facts. The first is that the struggle in
which the people of this country became
involved three and a half years ago is still
raging. The next is that unless the
western democracies frame the peace which
must some day come, the world which they
and Australia have known will pass into
eclipse. Wherefore, though a substantial
number of the Australian people were in
December against conscription, the problem
before them and all the Allies has still to be
grappled with. Stripped of irrelevancies
that problem, so far as Australia is con-
cerned, is crystallised in two questions: (1)
Where are the men and the means to carry
on the fight 7 (2) What is the war policy
of the Commonwealth 7 It is no use blun-
dering on in the belief that as the Conscrip-
tion issue was negatived there remains
nothing for the Parliament to do except to
sit around the Mace moaning and groaning
part of it over the iniquities of compul-
sion and the others about the futility of
voluntaryism. If the Legislature can do no
more than this it will have gone a long way
towards proving that the Commonwealth has
failed at the first critical test.
As it was never claimed by even the most
embittered opponent of compulsion that if
the Referendum was defeated the enemy
would be annihilated, nor asserted by the
most ardent conscriptionist that rejection
meant defeat without further protest, the
theory that Ministerial billets remain the
sole bone of contention can only be sup-
ported on the assumption that the war has
become a mere rumor. So long as a
majority of the community does not dispute
that there is an Australian army in the
field, the obligation upon the Legislature to
bend its mind to the business of war is as
heavy as when, before the electors, its mem-
bers individually declared their anxiety to
get busy in that direction. The essence of
the situation before Parliament is that there
are in this country thousands of men eligible
for military service, that enlistments are
negligible, and that it has been denied
authority to employ compulsion. Australia
knows what this Parliament will not do. It
will not raise reinforcements by conscription.
What Australia should know, and know
"quickly, is what the Legislature ivill do. The
obviously sane proceeding is to get itself
into a condition to look squarely at its duty,
and then, having decided what it wants to do
and what it can do within the limitations
imposed by the Referendum and the capacity
of the country to pay the bill, to proceed
with the job.
It is not a long job nor one of much diffi-
culty. Conscription being out of the
question, the country has to rely on volun-
taryism. The first questions for Parliament
to determine as a preliminary are these: —
(1) Is the Ministry in office the one best calcu-
lated to get the best results from the voluntary
system ?
(2) What, additional inducements can be held out
to men to enlist?
(3) What organisation of effort will bring more
men into camp?
On whether these questions are answered
in a spirit of narrow partisanship—other-
wise, of rank dishonesty—all that follows
wall depend. If there is a Ministry in office
that has antagonised the great bulk of the
population, there will be not only a failure
of voluntaryism, but a gross failure of Par-
liament. Then when the Commonwealth
has devised a policy that is a policy for
reinforcing the army, there is the other job
of considering the bill. This has been studi-
ously dodged by sucessive Ministries ever
since the war began, and the State Govern-
ments have given great assistance in sup-
plying imitations of Micawber. To speak
about either the Commonwealth or the
States having a financial policy for to-day
or even a shadowy comprehension of finan-
cial policy for to-morrow would be to perpe-
trate a jest. Yet, though there is an inti-
mate connection between war policy and
preservation of the public credit, it is the
bitter truth that Australia has neither
achieved the first nor shown any regard for
the second. If we are to escape the pitiful
experience of having to confess our armies
beaten, not by the German but by the failure
of our own Legislature, the Commonwealth
Parliament will have to get down to bed-
rock promptly and knock off playing the
goat.
SOME SCATTERED THOUGHTS
ABOUT MONEY.
One of these days ill-mannered people will
describe the political situation in Australia
as plain, unvarnished corruption, and will
add insult to injury by proving the charge.
In fact, some people are saying this sort of
thing already. The evil isn’t equally distri-
buted throughout Australia—some . States
have it worse than others, and the Ma
community takes a high place as usual —but
it is practically everywhere. It isn’t exactly
the crude corruption of other days and other
lands, where the price of a vote was handed
out, naked and unashamed, in visible cash.
Yet the manufacture of highly-paid Trade
Commissionerships, so that men who know
nothing about trade may live at ease in
pleasant places where there is no trade to
do, is crude enough. The same may be said
of the six State Agent-Generalships and
many other devices whereby dangerous oppo-
nents or equally dangerous supporters are
bought off. The business of putting returned
soldiers on the land opens up possibilities
for the future, there has been a sudden dis-
covery that a drove of extra Federal Under-
secretaries -w T ill soon be wanted, and it is
indeed a cold day when somebody isn't
appointed to specially inquire into something.
But these are cheap scandals at the worst.
Also they are humorous in a way. When
an aged and humble but consistent supporter
was once made Inspector of Scaffolding in
Sydney he didn’t cost so very much, and he
made the community smile for a full week.
His whiskers became almost historical, he
being a man with a flagrant jungle of hair
hanging at the root of a desolate counten-
ance.
Another form of public dry rot is less
tangible and vastly more expensive. It is
called “the development of the great natural
resources of the country” by means of “per-
manent and reproductive works” paid for
out of loans. Everybody knows that the
works aren’t permanent in the true sense of
the term, for nothing is—only some works
have a temporary immortality while some
are built to-day, and opened with a Minis-
terial lunch to-morrow and shut up as a
failure next year. And they aren’t repro-
ductive in any complete sense; nor are they
meant to be or expected to be. The first
essential of a public work built out of loans
is that it-mustn’t be something so large and
patriotic and far-seeing as to be beyond the
Ministry’s brain capacity. It must be some-
thing easy. Something easy and useless that
won’t pay has much more appeal in it than
something big and necessary that will pay.
The construction of a new r Zoo. at Sydney,
with enlarged accommodation for the bear
with a sore head, was a matter that any
gardener could have devised with the aid of
an intelligent dog, so £lOO.OOO or more was
spent on the job without the sanction of the
Public Works Committee, as provided by
law—the Public Works Committee has long
been a well-paid place of refuge for politi-
cians—and this at a time of stress when the
bear should have been boiled down for soup.
It was typical of a hundred other jobs which
there is no space to enumerate, but it was
a small one by comparison, and the bear is
an object of mirth, udiich is something to
its credit. The business is rotten in the
aggregate, and most of it isn’t mirthful. The
huge loans for “development” don’t develop;
consequently the two most populous States
are steadily losing population save in a
small area which in each case is more or less
closely attached to the bloated capital. There
are ports as capable as Sydney or Mel-
bourne which remain closed because the rail-
ways shy away from them like a startled
horse, and take long, costly detours to avoid
them because the big old cities hate competi-
tion. So the people who should be served
by these ports either fail to arrive or arrive
and get away, and the profitable _ centres
which should arise there don’t arise any
more than they did in Captain Cook’s day.
It would pay to have some new big cities,
but it takes something of a mind to upset
the deep-rooted congestion policy, and it is
less mental strain to run up houses for
returned soldiers on' pocket-handkerchief
suburban allotments. This device is in keep-
ing with the One Big City policy, for it tends
to draw to the crowded old spots population
which was. in part at least, a country
population before the war and to raise a
new generation of hardy bushmen close to
the tramline. #
The people have grown used to this sort
of thing, and the politicians who spend the
high-priced loans on small profitless deeds
because they lack the mental furniture for
big ones are kept in office by folk who have
grown used to the policy of small things and
plenty of them. The Holman species of
politician is partly the product of the times
and partly the cause of them, and the resem-
blance is so remarkable that it is hard to
say which is more like the other and which
isn’t. The system pays high wages and
plenty of them to supporters and possible
supporters, and that is the whole gospel of
politics. Of course, as the yearly expendi-
ture on the “reproductive” work increases
the works grow less reproductive because the
few which pay their way have to carry
the huge dead pauper of those which don’t,
and the pauper grows larger and deader
every day. In proportion to the resources
of the people the pension system of Australia
—or most of Australia —is one of the world’s
marvels, but the bulk of the pensioners aren’t
human —they are railways and dams and
land-resumptions and such-like. Many a
ravening Grand Duke is cheaper than a
permanent and reproductive work, and he
hasn’t to be renewed at a steadily-increasing
interest rate. In N. S. Wales’s most enthu-
siastic years it has probably taken £2,000,000
or £4,000,000 of costly loan money to keep
Premier Holman on his throne. That is to
say, when £7,000,000 or £9,000,000 of loan
outburst —allegedly reproductive—only yields
interest on £5.000,000, there is nothing to
show for the balance except that Premier
Holman and his colleagues retained their
jobs. They had to do something to justify
their existence, and they did the only thing
they knew of. When the present public
works system goes bankrupt, some other jus-
tification will have to be devised, and then
somebody will have to take to real hard work
or else turn Bolshevik and repudiate the past
and start afresh. It is only a question of
extending the scope of the moratorium and
making its operation permanent like the
reproductive works.
THE SHOW THAT FAILED.
War being a diversion, something devised
primarily to entertain and use up surplus
energy, any particular performance may
fairly come under criticism with regard to
its artistic excellence. Thus as to the Nor-
man Conquest of England—an entertain-
ment provided for restless Normans and
their friends who were feeling bored by life
in their own land, and in their boredom
were becoming dangerous—the experienced
eye simply cannot help noticing first the
excellence of the setting. The sensational
landing at Hastings, the repeated charges up
the hill at Senlac, the sudden death of
Harold, the swift collapse of the Saxon
kingdom—all these were dramatic events
which thoroughly interested both actors and
onlookers. William knew that his fol-
lowers wanted to be kept from dull mo-
notony. Kings are never in such danger as
when their fighting men eat up the surround-
ing air with yawns. There were no picture-
shows in the conquered territory, so Wil-
liam had to think out something for his men
to do. He managed it very cleverly.
Nobody got a big enough share of land in
any one part of the country to allow of his
raising an army on a single estate. The
valiant knight who had 2000 acres given
him in Norfolk got his next allotment of
spoil in Berkshire, and his third in Devon.
As a result he was kept riding backwards
and forwards from Norfolk to Devon by
way of Berkshire, in order to see that his
nossessions stayed in their places. This
gave him fine exercise, afforded him fre-
quent change of air'and left him very little
time to think about killing the king. But
as time went on and the novelty of keeping
Saxon churls in their place wore off, the
danger re-asserted itself and monarchs had
to think up new wars. Richard’s cru-
sading expedition, the attacks on Scotland
of Edward I. and Edward 11., the inva-
sions of France by Edward 111. and Henry
V. were all of this nature. Foreign wars
were the entertainment provided by the
king in return for his throne; and the kings
who didn’t prove good “producers” got into
trouble.
While war is stili tolerated by civilisation
and great military and naval establishments
are kept up, there is always the danger that
the modern king will follow the old line of
least resistance. Nothing rouses enthusiasm
like a good international bout of killing; and
though the real kings of these days may not
be the ones who wear gold hats, the tempta-
tion to let armies loose is still very great.
Government is a difficult business; and the
more autocratic it is the greater are its
dangers. Democracies —or the things that
wear the label —are ticklish things to handle;
but the results of failure to manage the
affairs of a more or less free people are
seldom serious. The autocratic boss, on the
other hand, has no minor difficulties to con-
tend with ; all his troubles are on a tragic
scale. He can steal, perpetrate injustice,
play the traitor, and do all manner of iniqui-
ties in a general way: but in blocking re-
forms and denying freedom he is in constant
dansrer of knife and bomb. To the simple
minds of the people who groan under auto-
era tic rule the first of all reforms is tne
murder of the autocrat. The peril is not
active at all times; but the moment the cry
becomes urgent for reform the astute tyrant
has to cast about for something to divert
public attention. Nothing lies so ready to
his hand as the old entertainment of war.
Give the people a fair-sized spectacle or
international bloodshed and they forget most
of their personal wrongs until long after the
curtain has been rung down on the last
charge. The present war probably owes its
magnitude and circumstance to the fact that
three tremendous despotisms were all in tne
same fix at the same time. Russian bosses felt
that they were on a volcano, and that nothing
but a grand fanfare of cannon could save
them; Austria’s Imperial dodderer felt the
same thing; Wilhelm of Germany weighed
the growing power of the Socialism he had
pretended to pet and foster, and saw a choice
between a revolution that left him shamed
and helpless and a war that might main-
tain the dynasty for years. So the guns
were wheeled out; and the terrific entertain-
ment began that was to arouse all onlook-
ers to a pitch of excitement under cover of
which the kings could keep on doing exactly
as they pleased.
# * * *
Viewed critically between the acts, the
performance thus far has not been a success.
Never before was a war staged with such
properties, dressed at such lavish cost and
played with such a numerous cast; yet the
spectacular triumphs aimed at have been so
poor that again and again the principal
actors have been hissed. The single battle
which William the Conqueror staged for a
few hours on the hill at Senlac had more
dramatic power about it than all that
Wilhelm and the Austrian actor-managers
have been able to display. The little war
in the Crimea, which provided England and
Russia with loud cheers for a short season,
had more striking situations than the whole
of the great Teuton effort. The fact is that
in the present war far too much attention
has been given to the mere mechanism of
the thing; every spectacle has been on so
exaggerated a scale that it has been impos-
sible to see the battles for the fighting; and
there has not been nearly enough comedy
element to relieve the utter gloom of the
tragic passages. The nations have not been
entertained; they have been bored and
wearied ; and there are probably not half a
dozen people on earth —outside the profiteers
who take the money at the door —who re-
gard the show as having been in any way
worth while. How far Wilhelm’s reputa-
tion has sunk in Germany it is impossible
to know; but Russia has already hooted the
principal performers off her end of the stage
and Austria appears to be hurling the dead
cat at her boss showmen. Summed up, on
the appearance of things at the close of the
fourth act —or is it only the third?—the
whole performance points to the certainty
that war as an entertainment provided _by
kings has lost its charm and popularity.
Bear-baiting might revive; the Roman
gladiatorial contests might be the natural
sequel and evolution of modern boxing ; but
war cannot come again. It has blown itself
dead with too many fireworks and spoiled
its pathos with too many graves. And if
that is so, what is to become of kings? An
attempt to solve the puzzle is probably keep-
ing more than one of them awake at nights.
THE BRITISH SOLOMON ISLANDS
AND THEIR ADMINISTRATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Until this century the Solomon Islands were
commercially of no value. The haunts of
savage and unfriendly nativefe, they had never-
theless become the richest recruiting grounds
for Queensland sugar fields, where the mon-
strous inhumanity of white men, calling
themselves Britishers, caused wholesale
devastation and, what is a hundred times
worse, passed on a curse in the form of
sexual disease which in more cases than one
has recoiled upon our race. That was
20 years and more ago; and the laws made
then for fiends who never settled in the
islands are still in force, and, what is more,
have been added to, not because white men's
crimes have increased, for it is quite the
contrary, but because British officials have,
through the gross ignorance of British states-
men who have added neglect to their ignor-
ance, been permitted to set up an official
autocracy.
In these days the Solomons are of incal-
culable commercial value ; immense cocoanut
plantations are scattered far and wide, no
longer clinging to the coastal lands, but
running away to the hill tops, and as vigor-
ous in theit growth there as those sucking
the sea waters; British and Australian
Capital has been invested to the extent of
millions; white population is rapidly increas-
ing, and hundreds of white women now
reside there. Copra that sold 20 years ago,
a rough, crude commercial nondescript, at
£S per ton, is selling to-day in France and
America at sometimes over £IOO per ton,
and frequently at £BO per ton; hardly a
steamer leaves the islands these days without
a cargo worth nearly £50,000.
Here we have on the one side well-
directed private enterprise striving to achieve
its laudable ambitions, and on the other a
Government that has in the most deliberate
manner set up every obstacle to commercial
progress—a Government that has openly
boasted, through its leading officials, that
it would prefer the Chinaman to the white
man at any time. Yet the white man to
be found in the Solomons is a keen, straight
goer, there for the purpose of working and
spending his money for the development of
the islands, and intent upon their progress.
There is little wonder that such men are in
almost open rebellion and for the second time
are petitioning the Imperial Government for
relief and a suitable administration. Within
the whole British Empire there will not be
found in any possession such a bitterness of
feeling, such an open aggressiveness towards
an administration, as now exists here.
The administration is responsible to the
Government of Fiji. The High Commis-
sioner of Fiji draws a good part of his salary
from the settlers. Yet not once in the whole
term of his office, which ends soon, has he
visited the islands. And why? Because there


is not at liis disposal any vessel of sufficient
comfort and of a class suitable to the
dignity of his position ! Official aggression
has had full freedom in the Solomons, and
there is little wonder that disloyalty to the
empire is heard from many settlers, who are
suffering tyranny hard to believe possible in
British territory.
This Administration gives native employees
bank holidays, when there is no such thing
as a bank in the whole of the Solomons, and
the natives don’t know what a bank is; it
demands such a dietary scale that every day
the waste on plantations of such foods as
rice, tinned meats, biscuits, etc., is posi-
tively wicked; it pampers the native and
makes him what he is, a loafer, and as cun-
ning as a racecourse sharper. And all the
time there is a grievous waste of public
money. For instance, the Government
steamer in 1916 cost for upkeep no less than
£4OOO, though for 290 days out of the 365
she lay idle in Tulagai harbor, a big item
for island finances to stand.
But there are features worse than these
that call for prompt inquiry by the Imperial
Government. Not only planters, traders
and recruiters, but missionaries, are con-
stantly appealing to the Administration for
protection from the natives, whose depreda-
tions include human life; and human life to
be taken not by bows and arrows, but by
the modern rifle. In the land of the head-
hunters, every plantation, every trading
station, every mission must have its armed
guard, day and night, to enable work to go
on and to keep stores safe fram raiding.
This is the meaning of British protection in
the Solomons—that the natives are allowed
to carry on tribal warfare, to burn, to mas-
sacre and to destroy; and then it is pro-
claimed there is a shortage of native labor !
And what does the Administration say when
taxed with this? “It is no . business of
ours!" Britishers made it their business to
stop the burning of widows in India. Why
cannot they stop tribal warfare in the Solo-
mons? The atrocities committed by the
natives upon women are almost unbelievable.
They are unspeakably horrible.
Confirmation can be given from an official
report which reads as follows: —
The native population has been estimated to be
150,000, including the natives of the outlying islands
of Santa Cruz and Lord Howe. Owing to various
causes the native population is said to be decreasing ;
during the past 18 months a large number has been
carried off by dysentery; the island of San Cristoval
appears to have lost more from this cause than other
islands, whilst in some districts whole villages were
wiped out. Malaita remains the most densely popu-
lated, but dysentery and the continual fighting among
themselves must have greatly reduced their num-
bers. On Choiseul, where there has been incessant
intertribal fighting during the past year, it is
reported that numbers of men, women and children
have been killed. In the Shortland district the
decrease is very marked.
On this confession alone it is time these
islands were put under more suitable admin-
istration. A continuation of what is going
on in the Solomons is impossible, unless we
are to leave Britain and British ideals, open
to misinterpretation and ridicule.
THOS. J. McMAHON.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
When Billjim Is Done With.
Dear Bulletin, —It is surprising how little
is heard in this country about War Pensions.
Certainly it is not because everyone is satis-
fied, and it can only be attributed to Bill-
jim’s cheerfulness and his casual way of
putting up with things. The story has been
spread, too, that the Australian War Pen-
sion is the highest in the world. This is
by no means the case. The Australian full
pension for a private (single) is 30s. per
week. In England it is 275. 6d., but there
a provision is made that, where a disabled
man can show that his minimum pension,
plus any allowance for children that he may
have plus the average earnings of which he
is still capable, make a sum less than his
average pre-war earnings, he may be granted
a pension which together with average earn-
ings of which he may be deemed capable
shall not exceed his pre-war earnings up to
a maximum of 50s. per week, plus half any
pre-war earnings between 50s. and 100 s. This
provision may best be understood by a con-
crete case. Take a man who was earning
£4 per week before the war and is now totally
disabled. For his total disablement his
weekly pension is £1 7s. 6d. Presuming he
can now earn nothing, he can claim a pen-
sion of £2 10s. plus half his former earnings
between £2 10s. and £4 (viz., 155.), making
a total of £3 ss. per week. It will thus be
seen that a man’s pension in England can
be considerably higher than here, though the
cost of living is lower there. Now in regard
to married men. In Australia a wife is
granted a pension in her own right of half
the amount her husband is receiving, and
pensions are also granted for children, if
any. In England a wife does not receive a
pension of her own, except when her hus-
band is receiving treatment or training, and
then she is granted what is there called a
separation allowance. Children are granted
pensions as here, but on a slightly iower
scale. It wouldjippear that in the granting
of pensions to wives in Australia some regu-
lation is required, for in a number of cases
wives on being granted their nension have
immediately left their husbands. A provision
is required enabling the cancellation of a
pension under such circumstances. Again,
should a single pensioner marry, his wife is
granted a pension of half her husband’s.
Now, there is no wish to prevent the man
who has fought for his country marrying;
but when cases,.are seen of men suffering
from tuberculosis and other infectious com-
plaints doing so immediately on their arrival
home it would seem that there is a class of
woman willing even at personal risk to
marry these poor fellows, sometimes, unfor-
tunately, solely with a view to obtaining
pensions for themselves. There is a class of
men who are laboring under a manifest in-
justice, i.e., men who are receiving treatment
and are in receipt of less than full pensions.
It would be impossible for these men to live
and continue their treatment if it were not
for the Patriotic Funds which supplement
their pensions; but is it right that the funds
should be called upon to do what is
undoubtedly the duty of the Commonwealth?
The class that presents the greatest difficulty
is largely the product of our voluntary sys-
tem of enlistment. It consists of men who
have been returned for some complaint or
injury which existed prior to their enlist-
ment. In some instances they have seen
active service; in many they have never
reached the firing-line. What is the posi-
tion ? Is the Government responsible for
having enlisted them and sent them away,
and are they entitled to be granted a pen-
sion on their present condition or to the
extent to vshich their complaint or injury
has been aggravated by service? This class
includes the old men who have mis-stated
their age on enlistment in order to get away.
Thev have had a few weeks in the trenches
and have been robbed of all future useful-
ness in life, and, though unemployable, are
discharged with a pension of 7s. 6d. per
w r eek, the result being that they must become
a charge on the patriotic funds, and, later,
on the State, which has had nothing to do
with wrongfully enlisting them and sending
them away. When it is said that a large
percentage of the men returned up to the
present are within this category it will be
realised how serious the position is. What
is the remedy? Pensions and Repatriation
should be merged into one. Pensions when
granted should be assessed on the permanent
incapacity, provision being made for the
automatic supplementation of the permanent
pension, if less than full, while the men are
in receipt of treatment or training and until
employment is obtained; provision also
being made for a reduction in the case of a
man refusing treatment or training when
certified as in need of and fit for the same.
Regulations governing the granting of pen-
sions to wives of pensioners should also be
drafted. And, finally, military and civil
doctors should be represented on the pen-
sion boards, which should consist of three
members, preferably one military and two
civil. —A Voluntary Worker.
We Can Do It as Well as Uncle Sam.
From a progressive Australian manufac-
turer :
After spending three months in the U.S.A. visiting
such cities as New York, Chicago, Boston, Cincin-
nati, Pittsburgh, Dayton, Detroit, Los Angeles and
’Frisco, I feel there are several things I want to
let Australians know.
Australia is the better country to work and live
in. The climate in the eastern States of America is
too cold —so cold, in fact, that it becomes a hard-
ship to be out of doors in winter. The cost of living
in Australia was, when I left about six months ago,
just about half what it is here, and rents in
Chicago and New York are very high. The living
conditions of the great bulk of the workers in the
big cities are wretched and cainnot be compared
with ours.
Most of the industries here work 54 hours per
week in summer and 60 in winter. There is little
or no Government control or regulation of either
wages or hours ; but in some States there are laws
about heating and ventilating factory buildings, and
certain restrictions are placed upon child labor.
In many of the large plants a very high state of
efficiency is reached, and it is in these plants that
the highest wages are earned, shortest hours worked
and the best conditions prevail; and in almost every
instance the product is so low-priced that it com-
mands a world’s market.’ I want to impress this
fact upon the employers as well as the workers in
Australia: It is the product of the up-to-date, well-
equipped, highly-paid factory that holds the markets
for the U.S.A.
A very large proportion of the workers here are
Italians, Hungarians, etc., and they cannot be com-
pared with our Australian workers, who are their
superior in every way. After having seen and
studied the American workman at work, I am con-
vinced that our Australians are quicker and better
workers, and if Australian employers could only
realise what magnificent material they have, as
compared with the American employer, we would
have more industrial peace.
One thing that has struck me throughout has been
the courteous way the big American employer speaks
to his employee, whether manager or laborer. Very
often, it is true, the one addressed cannot under-
stand English. Some of the factories have notices
posted in a dozen different languages. Australian
employers possess a great advantage in having
workers who can read and write English; it sim-
plifies management so much.
I have studied my own particular business very
carefully in America, and am proud to be able to
say that, generally speaking, our Australian workers
are producing as much per week as American workers,
working under like conditions, and are doing it in
six hours less. The wages earned are practically the
same in both countries.
What we want in Australia is more confidence in
ourselves, bigger ideas and more constructive
thought. When I see the wonderful business that
America has built up, both at home and abroad, and
the poor class of labor that she has done it with, l
have to take my hat off to her Captains of Industry.
They are truly big men with big ideas. They think
in continents. And I say to the employers of Aus-
tralia : Have confidence. Acquire the big outlook.
And get to it.
Re Hughes v. Watson.
Dear Bulletin, —When the settlement of
this action first took place I refrained from
disclosing the terms to the press, believing
that it was no part of my duty as a solicitor
to publish abroad my client’s affairs. In
view, however, of the statements made by
members during the recent no-confidence
debate in the House of Representatives, I
now feel it incumbent upon me to set out
the truth concerning the matter in justice to
the defendant, David Watson. In the course
of the debate it was asserted by members
sitting on the Government side of the House
that Watson had withdrawn his charges
and that Hughes had emerged triumphant
and covered in glory. The fact is that the
action was settled on terms favorable to my
client both in honor and from a monetary
standpoint, and the negotiations for settle-
ment were commenced, not by Watson, but
by or on behalf of the Prime Minister, 'mere
is no vestige of truth in the allegation that
Watson withdrew one single word of what
lie said either in the House or at the party
meeting. From first to last he stood firm
in the assertion that every word he had
spoken was the unvarnished truth, which
attitude he still maintains. Shortly before
the action was to come to trial overtures
were made by certain friends of Mr. Hughes
for a settlement, and proposals were sub-
mitted and discussed, the action being
adjourned pending the discussion of these
terms. Watson refused to entertain the
terms of settlement offered on behalf of
Hughes, but made two counter proposals,
and in September last I informed the other
side that unless they accepted one or the
other of those proposals without further
delay the action must proceed to trial. This
brought forth an immediate acceptance of
one of our offers, and the action was settled
on these terms-—that while Watson did not
withdraw anything that he had said either
in the Senate or at the party meeting, he
stated that his intention in making those
statements was not to charge Hughes with
bribery, but to protect his own and his
party’s interests. This offer, which was sub-
ject to the payment of Watson’s costs by
Hughes, was agreed to, and the costs were
ultimately paid.—Yours faithfully, Gordon
J Holmes, solicitor for David Watson.
If John Bull Votes No.
It is mentioned with regret that so far no
buyer has appeared for the new Australian
wheat crop. The State and Federal autho-
rities are, of course, pledged to buv the crop
at a cost of £16.000,000 or £17,000,000, but
if they can’t sell the goods the promissory-
note given in payment is likely to be a very
pathetic and depreciated document. And if
the goods can’t be exported, even the pro-
missory-note may not be forthcoming in
another 12 months. So far the British
Government has been the principal buyer—
at its own price of course; for when the
helpless Australian can’t deliver his wheat
in the world’s markets he is wholly at the
mercy of the rare purchaser who is willing
to take delivery here and be his own sea
carrier. Now, even Bull's immense mercan-
tile fleet is kept so busy in one way and
another by the war that he hasn’t managed
to get more than a fraction of the wheat he
has paid for away to his own hungry land.
Even if the price paid for Australian wheat
is not very high Bull therefore made a poor
bargain, and it is quite possible that when he
has finished paying up on the present con-
tract he may think hard before renewing it
.on the old terms. He may simply vote No.
Britain may be hungry, but over 9,000.000
bags of provender stacked at N. S. Wales
railway stations —which is over 20 times as
much as was similarly bogged at the same
places two years ago—make it no less
hungry, and N. S. Wales isn’t much worse in
this respect than its neighbors. Our valued
old parent may quite logically remark that
there is an almost unlimited and high-priced
market for foodstuffs delivered in London
or Liverpool or Glasgow, and that if the
seller will deliver there the old man will
be so glad to see him that he will nearly
blow off his waistcoat-buttons with applause.
In a country where it is quite common for
a loaf and 41b. of bacon to be delivered at
the buyer’s door this attitude—if our leading
customer should adopt it —will he quite
understandable. Australia has had one
referendum and another referendum, and it
has a hopelessly unfinished Sydney city rail-
way, and a practically uninhabited Federal
capital which has been a sink for money, and
a scheme for State life insurance in Queens-
land. and a dream about the N.S.W. Govern-
ment buying up all the Cornstalk coal-
mines and paying for them with its lOTJ,
but nothing has been done to provide for the
grievous possibility that the Bull Govern-
ment may be driven to pay for wheat and
wool and meat and copper only on delivery.
Yet when that handy old cargo-carrier the
Gabo, of 2000 tons, came on the market the
other day she was sold to a Chinaman instead
of being acquired as a wheat-ship. Not that
one Gabo would make much difference, hut
the ancient sea warrior was one of the
straws which show how the wind blows.
The Struggle in the Bush.
“R.F.W.” FKOM the Dorrigo big timber: —
“Mookumwh'YßEßa” (B. 3/l/’18) makes out a
good case for the increased timber royalties in N. S.
Wales, the apparent uselessness of the Forestry Com-
mission and the shameful waste of timber, especially
of the fast-disappearing hoop-pine; but when he
leaves such obvious facts and deals with the alleged
huge profits made by sawmillers, he gets
bogged. His statement that “the main factor in the
huge increase in the cost of pine is the practical
elimination from competition of Baltic Oregon’’ is,
as far as this part of N. S. Wales is concerned, totally
wrong. In three years we have seen the cost of
logs, labor, transport, saws, oils, etc., practically
doubled. “Mookumwhybera.” reckons that there is
a wide margin between 9d. per 100 ft. (presenc
royalty) and 255. to 30s. per 100 ft. charged for their
output by the millers. There is ; but the following
figures have to be crowded in:—‘
Cost per 100 ft. (in log) up to December, 1917 6/3
Deduction for waste, 20 p.c. (say) .. .. 1/-
Cost of milling, docking, stacking, classing, etc. 4/8
Freight to Bellingen .. .. . • ■ • 47-
Freight to Sydney .. .. .. •• 4/0
Total 20/3
To these charges must be added wharfage and insur-
ance and (from this month onward) a further 9d.
per 100 ft. increased royalty.
*Kven then a margin of profit is shown; but, often,
special conditions butt in. For instance, our Dorrigo
timber must be carted 20 miles over one of the worst
roads in the world, including seven miles down the
blastiferous Dorrigo mountain, while the ends of our
cherished 18in. boards bang an accompaniment. If
“Mookum, etc.,” could see some of the deductions
for “split ends,” he would not mind if I slipped
another shilling into the expense account. The
miller “glutton” quoted by “M.,” who made £3OOO
in six months, was not up against these conditions.
I strongly suspect him of being a liar, anyhow.
“Mookum’s” reference to the harassed toiler who
cannot make the price of bowyangs and dungarees
at cutting and hauling logs will bring a grin to the
dial of many a miller as he hands out the monthly
cheques. A pine-faller in this district would turn
the glassy eye on any proposition that did not yield
him £1 per day; and if the bullocky, after a full
month’s toil, does not average about 30s. per day he
proceeds to embalm the atmosphere in his time-
honored way.
Every miller with the interests of the industry at
heart must agree that the waste of the heads of trees
is horrible; but would “Mookumwhybeka” like to
cut box timber and broom handles at a price that would
not pay for haulage? Those lines are probably pro-
fitable to men in the business who are alongside a
railway, or who can take contracts with a reasonable
prospect of being able to fill them ; but we on the
Dorrigo cannot make forward contracts while our
timber is likely to lie at a bar-bound port for
months, and then, as frequently happens, be washed
out to sea by floods.
If a percentage of the increased royalty could be
retained in the district that earns it for the build-
ing of decent roads it would prove a blessing to a
sorely-harassed industry. Two mills, within 10 miles
of Dorrigo, have been unable to get a load of sawn
timber out of their yards for weeks, the track being
impassable. Our hopes of rail have vanished with
the lately-removed buildings that housed the Norton
Griffiths smell, so we must continue to battle with
the primitive conditions that obtained when the first
settlers, deluded with promises of a railway, began
to belt a living out of the scrub with axe and fire.
Within a five-mile radius of where I write timber
valued at £5,000,000 has gone up in smoke for the
lack of decent roads and transport facilities. And
there is a lot more to go up yet.

The Possibility of Talking Ships into
the Water.
As a general rule nothing is finished until
after it has been commenced, and acting
upon this principle a busy little Sydney citi-
zen has managed to achieve results while
other people were merely displacing the
atmosphere. Dr. Arthur, M.L.A., seized
with the notion that homes should be built
for returned soldiers, long ago began calling
upon his fellow citizens to help him build
the chimney, or put -on the roof, or deposit
material for the verandah on the section, or
supply a few nails. No matter what may
be said about all this, there is the inescap-
able fact that houses have materialised —that
where before was only air are now dwellings
composed of material previously lying about
the continent. Of course, Arthur has talked
a lot. He had to. But he talked bricks into
walls, and his conversation put timber on
carts and nailed windows in and put a handle
on the kitchen door. Now The Bulletin
earnestly implores another busy little man
to talk a ship into existence instead of talk-
ing Australia to death. For nine months W.
Hughes has been holding conferences about
shipbuilding, announcing that something
was going to be none, making final arrange-
ments, explaining that another conference
would be held shortly, bringing matters to a
head, getting everything into working order
and ensuring success for an industry which
unless firmly established would lead to the
disruption of the Empire. Why not appoint
Arthur as publicity-agent and get down to
plain business? Arthur, this paper takes it,
would at once proceed to waylay all the
reporters, with the result that to-morrow
every citizen would be reading something like
this: —•
SHIPS TO BE BUILT AT ONCE!
PRIME MINISTER TAKES ACTION.
FOOD FOR THE ALLIES.
It has been decided by Mr. Hughes that a begin-
ning shall be made on Saturday with the construc-
tion of ships to carry Australian produce oversea.
Flag of the Stars.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The flag of the Southern Cross was hoisted on
Eureka Stockade, Ballarat, 63 "years ago in Decem-
ber, 1854, to the accompaniment of the oath of the
rebellion: “We swear bv the Southern Cross to be
true to each other, and to fight to defend our rights
and liberties.”
Flag of the Stars! The azure gonfalon
That is the sign and symbol of our land,
Shot-torn and bloodied, when it lost it won
For us a destiny and high command —
Firm as our granite, deep as sky and sea,
The stainless faith that bows no craven knee!
Flag of the Stars ! Eureka’s sun still burns
The old-world draff, the old-world craft
and lies;
Steadfast we hold the quality that spurns
The quibble and the traitorous surmise.
Dare we forget the duty that you made
Ours when you floated over the Stockade?
Flag of the Stars! Spread nightly in our
skies,
Covering our Mother, body, foot and hand,
Nor king nor kaiser, nor their swords nor
lies,
Shall bear our banner from its high com-
mand ;
Nor baulk the destiny that gives thee wings
Above the bloody banners of all kings.
Flag of the Stars! Of our white continent
The shield, redemption, promise and the
sign ;
Depth of the sea, height of the firmament,
Be in our love for thee, and all of thine !
Emblem of wide content, of bale and barge ;
Hope of the white man’s world from marge
to marge.
Flag of the Stars! That sees old shibboleth —
Catch-words from old-world lands—grow
weak and die;
Old-world beliefs become but empty breath,
And kings as phantoms, myths and
mockery.
Land that did make us, take us, break us
when*
Thou, as our Mother, shall demand thy men.
Flag of the Stars! Height saves thee from
the mire
As distance saved when shame the lands
defiled ;
Fate held our white isle sea-walled from the
fire—
No tyrant piping our free ears beguiled.
So let our height and distance be from
wrong—
Till faith grow vocal in Australia’s song.
Flag of the Stars! Wave message to our
men,
To tender wife and old and failing sire,
To give, with gladness, life and courage, when
Come storm and stress ashore with steel
and fire ;
Giving our Mother, loving, loved and blest,
The life she gave back to her gracious breast.
Flag of the Stars ! Clean as thy high-swumg
field
Shall be our love for thee, our sacred land :
The brightness that the Cross of Stars can
yield
Be in our souls to make-pure our command.
Flag of the Stars! As aspirant and high
As thee, be our desire for thee to die!
QUEENSLAND.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

All that is wanted is material and men. Mr. Hughes
therefore invites every citizen willing to lend a hand
in this great national work to assemble at the Town
Hall at 6 a.m. on Sunday, bringing with him his
lunch, a hammer and some nails, and any spare
timber available. The citizens who answer this
summons will be conveyed on lorries to the water-
front, where immediately on arrival a start will be
made laying the keels of as many vessels as possible.
Donations oi material will be thankfully received,
and it is the particular wish of Mr. Hughes that
the ladies will take the opportunity of witnessing
the proceedings and providing additional refresh-
ments.
By the.se methods Arthur has achieved
houses. It is obviously within the compe-
tence of Hughes to achieve ships by the
same system if all other means fail to
assemble a single packet of bolts and a
crosscut-saw at any identifiable spot. It may
be that in a democracy all national under-
takings should be carried on by voluntary
effort, and that by this means we would ail
be inspired with a higher sense of citizenship
and common brotherhood. Hughes in asso-
ciation with Arthur might be the standard-
bearers of a new evangel. The Bulletin
does not know. It only knows that jawing
about ships is inexpressibly wearisome, and
that until somebody gets to work no ships
will be launched. Will Hughes do some-
thing? Or get out of the way and let some-
body else do something?
Cheese and Mites.
Woodrow Wilson having spoken again and
Lloyd George having delivered another
speech, the German Imperial Chancellor has
made a few remarks. And if anyone can
read into Von Hertling’s observations any
indication that the Kaiser is anxious to talk
peace except upon the basis of a substantial
swag for Prussia, then such person has an
advantage over this paper. The German
Chancellor says in effect to the Allies: “We
are ready to discuss bringing the war to an
end provided you are ready to give us what
we want. If you are not prepared to do this
—well, God is with us, and through Von
llindenbubg and Ludendorf will compel
you to.” No matter how this ’speech may
be discounted nor what ridicule is thrown
upon the German claims, there is no getting
away from this —that there is a confidence
about it which gives no support to the
theory that the German’s tail is down.
There has been no qualification, apparently,
about the German demands on Russia
either. Whatever differences there may be
between Berlin and Vienna concerning the
dismemberment of the Bear are in all pro-
bability being much exaggerated ; in any case
Germany and Austria are not likely to come
to loggerheads so long as both parties can
sit back and watch the Russian cut his own
throat. This he seems to be doing with
increasing thoroughness, to the great satis-
faction of Potsdam. Meanwhile though
General Rain has enforced a stay of pro-
ceedings in France and Flanders, the
enemy’s activities elsewhere have been fairly
extensive. The announcement that 1350
tons of bacon and 1800 tons of cheese had
been sunk during one week in December on
the way to John Bull is a reminder that
this country’s leg has been pulled about the
solving of the submarine and torpedo pro-
blems. The food difficulty is, indeed, begin-
ning to reach the stage to which the Ger-
man had counted on bringing it, and even if
it .be not. possible for him to starve the
Allies out it is a long way from being certain
that, after a time, there will be much more
in British kitchens than there is in the
German’s; and it is not at all certain either
that the British will stand an empty stomach
more stoically. Maybe this has something
to do with the development of a campaign
in London to secure a change in the higher
command of the British forces with a view
to pressing matters forward. Whether there
are better men available than those in com-
mand—how can we know ? We cannot even
know how bad (or good) those in command
are: for operations during the past two
years have been heavily camouflaged. So
much has this been so that in at least three
important instances there has be,en gross
distortion. That, of course, is not Haig’s
fault. It may not be his fault that distor-
tion was deemed necessary. But it may be.
Considering the school in which they were
trained, it would be almost miraculous if
Haig and Robertson could do their job in a
plain, business-like way. Already changes
have been initiated for co-ordinating the
strategy of the allied forces. Alterations
have been made in commands in Italy and
France and Germany. The Bulletin is
not going to be surprised to see something
of the kind occurring soon in connection with
the British armies, both at the War Office
and in the field. But it will be neither
elated nor disappointed, mainly because it
doesn’t know even a fractional part of what
there is to know about the matter. What
it does know is (1) that if the German can
be pitched out the time is ripe for someone
to do it, and (2) it is by no means certain
that, unless the American can push in very
quickly, Time is fighting with the Allies.
This makes it urgently necessary that we
should make our own utmost effort quickly.
The deplorable fact is that, our little tinpot
political crisis being past, we are making
practically no effort at all. Two months
ago the country was overrun with politicians
canvassing for votes. We shall be surprised
if there are in the whole continent six
politicians who at this moment are canvass-
ing for recruits.
No man ever started a job under more un-
favorable conditions than those that faced
S. A. Pethebridge when he was made
Administrator at Rabaul, German New
Guinea. The whole truth about the matter
will probably never be told; but it is
admitted that the very early Australian
“administration” left a stain which needed a
lot of wiping out, and Pethebridge, till then
secretary to the Defence Department, under-
took the job. The Bulletin’s contributor,
Tiios. J. McMahon, impartially summed-up
the results (B. 17/1/’18) after recent per-
sonal inspection and inquiry :
On his arrival in German New Guinea Samuel
Pethebridge found chaos such as might and would
have dismayed many a capable man; found revenues
and their channels destroyed; found the Germans
sullen, treacherous, and attempting risings almost
every day for months ; found trade smashed, and the
whole prospects of the territory so blighted that it
did not seem worth an effort to restore it. When
these tilings are remembered and the position to-day
is considered, there can be no question that good
work has been done. Not only has every previous
channel of revenue been restored, but new ones have
been added, until the annual revenues are practically
double what they ever were in the German days.
These revenues carry on the territory and entail no
direct charge on the Commonwealth for administra-
tion. By tact, combined with firmness, the Admin’s
trator has settled definitely the restlessness of the
Germans; they admit they are getting a fair deal,
such as they might expect under British military
occupation.
But the man has had to pay the price : he
contracted malaria and died of heart-failure
last week while on furlough in Melbourne.
Now, his work done, over his grave Aus-
tralia may well say her thanks to him for not
only retrieving her good name in the German
colony, but for demonstrating Australia’s
capacity to administer a captured territory
with tact and justice and all those other
qualities of which success is compounded.
The rumor that President Wilson has at
last taken a hand in Irish affairs does not
exactly snatch the breath away; for as the
U.S.A. are more Irish than the British Isles,
it seemed inevitable that sooner or later
America must speak. Like Hellas of
ancient memory, Ireland has no territorial
boundaries. Where the Hellenic language
was spoken and the Hellenic institutions
prevailed, there was Hellas. In the Crimea,
in Sicily, and in the south of Gaul there
were Greek colonies—they were all Greece
to the man in Athens or Corinth. So it has
come to be with the Irish race. Good
enough colonists under any flag, they still
have the passionate attachment to national
ideas and ideals ; and in numerical strength
the world-wide Ireland is pretty well as big
as England. So, this vast Ireland outside the
land betw’een Donegal and Wexford having
at last become articulate, there is a great
thrill of hope running from Moyle to Kerry,
and from Sydney to New York, that the
seven hundred years war between Celt and
Saxon is very nearly over.
