“Phthisis” : Of all the farces being staged
by the Defence Department the Mont Park
(Vic.) Sanatorium is the saddest. Young
men’s lives are being thrown away there.
Anybody who understands tuberculosis
knows that most early cases can be cured if
given a prolonged, uninterrupted course of
good food, fresh air and rest, with gentle,
graduated exercise; but anything in the
shape of dissipation, over-exertion or excite-
ment is fatal to progress, and a few hours’
indulgence may undo the work of months of
patient treatment. Yet to people living
near Mont Park it is a common thing to
see unfortunate men coming home at all
hours, dead tired, apparently well under the
influence of alcohol, some obviously in a
high fever, and with a long uphill walk
before them. Even in the sanatorium itself
life seems to be a round of billiards, cards,
concerts and visitors —all very enjoyable,
no doubt; but things which no State or pri-
vate sanatorium would tolerate. The suf-
ferers also travel up and down in the sub-
urban trains, and do not appear to be sup-
plied with sputum flasks. In a private in-
stitution four or five guineas a week would
be charged for treatment and board alone.
At Mont Park clothes and Red Cross com-
forts of all kinds are given in addition to
treatment and board, many extras are pro-
vided, and, to cap all, the men are on full
pay or pension. Surely, then, it is not too
much to expect the recipients to submit to
the necessary restrictions in their own in-
terests ! If they were incurables no one
would mind them having a short and merry
time of it; but hopeless cases are sent to
the Austin Hospital, where they lead a
much quieter life. Most of the patients at
Mont Park with six months’ careful treat-
ment would be well on the way to health.
The present methods mean death or relapse
to the sufferers and menace, to the public,
while the expenditure, judging by results,
is a shocking waste of money.
[Permission to publish which is graciously given
provided this is published, too: —•
SUBJECT: TREATMENT OF MEN SUFFERING
FROM PHTHISIS —MONT PARK SANATORIUM
ARTICLE SUBMITTED BY The Bulletin.
“A few cases of irregular conduct have occurred
amongst the patients at Mont Park Sanatorium. The
statements made in the article are grossly exag-
gerated.
“It must be remembered that most of these men
have been discharged from the Service, and, there-
fore, cannot be brought under the regular military
discipline.
“When a discharged man does not prove amenable
to the proper control demanded by his physical con- •
dition and Avill not obey the orders of those responsi-
ble for his welfare, he can only be discharged from
the Institution. This action is taken whenever neces-
sary, but in the best interests of the men themselves,
it is only done as a last resource, because undoubt-
edly such cases will usually be placed in a much
worse position as regards their prospects of recovery
when left to their own devices, than when retained
in the Sanatorium.”
The signature to which is A. H. Thwaites, Lt.-
Colonel.]
It seems pretty hopeless, this attempt to get
the opinion of the people upon any given
subject. A general election can’t do it, of
course; and under present conditions a
referendum isn’t much better. For in-
stance, Australia tried the other day to get
the verdict of the country on Conscription;
and since then The Bulletin has been
endeavoring to find out why various people
voted as thev did. And this is a character-
istic letter on the subject:—
As a reader for the past 20 years I thought it
might be interesting for you to know why I voted
No. 1 am in the three-ball business in this city
[Adelaide]. A few weeks after the war started the
Commonwealth Government announced that all who
owed money to people ip the same line of business
as myself and went into camp would be relieved of
their obligation to pay. Naturally that regulation
taken advantage of, and when we made applica-
tion for payment we were told to go to . Over
that we lost hundreds of pounds. One case: I lent a
man £B5. He paid off £lO. War broke out. An
English reservist, he enlisted. He is still here, and
has never been away. He is a staff sergeant-major
earning 14s. daily, but I have never had a penny
piece from him for 3% years. Principal security,
motor-car. Puzzle now, find the car.
A few months after the war started I awoke one
morning to find that a civilian moratorium had also
sprung into existence, compelling us to g;et consent
of court before taking possession of our security.
More people defied us. I went to law with one who
owed us £24, and had not paid anything for 18
months. We won the day, of course, got a verdict
(without costs). It left us £9 9s. out of pocket.
Most of the customers who take advantage of the
moratorium were never so well off in their lives
before. What, then, becomes of me? The bank calls
up its overdraft. Every penny I can get in goes
out that way. No moratorium for me, no nothing!
So another vote —probably Rebecca’s vote
as well as Abraham’s —went against Con-
sciiption, though, frankly, Abraham didn’t
consider the merits of compulsory versus
voluntary service at all. It becomes more
and more evident that, while democratic
sentiment insists upon the free use of the
referendum, safety and commonsense equally
insist upon a strenuous effort at the politi-
cal education of those who are to do the
voting.
Mitchell, the Adelaide S.M., declared the
other day that there wasn’t a man living
who knew all the War Precaution regula-
tions. Yet besides those regulations the
newspaper is supposed to know about 5000
Censorship instructions, which have been
dribbling in ever since the war started. Ot
course nine-tenths of them are most of the
time in tho dust-bin. If they were not, our
esteemed contemporary The Hen is about
the only paper that wouldn’t be liable to
have half its matter thrown out. And
even The Hen would have to be careful.
An announcement that eggs are plentiful at
Warwick (Q.) might be construed as preju-
dicial to recruiting in that district. More
idiotic things have happened. A paper was
warned once, for instance, not to make
the Fat man so fat and contented. It was
prejudicial to recruiting.
Nobody ever poked into the affairs of the
Defence Department without holding his
nose as he came away. Auditor-General
Israel is the latest. In his 1916-17 report
he mentions, among other things, a trifle of
£177,391 alleged to have bifcn paid to sol-
diers, but for which there are no vouchers.
In the Ordnance branch the stock sheets are
not worth the paper they are written on ;
and in Queensland stock has not been taken
since the beginning of the war. Naturally,
with business run on these lines, “receipt
vouchers are defective and serious irregu-
larities with regard to the issue of cloth and
other stores were disclosed.” Still, Par-
liament assumes that it would be a
national calamity if the men who are Min-
isterially responsible for this state of things
were superseded. So does the Argus. So
does the S. M. Herald. Presumably they
approve of this state of things.

A delicate fantasy is “The Willow Tree,”
presented at Sydney Criterion last Saturday.
The plot is slight, being merely the incar-
nation of an ancient legend of Japan. An
Englishman lapses to Japanese ways and
thought, living as a Japanese, and writing
poems to the rain-god. So occupied he falls in
love with a life-size image of a tree-spirit of
olden time, buys it from the ancient image-
maker, and places a mirror in the wooden
doll’s bosom. Naturally the image awakens
to life, and her charm infatuates the Eng-
lishman. But there is an English girl in
the background, and she arrives at the Gar-
den of Happiness. Just then comes the call
of the war. But the Englishman cannot
give up his princess. She, however, learn-
ing that his love for hej.’ is holding him back
from his duty, saves him from himself by
deliberately renouncing her brief life; and
as the willow-tree, of which she was the
spirit, is cut down by her orders, she sinks
back into the wooden effigy carved by the
old image-maker. The fantasy depends
solely on the beauty of its idea and upon the
atmosphere. Since the whole story takes
place within a tiny, one-apartment Jap-
anese garden house, there is little room for
action: the romance is practically static.
This effect —a legitimate effect in drama,
provided that it is sustained by the statu-
esque poses of the actors and the atmosphere
of unreality—is heightened by the fact that
the image even on awakening to life remains
fettered in her movements by the costume
and ceromonial of old Japan, and even at
her pathetic gayest is restrained by her
ghostly inheritance of immobility. Even
the man cannot break that calm, for drama
is heavily handicapped when it is horizontal
instead of perpendicular. The most impres-
sive moments pass while the two recline on
mats on the floor. And in the end move-
ment entirely ceases: the girl slowly freezes
back to wood, and the man lies at her feet
as if dead. On Saturday the beautiful stag-
ing and the romantic lighting of the
garden and its tiny house made the difficult
illusion more than possible. The play moved
with a solemn beauty from morning to
moonlight, the little stage in the garden
being thrown into an unearthly prominence
—a beautiful Maeterlinck effect. The only
jarring note was the unlikeness of the prin-
cess to her image. Even the image’s hair
was done in a different fashion.
With one exception the play was excel-
lently cast. Evidently there are two George
Barnums: the efficient producer and the

AN EXCELLENT OPPORTUNITY TO JOIN A TECHNICAL UNIT.
Our Instructors have arranged a Special Class for those desirous of serving the Empire in the Army.
The Tuition extends over four months —Instruction three evenings a week. The course will be made
quite practical, use being made of the installations in the school, also of an aircraft set now being
installed especially for this course. The instruction will include that essential - for the Cavalry
and Motor-car Stations, Aeroplane Apparatus, etc., etc.
A SPECIAL RATE OF £8 FOR THE FULL COURSE has been decided on.
All particulars on application to THE MARCONI SCHOOL OF WIRELESS, Dept. C, 97 Clarence Street, Sydney.

Every baby in Sydney is advised to shout
its grandfather to “Robinson Crusoe” at the
Grand Opera House. He will enjoy it.

“Turn to the Right,” the sugary play of
crooks and peaches, passed its 100th Sydney
performance at the Palace on Tuesday and
souvenirs were handed round. But it’s the
haunting fragrance of “Maw’s” peach-jam
that is drawing the crowd.
“The Bing Boys,” at Sydney Majesty’s,
are proudly wearing in their buttonholes an
approving comment clipped from a religious
paper’s theatrical column. But the worldly-
wise critics of some religious papers are not
as religious as usual when they take a night
ott and go to a fizzy show.
* * * *
With potted plays and canned pantomime
the Royal Strollers make the Sydney Play-
house gay. “Aladdin” is the tinned
burlesque that will be tin-opened to-night.
P.S.—There is no risk of ptomaine poisoning
in the Playhouse brand of potted panto-
mimes.
* * * #
“Parentage,” which is now enticing the
sentimental type of citizen and citizeness
to Sydney Royal with the promise “It will
stain your cheeks with tears,” is true to its
dismal word. It is built on the plan of the
old temperance chart which showed two
equally nice infants, and then contrasted
them, stage by stage, until one had developed
into a bleak, side-whiskered wowser of
purest ray serene and the other was a cheery,
beery ruin. A comedy film helps the long-
drawn sermon out, and the Australian toni-
cal pictures are interesting.
* * * H*
“Baby Mine” stifles its yells, throws its
feeding-bottle from the roof of Sydney
Tivoli, and fades away on Friday night.
(Flowers, by request. If the public doesn’t
provide them the management will have to.)
The bill still offers with proper pride bari-
tone Peter Dawson, and the managerial ad.
intimates that patrons desiring special songs
should address their remarks to the stage
manager. Buxom soprano Annette George,
the gentle Satinettos, with their art-drapery
turn, the Creole Fashion Plate and eomedi-
onne Etnel Vaugn fill in the rest of the
evening.
#* ❖ *
“Hashimura Togo,” a story based on the
Japanese conception of honor, holds pride
of place at Hoyt’s and the Haymarket in
Sydney. Sessue Hayakawa makes Togo a
convincing and attractive character. Added
to the exquisite Japanese settings and the
quaintly-phrased sub-titles is an all-round
excellent production.
Dorothy Kelly and Montagu Love make a
big appeal from the Crystal Palace screen.
As Marguerite and Jacoues they find and
nearly lose love again amid the color-tubes
and brushes of the Latin Quarter. How
the fair canvas of their romance escapes
smudging-out by the villain makes an effec-
tual and human story ; and for the rest of
the week Jack Livingston goes through
seven reels of adventure at the btrand
‘‘Because of a Woman.”
“Winnie Brooke, Widow,” the little musi-
cal comedy that Ada Reeve brought to Mel-
bourne Tivoli last Saturday, seems all the
more a success because it finishes with a fast
run after a somewhat sedate beginning. The
comedy has a plot from which there are no
wild departures —a plot founded on Ada’s
sweA resolve to burst up a mock-monastic
brotherhood” at Dunfield’s Priory, with a
view to marrying the celibate brother-in-
chief. The fact that two young girls of her
acquaintance are pining for minor members
of the futile brotherhood broadens the in-
terest in her matrimonial designs on the
establishment. “Winnie Brooke, Widow,”
is, in fact, a light and frivolous suggestion
of Glory Quayle planning to lure John Storm
from his “monkery” and make a harmless
man-of-the-world of him. To which extent
it confirms tliis writer in his suspicion that
somebody ought to get hold of Hall Caine
and turn him into a musical-comedy satirist
of his own gloomy melodramas ere he dies.
Nothing that happens in this whimsical
comedy is irrelevant to the simple narrative.
The musical items are incidental, rather than
accidental, to the dialogue, and the dances
are legitimately related to it. Also, the
characters are fitted to the co. The per-
formance is good stuff quite apart from the
re-introduction of some of the popular ditties
that Miss Reeve and Talleur Andrews in-
cluded in their vaudeville programme of
vore. Their duet towards the end of the
show is charming • all the concerted pieces
that occur thereabouts are catchy and full
of go, and the finale is a happy idea.
* * * *
Ada Reeve’s way of managing the affair
is inimitable, of course. An artist in all she
does, she has the rare knack of carrying her
audience with her all the time. She
naturalises the proceedings. She lends im-
portance to Clarence Blakiston (who can’t
sing or dance w r orth tuppence) because he is
supposed to be the cranky object of her
affections, and she gives an air of reality to
other people’s love-affairs which might be
profoundly unconvincing if she were not
there. Talleur Andrews walks easily
through the part of a solicitor, Ashton Jarry
and Jacobs are two lovesick novices from the
wondrous monkery, Allen and Miss De Lara
are an all-right couple of grotesque wowsers,
and Charles Albert and Lucie Linda are
distinctly valuable as a pair of humorous
menials who can dance. Everybody already
knows that Albert can be funny, but the
low-comedy talent of Lucie Linda comes as a
joyful surprise. A limited chorus does its
bit neatly, and is remarkable for never being
allowed to sing out of its turn. The chorus
in most musical comedies seems to come on
and sing whenever it likes.
* * *
At Melbourne Princess’s the Prince of
Sax-Steinberg (who is Wilkie in drunk’s
clothing) continues to swank into the toy
kingdom of Lividia, and demand the hand of
Princess Marie Ney in unholy matrimony.
Prince Edwards of Calvaria continues to
thwart his puvpose, but whether the three
Kingdoms mentioned call out their respective
invisible armies and fight to a finish is not
stated in the drama. Probably they go to
war and all the dynasties are obliterated b.v
Bolshevik revolutions, or they perish of food-
shortage. Anyhow, the play is called “The
Lifeguardsman,” and it will be performed as
before until further notice.
** * *
“Whittington,” at Her Majesty’s, is still
resisting the temptations to turn back to
London and be beguiled by Maggie Dickin-
son. The exact nature of the temptations is
still open to argument. They either come to
the young man in a dream, or else Arch-
Fiend Arnold conjures up visions of fair
women for Whittington’s undoing. In
neither case can the temptations be very
strong, seeing that the principal boy is a girl
to whom the glad eyes of the syrens will
hardly appeal.
“Aladdin,” too, is sticking to business at
Melbourne King’s. Here they have a harle-
quinade at the matinees, and the clown’s
awful thefts of meat from the butcher remind
one that clowns can hardly be doing the
same old felony in London just now. The
joke would be too cruel —even in panto.
:J; * * *
At Melbourne Royal comes a change of bill
on Saturday. “The Easiest Way” displaces
the “Bird of Paradise.” Muriel Starr
appears as tlie New York stage-lady who
chooses the easiest way, and the public has
sad misgivings that the way in question is
something young women are expected to feel
sorry for afterwards —especially in drama.
An emotional actress is rarely permitted to
sin with impunity unless the other culprit is
a king, as in Nell Gwynne’s case.
** * *
People are getting an unauthoritative
view of the war through the lens of the
Vitagraph camera at Melbourne Majestic
this week. The story is ■ entitled “For
France,” and is a study of that Yankee
heroism which grows towards invincibility
as the opposition increases. The valiant
Sammy in this fable makes a large Hun
regiment look like an epidemic of infantile
paralysis. “The Stainless Barrier,” also at
the Majestic, is about that “Southern
pride” which is one of the principal raw
materials of the American movie industry.
The Southerners in the present effusion
possess so much pride that they swallow a
lot of it when there is nothing else avail-
able. One of them also swallows his honor
and his sister's and smudges a family record
about 1000 years long.

Hoyt’s Melbourne supporters had their
first glimpse of the Jap screen actor, Sessue
Ilayakawa, on Saturday. Sessue floats
into the film, “Hashimura Togo,” and makes
a valid claim for the front place in a
dramatic equipment that suits the Japan-
easiness of the story. , Sessue does not
belong to the gesticulatory Yankee school.
He acts with his eyes and a half-size smile;
when he uses his hands he does it daintily,
as if he were fingering porcelain. Fanny
Ward idles away the time of a tame ten-
derfoot in the extra feature, “On the Level.”
Fan is the giggling Western heroine who
has to prove to the hero’s ma that some-
times all is gold that titters.
Samuel Houston, who carried a gun for
the Stars and Stripes in Texas and else-
where. is the photographer’s darling in the
Fox film called “The Conqueror,” at Mel-
bourne Auditorium. The picture fills in
gaps in Samuel’s history with fictitious par-
ticulars and credits his success to the
impetus of an amorous feeling. Eliza Allen,
the damsel whom he originally married and

Dr. Wilson (Louis Kimball) before and
after visiting the drowsy tropical island.
left within three months, appears intermit-
tently on the screen as a banal blonde who
goads the hero to celebrity with her scorn-
ful laughter. The second part of the yarn
is mostly devoted to Samuel’s struggle with
a crudely-manufactured villain for a life-

before his ammunition gives out.
The clever, all-round Anderson Co., which
has been playing melodrama of various quali-
ties at Adelaide Tivoli for over a year, is
going to take a spell and make room for
pantomime. Its closing effort is “For a
Child’s Sake,” which gives a good chance to
the clever young Gladys Gaul. The Royal
gets in first with a panto, on the screen—
with a real giant and hundreds of kiddies
enjoying themselves. A line and exciting
turn is that of Delmore and Lee at the
Majestic. They have moved on now, and
Ruth Budd is in their place; there’s a good
supporting co. —notably Percival in his
plain and fancy work on the piano. Roast-
ing weather has given a great chance to the
English Pierrots at the Exhibition Garden,
all rivalry of a similar kind being now at
an end. Elsa Langley is a pleasant circum-
stance, and the old St. Ivilda (Vic.) favor-
ites are winning a steady public in Adelaide,
too. Pictures are many and good. At
West’s, the bedroom door is smashed nightly
in "Bought and Paid For,” and at Wonder-
graph Douglas Fairbanks teaches a crowd of
formerly useless peop.e to live the Simple
Life.
“D.F.” : The wonderful story of the suc-
cessful operation—a world’s record in long-
distance surgery—which Dr. Holland of
Perth carried out by telegraph with the
patient at Hall’s Creek, near the border of
the Northern Territory, over 1500 miles
away, had a tragic sequel. After the post-
master at Hall’s Creek had carried out the
delicate and difficult operation under the
doctor’s telegraphed instructions, the pati-
ent’s brothers telegraphed to Dr. Holland
offering him a fee of £3OO to come up ana
see the case through. The doctor took the
boat to Derby and then started out in a
buckboard to meet someone who went down
from Hall’s Creek with horses. The man
who was to meet him camped too soon, and
there was some delay, with the result that
when Dr. Holland got to Hall’s Creek the
patient had been dead six hours. It wasn’t
anything to do with the operation that
killed him, though, but a severe attack of
malaria.
From the Gallery:—
Customs Minister Jensen is naturally annoyed at
having his motah expenses questioned. And when
you realise Jensen’s enormous services to the Empiah,
it really does seem ungrateful to deny him a few
hundreds for a limousine and etceteras. Indeed,
recognising that Parliamentary privilege is so vast
and the cashbox so easy, one ought to be delighted
with his moderation. But Jensen’s case is not pecu-
liar. He is only one out of 111 who endure the
painful perquisites of their position with stoical
suffering. The Commonwealth, which crowds £6OO
a year into their unwilling paws, tries to destroy
their independence with a free library, free billiards,
free games and free travelling. They are also
threatened with free food; but the fact that the
refreshment-room has been run at a loss of a mere
£lOOO a year show's that the threat hasn’t been
carried into full effect. As for the travelling, every
member and his family is condemned to immunity
from rail fares all over the Commonwealth. The
other day a member who is barely kept from the
brink of starvation by several thousands p.a. found
things so arranged that he and his human belongings
were, for all practical purposes, overlooked by the
ticket-collectors between Melbourne and Sydney. No
doubt this freedom from trouble in travelling, which
has been devised to prevent small worries annoying
minds full of Empiah problems, makes members
careless about the limits of their charitable relief.
The consequence is that they develop lifelong attach-
ments for soap, nail and hat brushes, boot blacking,
stationery, O.S. stamps and other trifles provided for
their convenience. There is now a movement on
foot to furnish free groceries, free vegetables, boots
and cloches for each M.P. Considering that almost
everything else is on the free list, the omission of
those items seems a rank injustice.
“GGG”: Anyone know why returned Bill-
jims refuse to light three men’s cigarettes
with the one match? I recently asked
several, but none was able to give me a
satisfying answer. One mentioned that it
was a recognised superstition among the
Aussies that the third man would find a
bullet.
From the Gallery :—•
The spectacle of William Irvine sitting in isola-
tion in a corner of the Heps, is an indication of his
unyielding attitude to the force of circumstances.
If Iceberg would only suspend his talk about Con-
scription and settle down to be a “good party man,
he might be allowed to contribute the force of his
intelligence to the Cabinet’s exclusive job of Win-
ning the War. But lrVine stays in his little corner
and looks down from the Himalayan altitude of a
“safe seat” on- those who are not so fortunately
circumstanced. Junker-like in his contempt for the
popular vote, his obstinacy on the question is either
tenacity or stupidity, according to your fueling for
the man; but there is no doubt that it is hardening
the personal hostility towards him without serving
any good purpose. When Maxwell, M.11.R., turns on
his legal brother with the straight tip to bury the
subject, one can see Iceberg losing his deposit in a
Popularity Contest. If he persists in calling for
Compulsion, there will surely be a free fight, and
the frozen dignity that has endured for 20 years
will suddenly disappear in a flood of boiling language.
Once or twice lately I have suspected that he was
very nearly losing his temper. We have seen him
grow pinker and pinker under Opposition attacks,
, and up here we have started to wonder how pink he
can get without exploding.

"one should always keep one’s powder dry.”
“True; but I believe in a fresh application
after each engagement.”
Queensland was busy last week doing
things which, so far as preliminary evi-
dence goes, will make the best previous
known deluge blush with shame on a back
seat. The evidence is scrappy as yet, for
telegraphs and telephones and railways are
crippled, and towns are cut off, and Mackay
is a sodden ruin, and isolated stations and
farms and hamlets will be a long time before
they send in their list of dead. As for the
solitary nomad w r ho was overtaken on the
track, he won’t send in any list, and pro-
bably the sludge will get his bones and keep
them. But a flood in Queensland leaves
behind it something besides sludge. In
addition to innumerable records of unobtru-
sive heroism the debris is glided with lavish
subscriptions for the homeless; for they are
a generous lot in the Land of Big Things,
and even such stodgy folk as “our respected
fellow townsman” and “a widely-esteemed
resident” do good deeds which almost excuse
their existence. The bush concert blows
forth much music in the good cause, and
the thud of boots at the charitable dance is
loud in the land. Then the broken bridges
are repaired and the surface of things be-
comes a brighter green, and the grass in
the main street of Port Douglas grows so
high that a small horse is liable to suffocate
amid the foliage. And Queensland presently
goes on its way rejoicing as a good, stalwart
white man in the tropics is always liable to
do.

The padre vouches for this yarn:—
I was cycling through the dark from Poperinghe
to Ypres when an obstruction in the middle of the
road blocked me. Blessed if it wasn’t one of our
heavy guns on a traction wagg;on, and not a soul
near it save myself. ’Twas a mystery to me, and I
went on wondering what part in the scheme of things
that big lone gun was destined to play. I’d pedalled
about three miles further when I heard the rumble
of a big traction-engine in front. I ranged along-
side and hailed the driver and his mate. “What’s
up?” said the driver. “Have a look behind,” T
chuckled. Bill looked back and then hopped down.
For a full minute he gazed in wonderment. Then
lie turned and shouted to his mate: “.Jim, me
if we ain’t gone an’ lost the —-- gun!”

A snort from France: —
I see that back home in Australia the defenders of
machine-militarism have been explaining that it is
the uniform that the soldier salutes, not the man.
You can tell ’em from" me tha,t that is rot.
We’ve got to salute the man even if he’s in pyjamas.
“Try Fluke”; Russell, Bay of Islands
(M.L.), scared itself into a panic when it
got news of the bolt of the German prisoners
from Motuihi Island. One of the boss bum-
bles of the old township, imagining that the
Huns were sure to call and turn the place into
a second Belgium (with a special eye to the
women), established a system of patrols and
refuge-places in the scrub. The dread alarum
was given at two o’clock one morning, and
the whole female population rose from its
blankets as one woman and made tracks for
the tall tea-tree. It was a wet morning, and
there weren’t many dry nighties when the
ladies were tracked to their lairs and led
shivering home to breakfast. It was a fish-
ing-boat coming home from the hapuka
grounds!
“Pull-through”: There is an estimable
sergeant-major, one of the good old bulldog
sort, in a M.L. camp, who has his troubles
in the matter of the letter aitch. At the
morning drill he called out the raw non-coms,
in turn to put the company through. There
must have been a ’orrible conspiracy afoot,
for one after another of the budding ser-
geants laid special emphasis on the “ ’Alt.”
The instructor dropped to the game in a
jiffy. “Look ’ere,” he said ferociously,
“there’s only two men in the regiment who’re
allowed to say ‘ ’Alt!’ One’s the Colonel, the
other’s me. All the others when they want
to say ‘ ’Alt’ must say ‘ ’Alt!’ with a haitch.
“Kola” : A few weeks ago a small army of
blacks, under a smart half-
caste, had the job of putting
up a big set of stockyards for a
station beyond Meekatharra
(W.A.). They finished the job
and were paid. Then they had
a confab as how to spend the
money. As a result the whole
tribe, men, women and kids,
trooped down to Meekatharra.
But not to the pub. Instead,
they called on the local motor-
garage boss, hired his cars, and
for the next w T eek Meekatharra
had the joy of seeing loads of
assorted nigger scooting over
the landscape. The women and
kids seemed to enjoy the fun
best, and the way they squealed
at a sharp turn was something
to be remembered. When the money gave
out a band of blacks, penniless but happy,
took the road to the bush again.
He had shyly dropped in at the office pass-
ing through to the Front, and when he got
there he reported progress:—
I’m doin’ well so fur. Rains have bin interfering
a good deal with the war lately. I haven’t put in
full time one week since I bin here, but it don’t
matter—they don’t stop nothin’ off, no matter how
much you don’t work. This is the only job I’ve had
where they don’t take time off fur wet wether. All
the same, they’d save a lot on this war if they let it
out on contrack.

“Tourniquet”: There is fine dignity in a
letter written to the Melbourne press by
Ilobert O. Tucker, of Belgrave, who uses the
death from overwork of his daughter, Nurse
Hazel Tucker, as a plea to the Melbourne
Hospital committee to lighten the duties of
its nursing staff. Someone wrote to The
Bulletin the other week pointing out that
the Royal Red Cross was an inadequate
reward for the nurse whose life is broken
on the edge of the battlefield. But what of
the nurse in a civil hospital who has not
even the hope of a Red Cross to decorate
her tombstone? Tucker has the support of
medical men in his contention that the
present four years’ course is not necessary
for effective training, and that it is cruel
to compel girls to rise at 5.30 a.m. and
w T ork 10 hours a day at high speed, often
without reasonable time for meals. His
daughter was admittedly a most efficient
nurse. She started her training in January,
1914, and began to break dowm early in her.
fourth year. She refused to ask for sick-
leave, however, being eager to finish her
training. Six weeks before she died, a ton-
silitis operation was followed by vomiting
and hemorrhage. Seven days later she was
sent home with myocarditis. She was
ordered to rest, and to report to the hospital
in a fortnight for medical examination. She
was in bed almost throughout her leave.
When she reported herself she was not
examined, and was sent on duty at six o’clock
next morning. That evening she broke down,
and nine days later she was dead. Those
who saw her die consider that her father has
made out a case which demands an answer.
And every nurse who knew she was dying,
and watched the plucky fight she made, is
waiting to hear w r hat answ r er the Melbourne
Hospital committee can give.
Truth can still give fiction a few' points.
Eric Crofts was a Dunedin medical student
when the war broke out, but a month ago
he was Captain Crofts, twice wounded, and
wearing the Military Cross. Also, he was
engaged to marry Miss Jardine, daughter of
Jardine, M.P., owner or iron-mines and
things. His career had been a sufficient
plot for a sentimental novel up to the even-
ing before the dav fixed for his marriage in
London. The novelist would, of course, ring
in a rival suitor possessed of all the virtues
save heroism, and this poor fellow' would be
eating his heart out. But even the novelist
would jib at the suggestion of bringing the
hero to a violent death on the eve of his in-
tended marriage. Fate didn’t jib, though.
It simply collared young Crofts, threw him
down a staircase and fractured his skull.

My absence is impossible. Someone has removed
my wife. My God, I am annoyed.
It doesn’t take much to irk some of these
natives.

There is a professional man who gave up
a couple of thousand a year to go into camp
well over six months ago and is there still.
“Well, what have you learned. ’ he was
asked the other day. He thought a moment.
Then: “Well, to tell you the truth, nothing
much except how to dress for the job.” It
recalls the story told in “Suvla Bay and
After” of the major who, with buttons,
badges and leggings gleaming out like helio-
graphs in the sun, stood bolt upright on a
knoll with his field-glasses fixed firmly at
his eyes, complaining with bitterness that
“those ruffian Turks were turning out half-
naked to meet the Fusiliers.” The
ruffians now hold Suvla Bay and the major
is dead.

The breeze upon the mountain-top is calling, soft
and low-;
Cool zephyrs from far gullies bid us pack our
bags and go ;
The weary spirit weakens in the battle for the Right,
The soul cries out for something that will help it
in the fight ;
The trout leaps for its breakfast in the sparkling
mountain-stream,
And of baked fish and coffee our drooping patriots
dream —-
Of rest upon the mountain-top, with new-laid eggs
and cream.
The blue sky and the drifting white of downy
clouds that crawl
Above the farthest ranges; ribbon-roads that rise
and fall
Across the summer landscape in the sunshine; and
the rare,
Rich perfume of ripe poultry in the kitchen, roast-
ing there—
These are kind Nature’s tonics for the one with
faltering feet:
A green and grateful pleasaunce where square meals
and Nature meet;
Blue, peaceful skies and gracious bush and stacks
of things to eat.
The fragrance of the blooming gums is like a voice
afar
That bids the glum, down-hearted one gets out his
motor-car,
Leaving the cares of office to the office-boy, and take
The old bush-track once more, to loaf for his dear
country’s sake;
To rot around awhile and view some fair and placid
scene
Where he may find the means to keep his wits and
courage keen,
With snoozes underneath the gums and snacklets in
between.
Yet all the while the blessed war goes on, alacka-
day!
And hour by hour good mates o’ mine proceed upon
their way
To death and glory, scars and slush, chill nights
and roaring morns,
While in Australia’s cool retreats her saviors rest
their corns
And give their brains a holiday. Ye patriots,
hearken where
The nation’s voice, or some of it, speaks from an
easy-chair
Upon the grass, whereon reclines a figure pale and
wan;
“Ah, yes, the war goes on! Alas, this awful war
goes on !....
Well, yes, just half a cup this time —and one more
buttered scone.”

The 500 free insurance policies for £2OO
each offered by Sam McGaughey to recruits
have now all been taken up. Over 90 per-
cent. of the applications came from married
men, and 1500 dependents are getting a little
extra shelter under the scheme, which is
costing £2O per policy per annum. Two of
the insured soldiers (both of them married)
died before they climbed aboard the trans-
port, and the benefits come tyj their people.
About 30 applications froui married men
arrived too late, and the N.S.W. Public
Trustee is listening for the footstep of
another generous citizen and the rustle of
his cheque book, the City Mutual Life hav-
ing intimated that it is prepared to issue
100 policies on the same terms as the Mutual
Life and Citizens handled the 500.

“Murknh”: For the fourth time within
10 months the futility of the postal registra-
tion scheme has been advertised by a big
loss —£1000 worth of banknotes this time.
The fact that the Department has no idea
where the parcel of notes disappeared and
doesn’t even know that it left Sydney is
proof of the rottenness of the system. The
registered articles for (say) Brewarrina are
slung into a. bag in Sydney mail-room and
PLENTY THERE.
THE BLOKE: “ They’ve all got so bloomin’
tame since the war that there’s nowhere
a bloke can find a bit of excitement on
Sunday afternoons now."
THE DEAR THING: “Oh, yes, there is. They
don’t stop fighting on Sundays, you
know."
enclosed in another bag containing the ordi-
nary mail. Both bags are sealed. There-
after, from Sydney to Brewarrina, the vari-
ous officials merely sign for a bag, without
reference to, or inspection of, tne contents.
That the point of loss is not even approxi-
mately known can thus be easily understood.
The seals afford no protection. The wax is
so brittle that a broken seal never excites
suspicion. In any case, the mark left by
a shilling is plenty near enough to pass
the. casual inspection of a straw official or
mail-contractor. The old registration-of-
contents plan with a chain of signatures is
urgently needed. Under this method the
clerks accepting letters from the public
examined the innards and issued a receipt
therefor. The nature of the contents was
marked on the envelope, which was then
scaled. Thereafter each man in the chain
signed for the articles themselves instead of,
as at present, for mere bags, the contents
of which are not even specified and into
which the articles may never have been put
The present method merely provides a heavy
fog (in this instance: 518 miles long) for the
detectives to lose themselves in.
The Melbourne Age has been making
allegations about the use of publicly-owned
motor-cars by certain Federal Ministers and
their female belongings for wholly private
purposes. It may be difficult to make out
a case against a Minister except jn very-
glaring instances, for when he goes joy-
riding in the nation’s vehicle he can at
least allege that he took his documents with
him. and bent his_ gigantic intellect on
public affairs while" speeding through the
scenery. But if it is true that the contrap-
tions which the nation pays for are used by
Ministerial wives .or sisters or cousins or
aunts or flappers to go shopping in. there
ought to be a plain case against the ladies for
misappropriation of petrol. Also there is
a law somewhere about using a car without
the permission of its owner. When plain
Bill Smith employs the railways without
authority he is arrested for not paying his way.
and it is called “travelling without a ticket.”
Yet Bill at his worst doesn’t order that a
train shall be brought out at 10.30 exactly
for his convenience, and tell the driver to
take him to Mordialloc or Parramatta and
there await his convenience. He lacks the
splendid effrontery of some people in higher
walks of life, which may be one reason why
he remains Bill to the end of his days. As
for his wife—well, there is many a hard-
worked washerwoman who might be affluent
if she had learned the art of saving her
boots in her youth.
“Bion”: Our sick transport had
some invalids confined to cots. One
was a young Billjim with home
and parents in Melbourne; but to
suit a whim of General Bed Tape
he was being carried on to Syd-
ney. His father, determined to
get aboard and see his boy, in spite
of regulations, borrowed a post-
man’s coat, slung a bag across his
shoulder and carried some enve-
lopes and papers in his hand. When
the first excitement of being along-
side had died down a bit, we
noticed an old postman coming up
the gangway. He casually nodded
to the watchman, then to the
guard, and made his way on deck.
When in a safe quarter, he sought
his son’s whereabouts. Luckily he
struck somebody who knew and
who guided him to the bedside.
There was a gale of laughter and
cheering when the joke flew round,
and the send-off the “postman” got
when he decided to return home
•tnd report progress to mother nearly blew
his cap off. # .
His friends had been writing dolefully;
and then this reply lobbed in on them from
Kenneth H. Brock, erstwhile mercantile
broker in Melbourne, in these days 0.0. a
trench-mortar battery :
We have never been afraid of Fritz, man for man.
All we wanted was artillery, and at last we have it.
Things like this happen now. Within the area of
our first objective at Polygon Wood was a sandy
mound giving command of all the country round for
some thousands of yaj’ds. This was immediately
seized by our people for an O.P. for artillery and
machine-guns. I had the pleasure of being there
when the troops for the main counter-attack were
endeavoring to form up about 1000 yards away. They
never had a dog's chance. The F. 0.0., directing his
batteries from the mound, poured salvo after salvo
into the assembling troops. The machine-gunners
had the target of their lives. In the excitement of
the moment, I wished I was a machine-gunner
myself as they sat behind (irony of Fate!) captured
German guns and literally mowed down the grey
masses. The attack, needless to say, never developed.
Those who escaped the artillery were caught by our
rifle and machine-gun fire. Fritz's casualties must
have been enormous.
None of us look forward to the winter with
great joy. We will, however, endure it as we have
done before, but now with a certain moral superi-
ority, derived from the occupation of positions and
certain high ground, intended by the Boche for his
winter quarters.
The game is not won yet, but we are bn the
winning side. We know it, every man, from the
General to the humblest private. So cheero, Australia !

“Wart”: Launceston is losing its queer
old Cataract mill chutes, which cling to the
rocks on 'the side of the Cataract Gorge. In
the old days the mill was driven by a
picturesque overshot wheel, but about 20
years ago this was replaced by turbines:
now they give place to the electric motor.
For a generation the chutes were all that
Launceston had to depend on for fresh
water. Wooden pipes carried the liquid to
elevated tanks, and water-carts used to back
under and obtain a fill for a shilling. The
mill itself w r as built by Andrew Sibbald. His
tombstone, which is still to be seen in the
Presbyterian cemetery in High-street, has
this quaint inscription:—-
Sacred to the memory of Andrew Sibbald, miller,
who departed this life 19th of March, 1842, aged 45
years. This inscription is intended to perpetuate the
memory of his valuable services to the inhabitants
of Launceston, having, after a period of two years,
accomplished the arduous undertaking of erecting the
trough which conveys the pure water to the tanks
and now supplies the town. What was he? He was
a man and what a man should be.
No man could be more, could he?
The members of the Australian Women’s
Service Corps in Sydney considered last
week how they could do honor to Billjim
returned. They decided, after meditation,
that they could not very well do it by taking
off their hats, because that would necessitate
halting the soldier while the hatpins were
drawn. Also they didn’t feel equal to throw-
ing. Bill the glad eye, because that might be
“misconstrued”; and they guessed a plain
military salute would get them rushed into
Callan Park. The idea of standing still as
the wounded hero passed was mooted but
was viewed as impracticable in a place
where everybody is in a hurry and walks on

FOUR ROADS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There runs a road in Flanders
Through meadows to the sea,
A long white road, a good straight road,
As all the world may see.
There runs a road in Flanders
Made dusty by men's feet,
And tramp of marching' soldiers
Rings through the noontide heat.
There runs a road in Flanders,
A broken thing of mud,
Where holes yawn wide and cannot hide
The dull red slime of blood.
There runs a road in Flanders,
And long and straight it goes,
A great white path of glory—
How great, the whole world knows.
QUEENSLAND.
R. A. SMITH.
THE SONG OF PEACE.

tend to let the Speck’s applegrowers perish
underneath their trees for lack of deep-sea
freight. A broad, swaggering, grim pirate
chief with a jowl as large as a harvested
wheatfield, the
complexion of a
baptised infant
and the knuckles
of a prize-
fighter, Hayes
started straight
after the inter-

s - A. Pethebridge, the Queensland-born
Administrator of German New Guinea, died
in Melbourne last week of malaria, aggra-
vated probably by cigarettes. His father
was formerly Inspector of Works in Queens-
land ; and h£ himself got into the Common-
wealth public service via the Queensland
Marine Board and the Queensland Naval
Brigade; he was secretary .to one and com-
mander of the other. The Bulletin knew
him as an ardent Australian and a fine jour-
nalist wasted.
Bigjohn Forrest was electioneering in the
West. Ilis wife was pushing the barrow for
the old man before a meeting of females,
and John, overcome by the weather, slum-
bered peacefully in a chair on the platform,
“fie said,” exclaimed the dame, dwelling on
the apathy of the woman-voter in the electo-
rate, “that you were made of wood.” The
sleeping statesman awoke with a start.
“What did you say, Margaret?” “I was
tolling them that when you came home after
addressing the ladies’ meeting here previ-
ously you said they were made of wood.”
“Never said anything of the sort,” growled
John, full of confusion. But his better half
persisted and the candidate subsided until
the feminines hilariously dispersed to spread
the joke. The Bulletin doesn’t’ know what
happened after that.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” discovers the
Oldest Water-waggoner:—
The Rev. AV. P. Crook, of the Congregational
church, whose portrait is in the Mitchell Gallery,
founded in Australia the first temperance society.
Crook was one of the original missionaries to
Tahiti, 1796, and is described later as a “gentle-
man s servant.*’ He got back to England somehow
and reappeared on the Australian coast as chaplain
or schoolmaster to the Collins expedition, which
rested awhile at what is now Sorrento, and three
months later sailed across the Bass to found Hobart
Town. He was certainly an itinerant. During the
Johnston-McArthur regime he was appointed chap-
lain and conducted services at old St. Philip’s;
but when Macquarie arrived that potentate refused
to allow him to administer the Sacrament. It was
on September 10, 1832, that he projected the N. S.
AVales Temperance Society. The first public meeting
was held in the house of cabinet-maker Hunt, in
.Tamison-street, premises not long demolished. Chief
•lustice Forbes presided, and Captain Sir Edward
Parry, the Arctic explorer, was one of the speakers.
Crook subsequently went to Port Phillip and died
in 1847. His body is in the Old Cemetery there.
Dead at S 3. Robert Haden Smith, who
came to Victoria 65 years ago and took the
law into his own hands in 1861, winning the
Supreme Court prize. Smith compiled the
first Victorian law-list, and continued a lead-
ing light in the six-and-eightpenny world. He
married a daughter of Frederick Lord Claye,
another old colonist and a well-known iden-
tity in the city’s earliest musical circles.
Thomas Williams is entered for the
record in his class. When arrested at Ade-
laide the other day for vagrancy he was
tried in the courthouse yard, as the police
superintendent described him as “a moun-
tain of filth with the odor of a burst sewer.”
The beak took a cautious sniff from a dis-
tance of 10 yards, and said the smell was
worth two months. It is understood that
Williams has been mixed up with Federal
politics.
The death of “Buffalo” Robinson, “King”
of Melville Island (N.T.), brought to light
a chapter of romance in the family history
of the Umphelbys. of Melbourne. Edward
Umphelby came from England to Melbourne
by way of Van Diemen’s Land in 1828,
bought land from John Batman now valued
at £132,000. and in 1838 sold it for a mare
in foal. Then he purchased 7500 sheep and
drove them from Mount Alexander (Vic.)
to Adelaide, a three months’ journey. Part
of the goods he got in payment for the sheep
was the schooner Lulworth, in which he
carried buffaloes from Timor to Port Essing-
ton as food for the mainland military settle-
ment. But a quarrel arose, and he landed one
lot of the animals at Melville Island, where
they bred with the stock originally there, and
years later provided Robinson with his life’s
job.
Lieut. Geake, recently awarded the Order
of the British Empire for carrying out risky
experiments in bomb-making, used to follow
the peaceful Sydney rag-trade for a living.
As a drummer he offset his teetotalism by'
a free distribution of cigars. Later he
became buyer for the mercery department of
a York-street house, but was found to be too
fond of doing what he is now rewarded for,
viz., carrying out risky experiments. He
was always wanting to give new ideas a
chancy Ten years ago a few Sydney firms
pooled £2O find handed him a buying
authority which made a run through the
Cold Co tntry and America possible. This
ended his fluff-trade notions. Coming back
to Australia he drifted into the motor-car
business and finally became, it appears, “con-
sulting enginee-.”
Gunner Harry W. B. Gleeson, who was
howled down at a Bendigo referendum meet-
ing, is the sole survivor of eight brothers
His dad was a British soldier, and Harry,
6ft. 2in. in height, was the smallest of the
jAinily. Two of the boys were killed in the
S African War, the other five in France, two
of them falling when the Old Contemptibles
fell back from Mons. Harry, a Dublin Fusi-
lier reservist, was farming in Gippsland
when the gong went. lie joined a Billjim
battalion, and his record includes the Land-
Heles ’ the Evacuation, and most of the
Billjim battles in France up to Armontieres
where he was gassed. The family came from
Tipperary.

teered in Canada recently, but the Australian
artist’s deafness disqualified him, so the Gov-
ernment decided to officially recognise his
recruiting work. He was made an honorary
captain and wears the khaki while address-

Dominion he raised much money for the
returned wounded, and he will return shortly
to conduct another recruiting and fund-
raising campaign. Levy, jun., was attached
to the Signallers, but has been transferred
to the Flying Corps.
George Wall, who died at Randwick last
week, aged 75, was a commercial pillar of
Sydney. As Customs and general agents
the firm of Geo. Wall lead the procession, its
head being president of the Chamber of
Commerce and chairman of the Royal
Exchange. Two sons are on active service,
one a Colonel, another a Major. Dr. Wall,
M.L.C., is also one of the boys:

Chaplain-Captain Joseph J. Booth, winner
of one of the last batch of Military Crosses in
France, is in his capacity of chaplain only
resuming an old connection with powder
and shot. Before his natural earnestness led
him to seek a new collar (Anglican brand)
young Booth was a munition worker at the
Colonial Ammunition Co.’s works at Foots-
cray, his home town. He has been now
about li years with a battalion, winning
golden opinions, for the young padre is
always up in the front where the bullets are
thickest.
Private Ephraim Moss, as you would
expect from his name, is 51. He was on
the Ballarat when she was torpedoed, and
is now at Salisbury Plain. He is the
youngest of four brothers who managed to
overlook the age limit. One was killed at
Lone Pine; another was dangerously
wounded; and the eldest, when the last
mail closed, was in the firing-line in France.
That one came from Echuca," and had 11
children and 10 grand-children when he left.
. A.mong the _ Sydneyside gunners recently
distinguished is Major R. M. Thompson,
who, though only 23, has a D.S.O. to pin
beside the M.C. which he won by remaining
for three days under fire, in an observing
post which was twice blown in on him, to
register the shooting of five batteries. He is
a Duntroon boy who joined the A.I.F. as a
lieutenant. Ilis dad recently enlisted at
Hongkong.
“Meet you in Germany !’’ was the cheery
farewell of Flight Lieut. Henderson, when
leaving Oxford for the training squadron.
Ilis pal j -was another Australian airman,
Eric Fulton, of Bendigo. It came off, but
not quite as they wanted it to. Fulton, one
of his petrol tanks having been destroyed,
was forced to land behind Fritz’s lines, and
on the same day a similar fate befel
Henderson. They met within 48 hours.
“Ivawana’s” strenuous friend :
“Mokau” Jones, passed out at New Plymouth
(M.L.) the other day, won his soubriquet by a
record-smashing career of litigation over a big block
of land on the banks of the Mokau River, West Coast
of the King Country. Back in the ’sixties on the
Victorian diggings he came upon a party of Maori
gold-hunters down on their luck, and supplied them
g-ratis with hard tack and some cash to go on with.
The brown men never forgot the young Taffy* who
helped them out of a hole, and years after, when
Joshua Jones met some of them in their own coun-
try, they gave him the hand of aroha. It was in
Sydney—“Poihakene” (Port Jackson) Hori always
calls it —that Jones met Wetere te Rerenga, the boss
man of the Mokau in the ’sixties and ’seventies,
and the chief gave the pakeha the run of his district
when the Welshman arrived on a land-hunting ven-
ture. Jones pitched on the Mokau, and got leases
and right of purchase over a huge block of land on
both sides of the river. He settled at the Heads, but
soon his troubles began, for his efforts to confirm
his titles, after Wetere’s death, took him from court
to court and on to the Privy He lived in
London for years, poor as a trench rat, bogged to
the eyes in a swamp of Law. The interminable legal
boggles killed the old man’s confidence in the integ-
lity of wig and gown, but never his courage. He
was as plucky to the end as a Billjim going over
the top.
JOEY’S JOB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
lii days before the trouble Jo was rated as a slob.
He, chose to sit in hourly expectation of a job.
He’d loop hisself upon a post, for seldom friends
had he,
A gift of patient waitin’ his distinctif quality.
He d linger in a doorway, or he'd loiter on the grass,
Hdgin modestly aside to let the fleetin’ moments
pass.
Jo’d beg a bob from mother, but more often got a
clout,
And settled down with cigarettes to smoke the devil
out.
The one consistent member of the Never Trouble
Club,
He put a satin finish on the frontage of the pub.
His shoulder-blades were pokin’ out from polishin’
the pine;
But if a job ran at him Joey’s footwork was divine.
Jo strayed in at the cobbler’s door, but, scoffed at
as a fool,
He found the conversation too exhaustin’, as a rule;
Or, canted on the smithy coke, he’d hoist his feet
and yawn,
His boots slid up his shinbones, and his pants dis-
playin’ brawn;
And if the copper chanced along ’twas beautiful to
see
| Jo wear away and make hisself a faded memory.
Then came the universal nark. The Kaiser let her
rip.
They cleared the ring. The scrap was for the
whole world’s championship.
Jo Brown was takin’ notice, lurkin’ shy beneath his
hat,
And every day he crept to see the drillin’ on the flat.
He waited, watchin’ from the furze the blokes in
butcher's blue,
For the burst of inspiration that would tell him
what to do.
He couldn’t lean, he couldn’t lie. He yelled out in
the night.
Jo understood—he’d all these years been spoilin’ for
a fight!
Bight into things he flung himself. He took his kit
and gun,
Mooched gladly in the dust, or roasted gaily in the
sun.
“Gorstruth,” he said, with shining eyes, “it means
a frightful war,
’N’ now I know this is the thing that Heaven meant
me for.”
Jo went away a corporal, and fought agin the Turk,
And like a duck to water Joey cottoned to the work.
If anythin’ was doin’ it would presently come out
That Joseph Brown from Booragool was there or
thereabout.
He got a batch of medals, and a glorious renown
Attached all of a, sudden to the name of Sergeant
Brown.
Then people talked of Joey as the dearest friend
they had ;
They were chummy with his uncles, or acquainted
with Mg dad.
Jo goes to France, and presently he figures as the
best
Two-handed all-in fighter in the armies of the West,
And men of every age at home and high and low
degree,
We gather now, once went ,to school with Sergeant
Brown, V.C.
Then Hayes and Jo in Flanders met, and very proud
was Hayes
To shake a townsman by the hand, and sing the
hero’s praise.
“Oh, yes,” says Jo, “I’m doin’ well, ’n’ yet I might
do more.
If I was in a hurry, mate, to finish up this war
| I’d lay out every Fritz on earth, but, strike me,
what a yob
A man would be to work himself out of a flamin’
job!”
Now Jo’s a swell lieutenant, and he’s keepin’ up the
pace.
The Record says Lieutenant Brown’s an honor to the
place.
The town gets special mention every time he scores.
We bet
If peace don’t mess his chances up, he’ll be Field-
Marshal yet.
Dad, mother and the uncles Brown and all our
people know
That Providence began this war to find a grip for Jo!
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
So, after all, there was an imitation of the
crayfish. The contract entered into by the
Victorian Government to take coal from the
Brown mines at Newcastle differs materially
from the one into which the N. S. Wales
Ministry was discovered to be in a hurry to
rush the two States. Premier Holman has,
of course, been since talking about the readi-
ness of the Victorian Ministry to “recognise
the binding nature of the understanding pre-
viously arrived fit” and the baseness of indi-
viduals in Sydney who had been looking at
the business with a cold eye. Unfortunately
for Holman, Bowser of Vic. has been giving
reasons for not recognising the binding
nature of the understanding which Holman
and co. alleged to exist. Instead of agreeing
to take 1,000,000 tons of coal annually for
five years from the Richmond and Relaw
Main mines at 15s. f.0.b., Bowser has under-
taken to accept delivery of 200.000 tons a year
at 235. 7d. delivered in trucks at Melbourne
or 235. sd. at Geelong. And he has done this
in order to “please the N. 8. Wales Govern-
ment.” Further, a clause has been put into
the contract providing that if the coal can-
not be supplied from the Brown mines the
X. S. Wales Ministry shall supply cpal from
other mines. Which isn’t a bit like the
original deal the Government tried to work
off. What is still shrouded in gloom is why
it should “please” N. S. Wales if Victoria
agreed to take Brown coal without calling
tenders for other coal and why N. S. Wales
should derive great satisfaction from dis-
posing of coal with a proviso that such coal
shall be “free from royalty.” And the mys-
tery about the nature of any preliminary
agreement or contract with Brown in which
tiie State had become involved rerjiains as
deep as ever. There may be a simple expla-
nation of the secretiveness of the Ministry,
of the mysterious flittings about of two or
three Ministers, of frequent conferences with
Brown, of the complete abandonment of one
proposal and the adoption of another. It
ought to be made, and in the Assembly
this week members should insist upon
the lid being lifted. The policy of winning
wars by converting Ministers into scouts for
John Brown is open to the mild objection
that Brown is liable to get someone by the
nose.

Previous to 1917 this co. issued half-yearly
accounts; now there is only an annual publi-
cation, and in the foregoing table the figures
for previous terms have been grouped for
convenience of comparison. The public, if
it is paying higher fares, has gained at least
one thing during the year: by applying to
the Government for a Commission to in-
quire whether higher fares were warranted,
the co. conceded the right of the public to
have a word to say in regard to the control
of privately-owned public utilities. F. N.
Yarwood, the Commissioner whom the Gov-
ernment appointed, reported :
(а) That the need for increased revenue has been
proven;
(б) That the additions to the fares now proposed
by the co. are under all the circumstances fair and
reasonable.
The increases came into force on September
9. “and,” the directors say, “these have been
the means of placing the eo.’s affairs in a
more satisfactory position.” The Commis-
sioner—F. N. Yarwood is a front-rank public
accountant and auditor—determined the
capital in the material assets used in the
business as not less than £585,000. The
share capital at the time was £466.667.
Thus, besides the visible reserves, there were
internal reserves £115,000. Of the £466,667
no less than £146,373 consists of undivided
profit distributed as capital during the seven
years prior to the sitting of the Commission ;
and there were distributions before that.
Thus of the material assets valued at
£585,000 less than half was paid for with
money provided by the shareholders. The
balance—more than half—consists of profit
in excess of the dividend of 10 p.c. or more
made a few years ago. Looking at the whole
position it is obvious that the interests of
the shareholders have been much better
attended to than the interests of the public.
In the days when the co. was making 15 p.c.
or more on its capital, as it obviously must
have been doing, no notice was taken of the
frequent demand for an inquiry into the
business of the co., with a view to determine
whether a reduction in fares was warranted
But as soon as the co. was pinched a bit,
there was a yell, and the Government
promptly listened, to ascertain whether an
increase wasn't called for. However, as
mentioned above, the co. has now conceded
the right of the public to look into its affairs;
and that is a right which will certainly be
insisted upon when costs are reduced.
“Taking the 1013 basis,” the Commis-
sioner reported, “the increase in wages costs
between that year and 1010 is 40 p.c., and
there has also been an increase between 1010
and 1017 of 0 p.c.” The Bulletin is, how-
ever, unable to find in the report anything
at all to justify these startling figures.
Appendix A shows that wages paid in 1013
were £84,275: in 1010 £100,132, and that is
an increase of no more than 20 p.c. Mean-
time, however, the services—or, at any rate,
the revenues—had increased ; so that actu-
ally the wages in proportion to gross earn-
ings increased only from 45 p.c. to 40 p.c.—
that is, by 4 p.c. The attempt that has been
made to show, or at least to suggest, that
the increase of wages to the wretchedly-paid
employees, from captains to deckhands, was
responsible for the impoverishment of the co
is wholly without warrant ; as a matter of
fact, wliile the wages in proportion to gross
earnings increased by only 4 p.c., the amount
paid for supplies, rents, etc., increased by 7
p.c. on the same basis.
The published accounts show that, after
making provision of an unspecified amount,
for depreciation, the net profit for the past
year was £28,(588. To this, however, must
be added £10(52 which has been put to an
insurance fund. Considering that the largely
increased fares came into operation only in
September and that the coal strike not only
added to costs but deprived the co. of the
new Zoo traffic (the Zoo being closed for a
couple of months), the figures are quite
satisfactory from the shareholders’ point of
view. The directors are apparently satis-
fied, too. because, whereas they paid only
24 p.c. for the first half, they put the rate
up to 34 p.c. for the second half, making
(5 p.c. for the year. The Commissioner, it
is true, considered that “at the present time
....a minimum of 8 p.c. to shareholders is
a fair and reasonable expectancy to share-
holders in this venture.” On this point,
however, the Commissioner’s opinion can be
worth no more than that of any other intel-
ligent person. And it is quite clear that a
very large number of people don’t agree w r ith
him, because at the present moment, while
only 0 p.c. div. is being paid, the shares are
above par in the open market—the 10s.
shares were sold at 11s. 6d. on the day the
present report was issued.
A couple of months back the co. took over
the Balmain New Ferry Co., paying 50,000
£1 shares for the undertaking: hence the
lew' hundred pounds additional required to
pay the past year’s dividend. The purchase
gives the co. not only some boats but also a
considerable estate at Middle Harbor. Landed
property is now valued in the books at
£168,222, as against £133,871 a year ago.
ship property at £323,283, as against
£301.800 (there have been sales as well as
purchases) and wharves and waiting rooms
at £40,141, as against £35,603. The move-
ment in the past four years has been this
way:—

Meantime there has been no issue of capital

South Brisbane Gas Co. —For three
years past applications for the accounts of
this co. have met with no response. It was
a fact, certainly, that previous accounts had
given The Bulletin no pleasure and little
satisfaction; and finally on Feb. 12, 1914.
shareholders were urged to find out whether
they were not living in a fool’s paradise and
to insist upon a competent accountant being
brought in from outside to overhaul the co.’s
affairs. Apparently, however, the share-
holders were content to live in the fool’s
paradise; and it was not until an investiga-
tion had to be made under the Queensland
Gas Act 1916 that the rotten condition of
things was exposed. Actually, it seems, at
the end of 1914 the directors had made what
purported to be a revaluation of assets and
thereupon issued to shareholders £lOO.OOO
worth of debenture stock free of cost. The
result of this revaluation of assets and issue
of bonus stock is seen in the latest balance-
sheet, which claims assets £318,588. For the
purpose of determining the price to be charged
for gas it was necessary for the Gas Referee
to value the assets. This was done upon evi-
dence, and the best the co.’s own expert could
do for it was £1<35,020, although he con-
sidered that something should be added for
expenditure not represented by tangible
assets. On evidence the Referee fixed “the
present value of the amount invested in the
undertaking” at £160,051.
Either the co.’s own expert and the Gas
Referee are thus hopelessly wrong, or the
issue to shareholders of the £lOO.OOO of de-
benture stock, as a bonus, was not only un-
justified but highly improper. Now. what
could have been the object of the transac-
tion V Early in 1014, it may be recalled, the
N. S. Wales Parliament got to work on a
Gas Bill; and there was good reason to sup-
pose that similar legislation would follow in
Queensland. Is it a reasonable thing to
assume that when the assets of the co. were
valued at a preposterous figure, and when
bonus stock was issued to shareholders, the
directors did not have this probable legisla-
tion in their minds? In that connection,
what is the meaning of this from the report
of the Gas Referee:—
On November 14 Mr. James Hurll, managing
director of the Ann of James Hurll and Co.. Ltd.,
called on me and informed me that Mr. Arthur
A. Tourney, one of the directors of his co., had
been notified by the South Brisbane Gas Co. that
he would be required to give evidence at the hear-
ing of the Reference supporting , the valuation he
made in November, 1914, and that as such valua-
tion had been made by him simple to agree with
an amount which had been furnished to him and
on the understanding that it was not in anv way
lor public use, and that as lie (Tourney) was quite
unable to justify the valuation, he "desired that
Mr. tourneys evidence be dispensed with.
Apparently Tourney’s valuation wasn't a
valuation at all. What happened, possibly
ami judging by Hurll’s remark, was that the
engineer was given a total and asked in
etiect; “If the assets arc in the aggregate
worth so much, what are the items worth?”
The questions are: Did the directors know
that the so-called valuation was not a genu-
ine oneV D.d they procure or assist in mak-
Hig a bogus valuation? If thev did. with
what object did they do it? Did they know
that the assets were not worth what they
were valued at when the bonus stock was
issued? If so, why was the bonus stock
issued? On the answers will depend whether
the directors or some of them should be pro-
secuted for conspiracy. The case certainly
demands .a searching inquiry.

The C. B. of A. made a colossal splash dur-
ing Melbourne’s big boom of the ’eighties and
early ’nineties; and the smash was in the
same class. Most of the old share capital
was then written off as lost: all that remains
of it (and _this and more was supplied by
colls) is £95,659 in 191,818 ordinary shares.
The depositors were, however, invited to take
4 p.c. pref. shares, and, though they knew
they were only likely to get 8 p.c. for many
years, they took £2.117,850 of this capital in
the bank. Unfortunately there had not been
hard enough writing down, and term by term
ever since a large part of the profit has gone
to reduce the estimated further deficiency.
The nursing and marketing of the old
flock of lame ducks was given over to an
Assets Trust, which, though nominally sepa-
rate. was for all practical purposes part of
the bank. Combining the figures of the two
we get these results :

Until two or three years ago there was among
the assets a gradually diminishing amount
representing “estimated value of the assets
of the old bank.” Then that item disap-
peared. and it looked as though the last of
the assets of the old bank had been realised ;
nevertheless this half-year the directors say:
“Recoveries in connection with old depen-
dencies have increased the actual capital to
the extent of £10.000.” It would be amazing
if the recovery was an exact £10,000; it is
much more probable that astute general-
manager Heron protected his valuation and
his subsequent realisations by a contingency
account, into which lie now dips for a round
£lO.OOO. In the past seven years the capital
deficiency has been made good to the extent
of £.‘>Bo.ooo, and £OBO,OOO is still to he pro-
vided, less whatever inner reserve may exist;
and on past performances that looks like a
12 years’ job. True, the more profit that is
accumulated the bigger the earnings should
he: another £300.000, for instance, should
easily earn £17,500 a year. And that should
reduce the period required for shifting the
dead horse. But it has to be noted that
for four years past the pref. capital
has been getting its maximum 4 p.e.
instead of the 3 p.c. with which it was
starved for many years while the deficit was
being made good (for the benefit mainly of
the ordinary capital) ; and, moreover, it is
highly probable that, as soon as the world’s
pulse is normal again, a proposal will be
revived to give the ordinaries a taste.
The Commercial of Australia has a well-
marked policy of its own ; it is a pioneering
bank, with no less than 272 branches and
agencies, or 40 more than it had four years
ago. And it will get 12 or 15 more in a
lump by absorbing the little National of
Tasmania. This, no doubt, means heavy
overhead charges—for salaries, etc. But a
bank of this character performs a big public
service. Also it has excellent chancds of
building up a very big business with the
development of the country. There are
many disappointments, no doubt; but now
and again there are surprises like the Malice,
which must have yielded a fine addition to
the C. B. of A.’s revenue. From the creditor’s
point of xuew there is one big thing to be
said for the cocky : lie nearly always pays.
The city man. if misfortune hits him and he
can’t pay within a few months, very often
thinks it, a fair thing not to pay at all. Thg
cocky, if he can’t pay this year, hardly ever
thinks that he shouldn’t pay next year; and
if he can’t do it then the chances are he will
turn up the following year with a smile and
the money and an invitation to his creditor
to come and have a drink on the strength of
it.
So the hank’s figures go on growing:—

Liabilities now total £5,(557.351; and liquid
assets represent nearly 48 p.c. of that
amount. Deducting liquid assets from the
liabilities, there is £4,540.762 of debt not
thus represented ; and against that there is
£5,703,834 of advances (after providing for
doubtful accounts) and premises written
down to £456,060. The position seems to
be thoroughly sound ; and it is steadily im-
proved, of course, by the excellent propor-
tion of profits put by. Some day, consider-
ing the huge proportion of capital which
carries' a maximum of 4 p.c., and in view of
the prospects of the business, the ordinaries
will get something worth having.

l'he reserves of £668,867 are made up in this
way:—•
Premiums on shares .. £558,330
General reserves . . . . 34,230
Meter renewal fund . . 9,991
Unappropriated profit . . 66,316
But in addition to those visible reserves
there is a large internal reserve consisting
of works paid for out of revenue. During
the past seven years £398,342 of profit has
been spent in extension and improvement of
plant. Only a small portion of this money
has been for replacements; most of it has
been for works that could fairly be charged
to capital account. One consequence has
been a very large increase in the, profit shown
in proportion to the total outlay charged to
capital:—
1907. 1917.
Total outlay charged to
capital .. .. •• £2,171,745 £2,472,540
Half-year’s revenue .. 224,428 472,228
Revenue p.c. of capital outlay 10.3 19.1
'thus, though only £300,795 of new works
constructed during the past 10 years have
been paid for by dipping into the share-
holders’ pockets or pledging their credit, the
half-year’s revenue has increased by £247,800.
In addition to providing shareholders with
their dividend, the co.’s customers are
being requireu to supply it with new revenue-
producing property to the extent of over
£50,000 a year. It seems a bit more than a
fair thing.
The past half-year’s profit has. however,
been a good deal less than usual for a July-
December term. The coal strike “compelled
us to purchase from other sources supplies
of coal at greatly enhanced prices.” Appar-
ently coal cost the co. about £35,000 more
than the quantity required to produce the
same quantity of gas would have done in the
corresponding half of 1916. Cost of coal
has, however, been steadily increasing for
many years
1900. 1907. 1916. 1917.

revenue . . . . 22 p.c. 27 p.c. 30 p.c. 37 p.c.
A still bigger advance would be shown if the
cost were taken in proportion to gas sales
only: a remarkable increase in the revenue
from by-products has helped the figures very
materially. In the latter half of 1907 these
by-products realised £42,818; in the past
half-year £105,294. In the next little com-
parison the total gross revenue is divided
among the contributors .

The by-products now may be said to provide
the profit. In fact, last half-year’s gas revenue
was £20,000 less than total costs, including
interest on borrowed money. But it is
doubtful whether by-products will show any-
thing like such figures after the war.

trace, not only the increased earnings, but

While revenue has thus increased in 10
years by 310 p.c., outgoings are 162 p.c.
higher. These outgoings are exclusive of
interest on capital and a few odds and ends
—bad debts, etc.
Still, when all this is said, there has been
a profit on the new business; and even last
half-year, after paying out over £85,000 for
repairs, renewals and replacements, and pro-
viding interest on debentures and other bor-
rowed money, the profit was 11J p.c. p.a. on
the share capital, including premium capital
paid on shares. If the profit (at £375,000 a
year) were capitalised at 7 p.c. it would
amount to £2.500.000, equal to a shade over
£l3 per share.
