“Wart’s” so-long to an old money-lender:
The National Bank of Tasmania, which is to be
absorbed by the Commercial of Australia, was
originally the Bank of Tasmania, and was estab-
lished in 1852. During the ’seventies and ’eighties
ic had heavy losses. In 1884 the manager of the
Beaconsfield branch was stuck up by armed men,
his keys were taken and about £3OOO w r as stolen.
Some suspected persons were put on their tiial
but discharged. They afterwards brought an action
against the bank and were awarded damages. One
of them was, later, convicted of circuiting the
stolen notes; another was convicted of perjury and
turned informer, incriminating those who had pre-
viously been on trial. Two of them were again
tried, but without a conviction. In 1885, after
receiving a report on the position of the bank from
a Melbourne accountant, George Horne, the man-
agement decided to reorganise. A new co. was
formed and called the National Bank of Tasmania,
Ltd., and George Horne was appointed manager.
Since then things have gone pretty smoothly, and
the shareholders have got in dividends twice the
amount of their capital. It is interesting, by the
way, to recall that both the Union Bank of Aus-
tralia and the Bank of Australasia had their genesis
in Launceston.
“Blue Geebung” : The co. which supplies
the city of Sydney with gas gives the whole
of its coal contract to one firm (J. and A.
Brown), with the result that it is unable to
accumulate a reserve stock of coal. It had
during the late coal strike to cease the
supply of gas to consumers several, weeks
earlier than the co. which has to get its coal
carried 000 miles further to supply Mel-
bourne with iiluminant; and which, dividing
its contract between two firms (Hetton and
Caledonian Collieries), got a more regular
supply and was enabled to accumulate a
a rv necessary reserve of raw product.
What position will Sydney be in when the
coal-miner next refuses to go below, since it
will be getting only the remnants of coal the
Brown firm can spare after satisfying ttie
large wants of the Victorian Government ?
* * * *
Melbourne-owned Adelong Creek (M.S.W.)
wound up its December half-year with a
working profit of £5672. This was a record
for the co., and was due to No. 1 dredge
sluicing 2110 oz. gold out of 338,900 cubic
yards of ground. Costs were low at 2.93 d.
per cubic yard, and a profit of £4OlB was
earned. No. 2 plant, on almost as good
ground, put through 260,600 cubic yards,
but with costs at 4.20 d. the ' -orking profit
was only £1655. Enough cash was earned
to pay a couple of Is. divs. and bin £lOOO
of war bonds. Yields for this month have
not been so good, but they will improve
again as the No. 1 dredge gets into the bet-
ter ground ahead.
Amalgamated Hill End (N.SW.) is stak-
ing almost everything on the deepening of
the Star of Peace shaft another 150 ft. or
so. In the early ’7o’s this shaft contributed
largely to the 17.1430 z. of gold got from the
mine in four years; but the bottom has not
been explored for over 40 years. Substantial
Government aid is said to have been granted
to help the co # test the deep levels. Mean-
while. the Reward, to the north, is running
the 14-head stamp one shift and keeping the
drills going on*the stone below. The Central,
between the Reward and the Amalgamated,
continues the drive that will unwater the
Reward to 900 ft. Several attempts Lave
been made on the field lately to locate some
of the rich stone that exists in tradition, but
nothing favorable has been reported.
Mount Boppy, Cobar (N.S.W.), intends to
resume production; the works were hung up
early last year for want of development. In
plain English, for want of void in the stone
available. In recent times the output has
been from 70,000 to 80,000 tons a year, but
values w'ere lately under 10s. per ton, though
the treatment was the most elaborate in
the State. Lateral development has time
after time proved ore bodies parallel to the
main lode, and this has induced the directors
to resume wmrk. In normal times costs
amounted to 225. Od. per ton of ore.
Great Britain sluicing (N.S.W.) is to
hand out another Is. on its modest little
10.000 shares. This makes 63 distributions
or more than six times the capital.
Justice Neville, of the English Chancery
Court, has sanctioned the payment of £1
per share (£68,659) by the old E. and A.
Copper Co., which ceased smelting at Wara-
t.ah f N.S.W.) last year. The authorised
capital is £105,000, in 70,000 shares of 30s.
each, and 68,659 shares are issued. The
S.A. Government took over the wharf owned
by the co. at Port Adelaide, and that was a
lift. Most shareholders will consider them-
selves lucky to get £1 per share by the
liquidation of a business that in recent years
lias paid only Is. per share p.a.
Boyd, general manager of Mt. Morgan,
has been reinforced by the appointment of
Horsburgh as assistant g.-m. This ought to
turn out good business. Boyd is weakest on
the metallurgical side, and Horsburgh is
strongest there. After taking a course at
Sydney Uni., where he impressed the men
he was working with, Horsburgh went north,
and was metallurgist at Molloy in its divi-
dend days. From there he went to Chillagoe
as metallurgist, and lately has been manager.
Queensland Mines Department has made
a start at opening up the arsenic deposits
outside Stanthorpe. The Government has
reserved about 150 acres, and contemplates
erecting a large treatment plant if results
justify it. Sample ore has given 30 p.e.
arsenic. The eradication of prickly-pear
with cost-price acid is one of the ulterior
objects of the enterprise.
Did Edna May Deeps last half-year crush
only 3760 tons from the 566 ft. level, or was
the yield of 545G0z. reported by Bob Stuckey
inclusive of gold got from 480 tons which he
says was drawn from the 4<oft. level? That
dirt is stuff, so he might explain whether
it went to the battery. On November 6 the
co.’s cash position glittered with bank credits
of £11,400, but that included £6OOO from the
sale of reserve shares and from calls. Things
would have been even better if costs hadn't
soared. Wages, stores, fuel, timber, repairs
and water amounted to over 675. per ton,
and, if the expenditure on plant is added, the
whole of the mine charges aggregated
£16,653. That sum divided by the tonnage
reported gives 89s. per ton as costs, apart
from head-office expenses. Stuckey estimates
ore reserves as follows:

There are also 5060 tons valued at 205.,
bringing the total up to £104.040. Stuckey
plans to leave as much of this ore as he can
while he shoves the winzes down 250 ft.
These must act as blind shafts, and will
furnish pay dirt while finding out the
behavior of the reef underfoot. If the shoot
pitches north, he can convert the present
shaft into an underlie ; it it makes more to
the south, as he indicates, he may keep
the shaft vertical. Incidentally, he will also
get evidence on the water problem ; but his
penalty for working like this must be to
keep up costs. And while costs are at or
above the £3 mark profits will be about
£2OOO per month, winch won’t give enough
to maintain the present 6d. div. However.
Stuekev reckons (1) that he has locked out
the Edna May water ; (2) that even if some
gets in. his plant will keep it under: and
' that with cash in hand, and returns
from workin"' in ore and reduction in costs,
he can pay at least the 6d. divs. His gamble
is to be in the western ground, where he is
driving and boring.
* * *
The Olonbinane Gold and Antimony Co.,
near Kilmore (Vic.), one of the wild cats
brought to life again by the war-demand for
antimony, snuffed out recently after a few
months’ work. The other day the £5OOO of
plant, which is under 12 months old, was
put up for sale by auction, but £4OO was
the best offer. The whole outfit is said to
have been sold privately since for £5OO, so
the mostl.v-Melbonfne shareholders have
dropped £4500 on the machinery alone.
Failure was due to excessive cost of ore
treatment, owing to technical difficulties.
The mine was worked years ago for gold,
but the cheap antimony refused to be sepa-
rated from the gold. The new co. was after
the antimony, the gold being considered
merely as a side-line.

The Sutherland regime at Tasman and
Crown I .veil is over. The co. is now in W.
B. Arnold’s office, and a call or two will be
made to do some more finicking with the
ore and to link un the new power. Then if
the war keeps metals up. profits are to come.
Thus talk the optimists. The bears lick
their chaps in anticipation.
“Karaji”: What’s the use of having great
natural resources, anyhow? One of the
great wants of Westralia is coal. Collie
coal-measures are of the same age as the
Clarence River CN.S.W.) beds, and cannot
be regarded as better than second-class: but
in the Irwin River district, not far from
navigable water, the permo-carboniferous
formation is largely developed, and in those
beds are found the glacial deposits which are
associated with the rich coal seams of the
South Maitland field, also of permo-carbon-
iferous age. Yet no attempt has been made
to prospect those deposits, although a tithe
of the money lost last year in the States
agricultural implement factory would suffice.
The Consols, owned by Mount Elliott,
Clone urry, is on fire «it the deepest lev el
(480 ft.). This mine adjoins the, Hampden,
where such encouraging developments have
occurred recently at the 500 ft. and 600 ft.
levels. Bursts of carbonic-acid gas have
been frequent in the Consols, indicating in-
tense chemical action. Elliott had arranged
to commence another smelting campaign, so
the outbreak falls very awkwardly. _ And
these mine fires are the very mischief to
handle. #

BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXIONS
WITHOUT ROUGE.
Ladies who dislike rouge and “make-up”
—and what lady of refinement does not?—
often ask what they can do to restore the
natural velvety bloom of childhood to faded,
sallow, wrinkled cheeks. All such ladies, as
well as those who now have good com-
plexions and wish to preserve them, can
realise their hearts’ desire by getting their
chemist to prepare a lotion composed of 2oz.
rose water, 1 dram tincture of benzoin and
2oz. flowers of oxzoin. Before using shake
thoroughly, and then apply with a soft cloth
or sponge. When dry brush lightly with a
soft cloth or piece of chamois leather. If
you will do this whenever you go out you
will be astonished at the absolutely natural
and refined improvement in your appearance.
This lotion is also exceptionally beneficial in
relieving and preventing chapped faces and
hands as well as redness due to exposure to
weather.— Advt.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, January 28, 1918.
My Dear .Akim, —
Anniversary Day brought the usual fea-
tures, served up on ice, or with merely tepid
ginger-pop, as circumstances dictated. Judge
Backhouse stepped out of the regatta com-
mittee’s best chair this year—as a protest
against sport in war-time. But most of the
sports at the Harbor party were well over
fighting age, and, wuii A. G. Milson as act-
ing-president, told mild tales of tubs of long
ago. Q. L. Deloitte, an amateur mariner of
great form in the ’sixties, discursed on bygone
battles of the sculls. The racing events of
Saturday were mere pale ghosts of the gam-
bols of pre-war days, the committee’s object
being merely to keep this oldest regatta in
the world alive.
Under the shade of an uncertain umbrella
R. J. Black, M.L.C., read several pages of
ancient Australian story. The spot was
close by the soft splash of the fountain which
has the bold figure of the indomitable
Phillip, in bronze, as presiding deity. From
that green terrace on the summit of the
Palace Garden lawns the skipper of the his-
toric First Fleet keeps eternal vigil, with
the thin hook-nose turned towards the
Pacific. As president of the Pioneer Club,
Black, M.L.C., held the sward for some time.
Then came the breaking of the Jack on an
adjacent pole, after which the company
trotted off to Hunter-street, where the club
sandwich lay in waiting.
Pipes skirled around the statue of Bobbie
Burns on Saturday afternoon. Prof. Andy
Stuart, after composing his features for the
camera, broke out into interesting details of
the poet-ploughman’s life. Under his arm he
carried two ancient calf-bound tomes, and
awe-stricken silence followed his dramatic
opening of both volumes. The Scawtcli chief
of the Sydney medical school was a lad in
Dumfries years ago. Near that old town
in 1791 Burns wrote immortal verse and
farmed his land; and in Dumfries young
Andy saw tiie funeral of the poet’s last two
sons, Colonel Nicol and Colonel James
Burns. In the old books, given to the Pro-
fessor in the long ago, is the original “Auld
Lang Syne”—it was afterwards altered and
added to by Burns —also the Jacobite li>t,
‘ ocots Wha Hae.” So the crowd that gath-
ered round the statue got more than it ex-
pected, and one ancient Caledonian went off
in vocal fireworks that sounded like “Hech !
Hoot! Hey ! Hi—h-s-s-s-h !”
The Davidsons bring two little girls,
Diana and Daphne, aged seven and eight,
to old Government House in Macquarie-
street. Possibly a wounded Coldstream or
two will come on sick leave as part of the
staff. Lady Davidson’s brother, Colonel
Feilding, is in command of that gilt-edged
regiment; hence the chance of its sending a
hurt-in-war sample or two of manly beauty
to cheer the local belle. But this is all
guessing. The new Governor maintains a
masterly reticence as to who or what comes
in his train from Newfoundland.
St. Vincent’s Hospital Nightingales wore
the heroines of a Roll-of-Honor gathering the
other moonlit night. The party began with
euchre and bell-ringing—the latter by a
model of industry who brought belated
players to the tables. A huge crowd —
tickets at ss. each represented the spoils
which go to the Nurses’ Home building
fund —sauntered into the garden space. Here
the four walls of the Home, built in Spanish
quadrangle style, shut out the dust that is
Darlinghurst. Entirely surrounded by re-
freshments, visitors listened to the remarks
of Surgeon Howse, not so long back from
the Front—it was he who pulled aside the
white-and-blue drapery to show the hand-
some honor board. On it are the names of
St. Vincent’s nurses who have tended the
wounded wherever the Allies’ soldiers march
into battle. Returned healers, in red capes
and grey gowns, sang “God Save” so softly
that it sounded like a gentle lullaby to those
who had fallen to sleep under alien skies.
Harley McLaughlin was master of cere-
monies.
Justice Chubb has been spending the long
vacation in this State. With a married
daughter (Mrs. North) and her pretty chick
he’s been doing Jenolan Caves—which take
seme doing—and seeing the cream and roses
of the South Coast.
A very quiet wedding took place at St.
John’s, Darlinghurst, when Olive Cunning-
hame, of Bayswater-road, said she would to
Montague Chubb. He’s a son of the Queens-
land Judge. The bride was in foamy white,
with a large white hat over her piquant face.
With her hand tucked in the arm of Mr.
Harry Abbott, who handed her over to her
new husband, she looked like a little school-
miss beside the big guardian. Dorothy Per-
rins, sole bridesmaiden, was in ivory and
azure finery. Leonard Stephen was second-
best man. Justice Chubb was in the party;
so was the youthful-looking mother of the
bride. The toasts were drunk at the Aus-
tralia.
Ilendy-Pooley, of a well-known N. S.
Wales family, heard the bugles call. He
was then in a comfortable job in the Regis-
trar-General’s office, and there were seven
good reasons—a wife and six youngsters—
to keep him there. But he put them aside ; and
“Killed in action” is his brief epitaph. But
his mates won’t let it rest at that. They’re
formed into a Building Fund Committee to
help the little things this citizen-soldier left
behind him; and the State War Council
approves of th,eir concert in St. James’s Hall
this Friday evening. The programme is so
good that you’ll have five shillings’ worth for
the 2s. asked for a reserved seat.
Mrs. Lewis, a war widow who used to be
Gertrude Tait, daughter of the old Arch-
deacon of Parkes, has gone north again,
taking her baby with her. She was matron
of Pt. Darwin Hospital when she married
Captain Lewis, a permanent military man
there. He was killed; and so miserable is
the Australian military pension that the
widow bows her head and takes her precious
infant back with her to her old job.
Captain Archie Collins, one of the latest
batch of Australians to win the Military
Cross, took his medical degree with first-
class honors at Sydney Uni. a couple of
years ago, and was acting-superintendent at
Prince Alfred Hospital for a while before
enlisting. His work in pathology and micro-
biology brought him tbe first travelling
fellowship under the Walter and Eliza Hal!
foundation for research abroad ; but he did
his travelling in a troopship instead, and
left scientific research to wait till a press-
ing job in Flanders is completed. A brother,
who also wears three stars, was wounded
at Passchendale, and is being patched
up in an English hospital. Their father,
now deceased, was headmaster of the State
school at Manly.
You will remember painter Gordon Coutts,
one-time instructor at Sydney Royal Art
Society and represented in the Gallery by
a portrait of The Bulletin’s old friend,
George Reid, and other canvases. One of
his promising Sydney students was Miss
Alice Hobbs, whom he afterwards married
in ’Frisco. They were in Paris when the
war started, but hurriedly packed up
and returned to the U.S.A. After
living in Piedmont for two years,
Coutts returned to Paris, but his wife
refused to risk the U-boats. Then the artist
decided to do his bit, and was with the Bed
Cross in France for some time. Becoming
ill, he went to Tangiers to recuperate, and
was glorying in that country’s color when
he was astounded to have divorce papers
served on him. He flung his brushes and
palette aside, and hastened to Beno, where
Mrs. Coutts had become domiciled, to con-
test the suit. The lady alleged that her
husband’s artistic temperament was too
much for her and constituted cruelty.
Coutts tried to effect a reconciliation, but
failed, and finally filed an answer, mention-
ing an ex-Maoriland lawyer (who had
already secured a Beno divorce) as co-re.
Coutts’s solicitor, however, advised him to
drop the cross-suit. “You will get the worst
of it,” he said; “a woman has only to shed
a few tears on the stand in a Reno divorce
court, and the best case in the world is
lost.” So the case was uncontested, and
Beno decided to free the lady from the
cruelty of an artistic temperament. “When
I was 21,” writes Coutts bitterly, “I thought
I knew women; now I know I don’t know
them and never did.” Here is the lady,
from a charming portrait by her husband
hung on the line of the Boyal Academy last
year.
The Bev. Mother of the Sacred Heart
Convent, Sydney, who died last week, was
sister of the once well-known Monsignor
Weld, of London, and niece of Frederick
Aloysius Weld, once Governor of Maoriland,
Tasmania and Westralia. The Welds are
closely related to the Vaughans, most emi-
nent of English B.C. families. The deceased
nun had worked many years in Australia,
chiefly in Sydney.
Sydney violinist Donald Mcßeath sends a
line. His training—he’s in the Canadian
Flying Corps—took longer than he expected,
and his wings were slow in growing. But
“nearly fledged now,” he says, “and leav-
ing soon for France.... The American girl
I leave behind me is Eileen Miller.”
Governors come and Governors go, but the
Official Secretary, like the perpetual curate
in Trollope’s tales, goes on for ever. The
individual is gathered to his fathers. The
office remains. The name of Budge has
been associated with the N. S. Wales Pub-
lic Service for the last 00 years. Budge
pare came from England while we were still
a Crown colony, cold-shouldered by the
Titus Barnacles
o f Downing-street.
After many years in
the service, he was
appointed Official
Secretary and Clerk
of the Executive
Council. ’Way back
in earlier years
Edward Merewether,
Charles Cowper and
othe r well-known
men held the billet.
Possibly the oldest
in the Public Ser-
vice, it is the nearest
approach there is to
a link between the
Crown, as repre-
sented by a Cocked
Hat, and the Man
in the Street. When Sir Harry Baw-
son, inexperienced in gubernatorial ways,
arrived, the Official Secretaryship had
become a shadowy thing. The plain-
spoken old Admiral asked for a new
man—the best for the special task of keep-
ing a strange Governor off the shoals.
Critchett Walker, then Principal Under-
Sec., named H. C. Budge, son of that
former able official who reigned long in the
billet, and he has cultivated a charming
manner and a silent tongue in the office ever
since. He grew a few lines in his face
while he sat at the elbow of the ever-restless
up-at-dawn Governor Strickland. Private
Secretaries came and went. The confiden-
tial Australian nib plodded on. In spite
of many difficulties —for the Chief was hard
to please—relations were never strained.
The Strickland family left Australia with
regret; and they continue a friendly and
frequent correspondence with their old Offi-
cial Secretary, whose loyalty, courtesy and
tact appealed to them all, as it has to so
many of us who have come in occasional
contact with him.
Popular Lily Lyon, daughter of the late
John Lamb Lyon, of Long Nose Point, was
wed in Brisbane to engineer William Chap-
man, a son of that skipper whose forefathers
have gone down to the sea in ships for gener-
ations. Daddy Lyon, of Lyon and Cottier,
was one of this State’s leading stained-glass
decorators. A year or so ago, at the age of
80-something, the old gentleman retired from
the business, leaving his two sons and daugh-
ter Lily to take joint charge. The “firm”
didn’t like to part with her, but her husband
and the butcher and the vacuum-cleaner
demand her time.
Leon Gellert, tall, fair-haired and the
owner of a sensitive face, has been over
from Adelaide, holidaying with Norman
Lindsay. You can find his poetic fancy
floating free in Norman’s post-referendum
drawing, “Only the Ghosts of Yes” —shamed
phantoms marching to the trenches, instead
of a cohort of flesh-and-blood reinforce-
ments. Leon wears the returned soldier’s
badge, and is, as you know, the aesthetic
warrior who wrote the exquisitely melodious
“Songs of a Campaign.” He spent an even-
ing—a genuine poet’s hour —in Premier W.
A. Holman’s home on Bellevue Hill. A flat
roof that overlooked a moonlit harbor and
the honeyed voice of W.A. reading Gellert’s
verse to the little party of three literati gave
one of them (so he says) a Persian-garden
feeling down his spinal marrow.
The new Voluntary Workers’ Cafd is
comfortably housed in McNaught’s base-
ment, Pitt-street, thanks to the kindness of
the lessee of the premises. That’s where
the A.B.C. used to be, you remember?
There was a very solemn gathering at
the Mitchell Library the other afternoon.
Never a joke could come anigh as Deputy-
Governor Cullen handed over the stamp
collection, valued at £15,000, presented by
11. L. White, of Belltrees. Philatelists
spoke in soft whispers, and advanced with
hushed steps towards the Shrine. A hand-
some affair made of Australian wood, with
layers of tiny drawers, houses the treasures
under glass. Carefully penned, like prize
sheep, the distinguished guests have had
their pedigrees drawn out by philatelist
Fred Hagen. On behalf of the trustees
Arthur Dowling rambled amiably round
stamps in general and the H. L. White
gift in particular. A. C. Ebsworth spoke
up on behalf of the owner, who was too shy
to receive in person the welter of oratory in
his honor. C. F. Williams, who presides
over meetings of the Sydney Philatelic Club,
talked waterlines, laureates and diadems;
and Cullen, C.J., broke into such passionate
patriotism—skirting carefully round Aus-
tralia’s status after the war —that a volley
of “Hear, hears” shattered the solemn
silence of the good philatelists.
Beg Wykeham (Hugh Ward’s and Grace
I’alotta’s r,ght-hand man in the days before
Hugh had joined J.C.W.) writes from
France (20/13 /’l7) to say cheer-oh :
I’m still alive, and although we’ve had a pretty
tough time lately I’ve managed to dodge any serious
hurt. A high explosive shell lifted me up the other
day and gently but firmly dropped me on the back
of my neck, but, bar a shaking up, I was none the
worse for it the next day. I’m hoping to get some
English leave in January—l've had 14 months in
France without a break, and am suffering a bit
from “waritis.” With luck, this time next year
should see us all home again.
Died suddenly, at Brisbane, Stephen
Itussell, iiged 03, well known some 15 years
ago in Sydney commercial circles, and an
occasional contributor to The Bulletin.
The immediate cause of death was shock
on receiving news of the death of his only
surviving son, on active service. Mrs. M.
Lalor, a prominent N. S. Wales Labor sup-
porter, is a sister.
“Bosna” still regards the war as a matri-
monial bureau :
Poor old Hymen has been heavily engaged with
Anglo-Australia as usual. lie has had jobs from the
Charles Maclcinnons’ son, of Derribong (N.S.W.),
a sub. in a Reserve Household Battalion, and Alva
Gertrude, daughter of Lt.-Col. Miller, of Alberstone,
Hants; from Major Ilore, of Albury, and Sybil

Mrs. John Fell is gathering her friends —
they are mostly tired city girls—round her
in the South Coast bungalow with the
unique name. “No llurri” broods over the
sea on the edge of a little headland. The
wild waves’ chatter is so intimate that one
visitor with an imagination expected them to
walk in as soon as the lights went out.
David Stead, F.L.S., president of the Wild
Life Preservation Society, pleads for a
keener public interest in saving the feath-
ered loveliness of the bush. The IV' >-1.7
report, just out, comments on the pass ? _ .of
the birds o’ paradise and osprey plume as
fashionable gewgaws in the Best Hat. But
lovely woman gets no credit for angelic
desires to save the white heron from extinc-
tion : it is the war that is credited witli
putting the osprey and ofher showy plumes
away in a handbox. However, the infant
population is being duly sworn in as mem-
bers of the Don’t-Kill-Cockrobin Society, and
50,000 kiddies have taken the pledge not to
burgle the bush songster’s nest.
April 26 is to be the grand climax of a
month spent in scratching for funds for our
Bed Cross. It hasn’t been gone into yet.
but possibly the military camps will get up
sports on April 20 to help. They will if
they’re asked.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

Melbourne, January 28, 1918.
My Dear Yaurie, —
The State Excellency motored to Bol-
warrah on Thursday morning for a day's
fly-fishing in the Moorabool reservoir; but
owing to the heat fishing was voted too
strenuous until evening, when several trout
were landed, including a beautiful four-
pounder—aide Conant is prepared to swear
an affidavit as to the weight, or, if neces-
sary, go so far as to make a wager. On
Friday morning his Ex. and Conant were up
at the milkman’s hour and managed to land
a few fish before dashing back to Melbourne
to resume toil at the old address.
The story goes the rounds that the Ranee
of Pudukota, being white and not Indian-
born, connot keep the wonderful State
jewels that hubby showered on his bride.
They’ve got to be gathered up and held over
for some princess of the blood. This, if
trhe, will give you some idea of how mixed
marriages of this kind are regarded in India,
not only among the whites, but among the
upper-crust natives.
That social dynamo, Mrs. Charles Fair-
bairn seems to have tired of trunks itnd
travel, for hubby has bought her the spin-
ster Payne’s South Yarra house. The Fair-
bairn lady is just back from her sixth trip
to England since the war began. Her last
errand was (1) to see daughter Esther
married to her Argyll Highlander, Major
Wheatley, D. 5.0., and settle the bride in her
Khightbridge flat; (2) to inquire after son
Jim of the R.F.C., wounded and in the hands
of the Boches • and (3) to tend to flying-son
Osborne, also wounded after a gallant piece
of aircraft work. Now she will give some
time to her girleen Betty, who will equal if
not out-distance sister Esther in good looks.
Poor old Bigjolin Forrest has to endure
some more head surgery. He is in a private
hospital again while Mrs. John is within
cooee at the Grand.
You’ll remember pretty Nellie Porter, who
used to live out St. Kilda way and look like
a Madonna in modern clothes. She is now
Mrs. Norman Parbury—and she and her
husband have been quietly growing apples
and kiddies near Hobart. They have gath-
ered their crop of pippins, and are putting
the little Parburys to school. That done,
Norman goes off to worry the War Office
for a job.
There is a strange warrior bird on the
Block at present. He wears a blue-grey
frieze coat with a flying-corps wing on his
arm; but atop of Seawtch red hair and
freckles is a Glengarry cap marked with
brass figures and hieroglyphics. No, not
kilts—just trousers.
The elder of the Como Armytage spin-
sters hopes to reinforce her banking account
with the squab-pigeon industry. The family
acres at Beaconsfield, which she acquired
after they had been auctioned by the Army-
tage trustees, are now white with birds at
feeding-ume.
President Mrs. Hughes, of the N.W.L.,
has just got back from Bananaland, where
her elder hope was ordained. Odd that he
didn’t get his collar fastened front to back
in his native city.
The Henry Cornish pair, lately at Tahiti,
stayed with Sir Robert Walker and his wife
on a tiny island with an unpronounceable
name. Administrator Walker was concerned
in the exciting capture of the German
raider’s crew and the armed launch. His
wife, a young and alarmingly beautiful Eng-
lish girl, was Thursby Bellamy, a niece of
Admiral Farquhar and a relative of Jellicoe.
She is in her early twenties and is an exqui-
site type of English blonde.
Those who remember her as Leah Ilalin-
bonrg will be sorry to hear that Mrs. James
Gillies has lost iier soldier husband. He
went to France as a sergeant-major and was
wounded ; then while he was being carried to
a casualty station a shell exploded that wiped
out the stretcher-bearers and their burden.
Mrs. Gillies, a handsome young Jewess, was
seen on the stage, both under repertory
auspices and with the Firm. She was
married just before her soldier left Australia,
and Leah followed him to England. She is
now with his parents in Glasgow.
Hal Gye has news of Trooper Waller,
his brother, who has been shot through the
elbow. Another warrior brother was killed
some months ago; a third (Percy) is
still in the field. They all started out early
in the whr.
Mrs. Forrest is in Melbourne with her
new daughter-in-law. Her son, Captain
Forrest, is working at Caulfield Military
Hospital for the present. They all hail
from Charters Towers. Mrs. Forrest’s sis-
ter is married to the fighting Dr. Huxtable
who was lately D.S.O.’d.
From a soldier brother, now drearily con-
valescing in a French hospital. (He has
been on active service since Christmas,
15)14) :
I suppose this business must come to an end some
day, and maybe I shall sail into Port Melbourne
again, but I don’t, seem to realise the possibility.
Civil life seems so far off —a separate existence.
Those who have been out here a while sometimes
discuss “after the war” and the wonderful things
they will do when peace is declared. They don't
really mean it; they speak as small boys speak of
what they would do if they had a million pounds.
Shell-shock won’t knock over as many men as peace-
shock.
Lieut.-Colonel Angus Fraser, D. 5.0.. who
has just come back to Australia three
years abroad, is a son of Samuel Fraser,
who had charge of the Scotclibyterian flock
at Terang for many years. Colonel Fraser,
who won his decoration as a surgeon with
a Light Horse field-ambulance, returns in
company with another war-veteran —his
wife, who did hospital work while her Angus
was in the firing-line.
The Electrical Engineering School at the
Melbourne Uni. has a handsome war-record.
The only student to complete his course
during ill]7 was Fred. Ewart Moreton, who
is now a busy B.E.E. The other advanced
men are all at the Front. The new elec-
trical bachelor, who is a son of Dr.
Frederick Moreton, is an original Anzac.
having been one of the earliest ashore during
the Landing.
Miss Adelaide’s assortment: —
The hot January that Inclement Wragge promised
us arrived. Last week the thermometer bubbled well
over the century and reduced US’ all to pulp. A pro-
mised change at the week-end was fulfilled, but it
amounted only to a brief squall that flooded and
unroofed a house or two in the hills and made the
plains steam like a Turkish hath. If February is
going to be a repetition of January you will pro-
bably hear of the collapse of most of our patriotic
workers, because next month sees the renewal of
button days, carnivals, etc.
For 1918 the Vvar Council has adopted a new
method of settling the claims of rival societies for
dates. It has mapped the year’s programme and
allotted to each fund a consecutive fortnight or
month for its brigandage. The Soldiers’ Home
League at Fullarton leads off with the first fortnight
in February; then comes the Trench Comforts Fund
to end the month. In March there will be a War
Savings appeal, and for April the Returned Soldiers’
Association is arranging for a strenuous fortnight.
The Master of the Supreme Court, W. L. Stuart,
who is also president of the Rejected Volunteers’
Association, and was for some time in command of
the Mitcham Camp, is to fill the late Commissioner
Russell’s place on the Teachers’ Superannuation,
Fund. While he is busy with the Pethick Board of-
Inquiry (another offshoot of the land scandals in-
vestigations) his work at the court will be done by
E. M. Sabine.
Distinctions have come thick and fast to the
Swifts’ two sons at the Front. Three weeks after
their youngest son,, Neville, was awarded the Military
Cross came news of the same honor going to the
second son, Brian, who is a captain with the
R.A.M.C., and now the youngest boy has a bar to
Ms medal. The eldest son had the bad luck to be in
Germany when war broke out, and has been interned
in that devil-spawned country ever since.
Lady Galway is still at Victor Harbor, though she
put in a few days in town during the past week.
Ilis Ex. has gone to Melbourne to pass the time o’
day to the Munro-Fergusons.
Cpl. Pitts, a South Australian by adoption, who
before he enlisted was an A.B. on the Governor Mus-
grave, has escaped from Germany. He and another
Australian broke through the bars of their prison
windows, slid down a steel rope from the third;
storey, managed to get to Dusseldorf by train, and
then walked three nights to the frontier. Their
imprisonment dated from 1916.
There is to be at least one returned soldier
amongst the candidates at the State elections on
April 6 —Capt. Blackburn, V.C., who has been nomi-
nated by the. Nationalists for Sturt. Major Smeaton
stood down in his favor so that peace might prevail
between two factions. Also, we are to have at least
one woman candidate. She aspires to be one of the
members for Adelaide.
Something unusual and French happened at
Gawler when the wife of the retiring mayor at-
tended upon his successor to avenge a fancied in-
sult paid to her husband. Not being quite Parisian,
she did not shoot or rffhb his Worship; she merely
smacked his face hard. In Court a good deal was
made of an “open letter” put in the loeal paper by
the new mayor. It claimed that Gawler was
“approaching a state of things common in Tibet,
where the holy men abode outside the city, and
'lived on the bodies of the dead.” The Court said it
was satisfied that this mysterious insult referred to
the ex-mayor, and “admired any woman who upheld
her husband,” though it had no sympathy with the
assault. Claimed, £49; suggested as sufficient, Is.;
£3.
Rex Syme, the youthful editor and manager of
Adelaide Sunday Mail, has been congratulated by
his staff (with a few solid objects as a side-line)
on his marriage. Syme left the Register, with
Clarence Moody, to start the Mail, and on Moody’s
dropping out of the management, succeeded to it.
Our Returned Soldiers' Assoc, is to' have an
attractive home. The Repatriation Minister has made
a deal for a place in Grenfell-street, now occupied
by Dalgety’s, who are building in Currie-street and
will move there about next dune. The Government
has undertaken to pay £750 a year for five years,
plus rates, insurance and repairs. But “no liquor,”
says the Minister; “and no politics.” What are
politics, though? For instance, does this mean that
the assoc, isn’t to squeal no matter how badly its
members are treated?
Big Bill Baillieu’s second daughter, Bertie,
has agreed to risk it with Bob Ramsay, son
of the Brighton Ramsays. Their engage-
ment is just announced. He is a Royal Field
Artillery lieutenant, who has been gassed
and wounded on land and torpedoed—in the
Mongolia—at sea.
The sporting Miss Creswick, of Yarrien, is
linking up with Dr. Martin. To equalise
this and the Ramsay-Baillieu engagement
on the society card, the remaining daughter
of an impressive-looking Toorak widow has
scratched with the returned soldier of Scotch
parentage; and the buxom, fair and popu-
lar daughter of a lately-deceased Banana-
land squatter has discarded her naval fiance.
Two Melbonrnites will marry two
Bananalanders in March.. Both weddings
are booked for the Grammar School chapel.
Gwen Warnock weds with young Wharton,
son of mining-man Wharton, and will be
carried away by her bridegroom to Clon-
curry. Nan O’Hara is the other bride;
her home also will lie in the far North, but
very close to sister Mrs. nowan. Name of
Nan’s bridegroom? Good gracious, I’ve for-
gotten it 1
Benedict Michael Ivlatchko, the handsome
Russian doctor, sends a humorous word of
warning from a Japanese boat outward
bound. “Never marry a lady with a big
trousseau.” he writes. “The roomy cabins
of this ship are sufficient for my Billie and
her boxes, but not for me and my big black
boots. Such a life!” Ivlatchko married'
Billie, daughter of iron merchant Duckett.
The lovesick bridegroom, with his metaphors
rather mixed, adds that his sweet little
wattle-blossom will try her wings in Japan
before settling down in Russia. But can
anyone settle down in Russia? Or perhaps
it’s easier to settle down than settle up.
A girl-friend back from Sydney says she
saw Norah Dantoine’s twins and found them
very nice pink babies. Mrs. Dantoine was
one of the squatting Fields, of the Apple
Island, before she married one of Melbourne's
best-looking bachelors. Norah yearned to
possess a girl baby, but her patriotic con-
science hoped for a boy ; so Providence sent
her mixed twins.
The newlywed Winter-living pair have
lied to the Apple Isle for their honeymoon.
The bridegroom’s Military Cross announce-
ment got here the Very week of the wending.
This flying Captain is suffering from shat-
tered nerves. Matrimony seems to be the
fashionable cure for nerve-shatter.
The excellent Pimple Cross Service of
Victoria, which latterly has been somewhat
neglected by the public, has been making
another attempt to rake in funds. What the
Red Cross does for the soldier the Purple
Cross does for the warlnrse; but it can’t be
done without cash. Wherefore the manage-
ment has been reaching out into the country.
A “patriotic carnival” has been held at
Dandenong, a big-stock centre where the
Montgomery sisters helped to put the Purple
Cross girls through their capers, while some-
where in the background, calm but persist-
ent, the Purple Cross "ioneer. Maud Harvie,
attended to the labelling of the bags of coin
for dispatch to the service abroad.
Red Cross workers in Melbourne are re-
marking that the wives of politicians in
general, and of one politician in particular,
are no longer figuring prominently in the
monotonous business. You see, the State
election is just over, and the danger of an
early Federal election seems past. It’s a
nice’ degrading business, this politics.
Regarding the thickness of the German
national skin, here is a yarn from an up-
country health resort. Two Melbourne girls,
staying at the principal boarding-house,
were pestered by the companionship of a
young German. If they evaded him on the
premises they were bound to find him on the
verandah, watching for their out-going. The
young girls have "boys” and brothers at the
war, so the pertinacious attentions of the
enemy alien hurt them. But snubbing was
wasted on the smiling bounder. Their
endeavors to get him to take a hint were a
failure every time. Then they carried their
grievance to a tall, heavyweight Melbourne
solicitor who was rusticating at the house.
The German person was spoiling their holi-
day, they said, and if they couldn’t shake
him off they were going back to town at
once. The verv smirk of him made them
sick. The huge man-of-law went along to
the German person and told him to stop his
persecution at once or have his head knocked
off; and then, for the first time, the young
nuisance understood that he was not popular
with the fair sex. His hide was penetrated.
The threat of personal violence did it.
On Satuiday night Lois and Cacialli
began a series of operatic concerts at the
Town Hall. Melbourne, grilling and stew-
ing in sweltering heat, could not send a
good house. It was the city’s bad luck, for
the artists were in fine form. Lois especially
was in tremendous voice and very fit after
his successful eye operation. Dr. Ryan sat
proudly in the front rows and gazed on the
case he had saved from blindness. Lois
excitedly noticed him, and also signalled the
beloved matron of the hospital and his pet
nurse. Mrs. Pohlman motored up from her
seaside place. She wore filmy black lace
and some diamonds. Mrs. Lois showed a
long swan-like neck out of a sea-green dress.
Gertrude Johnson, a Melbourne operatic
artist who has been touring Bananaland,
gave extracts from some little-known things
by Donizetti. Her dress was amber-colored
tulle, and she wore diamond earrings and a
gilt laurel-wreath on her hair. Elsie Davies,
working wonders with lyric opera, was an
astounding presence. She wore a cap and
chin-strap of pearls, a breastplate of dia-
monds and a white satin gown with a top
decoration of pearl strings. If the pearls
had been real, some brave bushranger would
have abducted the large singer and floated
her into a company. Ann Williams, show-
ing smart fawn ankles under a black glacd
spread of skirt, was another decorative item
on the platform. Lois was groomed to per-
fection as usual. Cacialli wore gorgeous
waistcoat buttons and had his coat braced
across his chest with straps—possibly to
save his garments in the stress of the deep
lower notes. The season goes on at intervals
during the fortnight, and the programmes
are worth stewing for.
On the first-night of “Winnie Brooke,
Widow,” at the Tivoli. Ada Reeve somehow
contrived to look fresh and cool. Dr. Mar-
garet McLorinan was with a party in the
front row of the circle, and Major Charley
Cunningham, lately back from London, put
in an appearance. A bunch of returned
soldiers in the stalls gave Anzac Ada the
reception she deserved. But the beaches
were calling, and there were many vacant
pens.
Some Melbourne girls are fussing over
the Hawaiian men of the Muriel Starr eo.
in the same mad way as they rushed the
Fijians. They take them for motor-rides
and tea-parties, and generally sit at the
feet of the brown visitors.
Authoress Kathleen Dearden is down
from Bananaland looking for a cool spot
to rest in. She has with her an IS-year-
old son who is so terribly Stage-struck
that he would even take tickets at the door,
so as to be in the “atmosphere.” This
comes of having a brilliant mother who
writes plays. Young Charles has some-
thing to work on ; he gained a good deal of
approving comment with his amateur
theatrical work in Brisbane.
Bert Bailey is beginning to pick up his
shattered health again. He suffered simul-
taneously from gastritis, lumbago and ten-
shows-a-week—and the worst of them was
ten-shows. He is somewhat thinner now,
but almost as cheerful as ever. As a panto,
dame he wins the envious admiration of
the woman because of his neat footwear and
ankles. His outfits are weird but exquisitely
fitted.
Incidentally, there’s a riddle going around.
If Bert Bailey, a hardened, mature man,
breaks down under the unaccustomed strain
of 10 panto, shows a week, how much good
do 10 panto, shows a week do the growing
youngsters of a children’s panto.?
Yours sincerely.
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-

“F.Y.7”: “Ses” need not worry —the
carpet-snake is not disappearing from our midst,
but is flourishing in the land. Likewise, far from
being restricted to a narrow strip of Ihe East Coast,
it is found all over the west, particularly in forest
country, where its bosom friend and chief ort,
the ’possum, has his habitat. “Ses” is right in
commending it as a ratter. The Graziers’ Butcher-
ing Co. had a big carpet for years in its skin
store in South Brisbane, and curious visitors were
often taken to inspect it w’hile it was sleeping
curled up on the roof beams. Billy Hancock, who
6onie years ago ran a store at Tamata, on the Gira
(New Guinea) goldfield, had a carpet, and fre-
quently got into trouble with miners who happened
to come on the streak unexpectedly. Like “Ses’s”
friend who wallowed in the milk vat, this fellow
had a passion for cow-juice, but preferred it
well mixed with rum. This led to it occasionally
seeing imaginary human beings, with distressing
results. The poor thing's end was a shocking one.
It woke up in the night with a fearful thirst and
making its way to the bar coiled itself about a
bottle of rum with the idea of constricting it.
Unhappily, owing to the state of its nerves, it over-
did it, with the result that Bilty found it in the
morning cut into neat sections by the broken glass.
Billy’s native carriers, who held the serpent in
great esteem, despite its failings, wept bitterly at
its funeral—which was also their breakfast.
“Karee”: “S. S. Severn’’ (B. 20/12/’l7)
says that pourri-pourri' exists in England. Ireland’s
got her share, and so has Brittany, where “charms”
are sold for many things, and there is a practice
savoring of “pointing the bone.” Should you wish
to get rid of an enemy, you give the sorcerer a piece
of his clothing, hair or nails, which he’ll affix-to a
wax doll, duly christened in the enemy’s name. A
fortnight after the doll will be stabbed, and your
friend will go west suddenly. Should you prefer for
him a lingering death the doll is slowly melted in
the sun. I saw a woman in Bejgium rush out when
a mob of sheep was passing and bump her scared
four-year-old on the back of the jumbucks as fast as
she could before they scattered. To the outraged
shepherd, wh'o pointed out the error of her ways in a
firework of ( Flemish expletives, she replied that it
was a sure way of preventing croup, and I have no
reason for doubting her veracity, though she did
charge me two francs for one “eggs-cook.”
“Jimmie Pannikin": What’s the best time
to get playtpus skins? Most of the Muddybiclgee
whalers, hunters and fishers with whom I discussed
the matter held that, “of course, winter is the time
to get furs of any kind” ; but the men of the abo.
station at Brungle, very keen hunters and fishers,
contended just as strongly that, though winter was
the time when most furs were at their best, the
platypus’s coat is an exception. It is in its finest
condition, they said, during the summer months.
For myself, I obtained skins both during summer and
winter and found little difference in the quality of
the fur.
* * * *
“Pick”: “Wart” (B. 3/l/’3S). in quoting
from Surveyor Hodgkinson, opines that the latter,
in referring to “bush biscuit,” had never heard of
damper. “Wart” is on the wrong spoor. The
flat cakes baked on the coals are, to the polite,
known as johnny-cakes; bushmen know them better
by a name that a religious paper wouldn’t print.
Damper is a loaf of unleavened bread, cooked in the
ashes. Johnny-cake is the hurried expedient of
the breadless wayfarer on trek ; damper the more
leisurely preparation of the established camp.

goin' like that?”
BROWNIE: “ Well, ole man, if I stick to beer
I git fall before I'm shirk, an’ if I stick
to yin Cm shick before I'm full.
THIS way I yet shick and full at the
same time!"

m a competition, lose anything that the leatherhead

“McNorth”: A Papuan planter had a
thousand-gallon galvanised-iron tank made and landed
on the beach nearest his place. There were 15 miles
of bush-track between the plantation and the sea, and
no vehicles, and as the tank was too bulky to be
carried on poles the planter was puzzled. At last
he sent a gang of niggers and told them to do the
best they could. Those coons rose to the occasion.
Two of their number roughly cleared the track of
bushes and other obstructions, and the mob rolled
the tank along the ground. At
the end of a week it arrived little
the worse for its bumpy journey.
Some years ago half a dozen abos.
carried a 10ft. dinghy through the
bush from Cape York to the spot
where the telegraph-line crosses
the .Tardine River. Distance,
30-odd miles; time, five days. A
traction-engine attached to a pair
of loaded waggons got caught by
a phenomenally early wet season
in the Gulf country. There were
20 miles of greasy mud ahead and
a flooded river astern. The boss
didn’t want to camp there for
three or four months till the end
of the wet, so he made a shot at
getting out. As the engine’s
wheels would not grip in the mud
he fastened the winding-rope
which is attached to all such
craft to a tree ahead and set the
winding-gear going. The engine
slowly dragged its lumbering self
up to the tree. The waggons were
■ then hooked on to the rope and
brought up to the engine. Then
the rope was taken ahead again
and the whole manoeuvre repeated ad inf. The boss
reckoned that 20 miles the longest he ever covered.
It took a month.

“C. direction”: The egg-mines of
“xMcNorth” at Cape York (B. 3/1 /’ 1S) are all very
well for bloated proprietors of Binghis ; but there is
too much uncertainty about the fowl-berry mined
from the scrub-hen’s dump. This scribe prefers,
when the egg-madness comes upon him, to make for
one of the reef-islets north of Cooktown, Two Isles
for example, about August. Eggs there outcrop on
the surface, so that the trouble of putting down a
shaft is saved. The only tools needed are a twig
broom and a bucket. Selecting a well-egged area in
the low scrub, the ground is swept clear for a space
corresponding to Jthe appetite. This is best done in
the afternoon when the birds are away fishing. Next
morning another call allows the outcrop to be col-
lected, all fresli and ready for the ' pot, and the
ground is prepared for the next day. The genuine
egg-enthusiast can fill in the weary hours of waiting,
on the first night, by selecting likely samples and
examining them over a candle in a kerosene-tin until
he has enough for supper.
“Barcoo”: “McNorth’s” black gin’s catch
of 48 scrub-turkeys’ eggs in a day’s forage in
North Queensland (B. 3/1/78) isn’t much com-
pared with what can be done around Lakes Galilee
and Buchanan. The former is one of Australia’s
sanctuaries for waterfowl of every description, from
the black swan to the insignificant dab-chick. It is
90 miles in circumference; and in the reeds and
undergrowth which go right round it the fowl-fruit
is deposited in uncountable quantities. The locality
is periodically visited by kangaroo-shooters and
selectors, accompanied by numerous empty petrol
tins, some mutton fat and coarse salt. The eggs
are smeared with the dripping, packed in layers in
the tins and covered with the salt. In this way the
spoil will keep for 12 months at least. Dozens of

JACK VALIANT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jack Valiant’s left the ring at last —
Through forty years he fought unshamed.
The stress is o’er, the struggle past—
Jack Valiant stands with soul untamed.
The odds were great, yet great his will ;
And evermore, though sent to ground,
lie beat the count, and, smiling still,
Came back to fight another round.
Gaunt Drought and Ruin, savage foes,
Through year and year with brutal power
Beat down his guard, blows thick on blows;
And yet he did not cringe nor cower.
«
A hero he; no wise akin
To those whose deeds are cried aloud—
The petty mannikins who win
The plaudits of the gaping crowd.
He met such odds as well might break
A lesser strength with iron nerve,
Watched over by the gods who take
Account of those who truly serve.
A fighter and a builder too !
Beside his life-work, sure and sound,
How stand the windy, wordy crew
Who make a mountain of a mound !
As boy and man he tamed and hewed
The tree, the rock, the stubborn soil,
And every dawn beheld renewed
His life of stern and searing toil.
Afar from city lights withdrawn
He chose his ground and made his fight;
The sunset knew him and the dawn,
The midnight and the stars of night.
Day in day out, in glow and gloom,
He strove as slaves and heroes strive—
Dug dams to make the desert bloom,
Made grass to grow and flocks to thrive;
Built fences, miles and miles of fence ;
Rode leagues and leagues of route and track,
Green dawn at hand, or noon intense,
With stirless heat, or midnight black.
Hot days and breathless nights he knew,
Red realms that ached and ached for rain,
Where duststorms, blotting out the blue,
Fierce-scourged the landscape, ridge and plain
And many times, his pastures parched,
With praying lips and yearning eyes
He watched, while cloud-battalions marched,
A rainless host, across the skies.
And many times false mirage-gleams
Shone like the hopes of youth and age—
Far lures, far fantasies, far dreams
That drew him on from stage to stage.
And many times rough-riding Fate
With iron jaws and brows of strife,
And nostrils breathing smoke of hate,
Came thundering down his track of life.
And yet, withal, his heart was such
That, ste.el at times, at times light air,
It yielded straightway to the touch
Of Pity and the cry of Care.
Ah, nights of sweetness and of calm,
Wide nights of gloom and starry thrall,
The earth outbreatliing cooling balm—
Jack Valiant, how you loved them all!
And how you loved the song, the jest,
The lifted glass, the toast, the prank,
The mirth and play that kept the West,
With all its cares, so glad and frank 1
For years that sought to bow and bend
May gracious years of peace atone !
For you, Jack Valiant, were my friend,
My country’s friend, her very own !
True pioneer, you served her true —-
Gave all you had, your strength, your years,
To make her great; and great are you,
And great are all her pioneers!
And though the lights no longer glow
Around the ring, where all is night
Corona, Netlev, Momba know
The fine endurance of your fight!
N.S.W.

“P. Winkle”: After a long spell (about
seven years) locusts are again devastating the green
grass areas of Northern Vic. Where do the pests
come from ? Do their eggs take seven years to
hatch out ? Round Lake Cooper trees and stumps
are covered with sheaths from which the devastators
emerged to attack the settlers’ patches. I always
thought that locusts laid their eggs in tlTe earth.
Anyhow, there is no doubt about their appetite. A
mob stripped a 20-acre paddock of lucerne in a day
at Nanneella, close to the Mallee-Waronga channel,
which they crossed. The same paddock carried
about 40 head of stock for three months without
showing any diminution of growth. A paddock of
similar extent adjoining was saved from the
invaders by being flooded.
* * * *
“Bill R.”: When the Abo.-quacks have
written their prescriptions for treating a dog with
a tick, let me remark that all will be futile if the
animal is allowed a sun-bath. I had assisted many
valued tykes to their happy hunting-grounds by
administering unto them the so-called unfailing
specifics until an Irish setter of mine that had
been missing for 10 days crawled out of an aban-
doned miner’s tunnel in the convalescent stage
after tick fever. I gave the poor fellow a drink
and drove him back into the dampness and shade.
He recovered. Since then I have cured many dogs
and cats by simply pulling off the ticks and keeping
the patient on wet bags in a dark place. Feeding or
lying in the sun is invariably fatal.
* * * *
“Machete”: “ Jas. Hyde,” in a recent
Bulletin, says he never heard of cannibalism among
Queensland blacks south of the Johnstone River
(N.Q.). It seems to have been pretty common, all
the same. Stewart Russell, in his “Genesis of
Queensland,” a standard work on the early days
of the State, mentions it as being a regular feature
at the great meetings of the darkies of
South Queensland at the Bunya Mountains on the
Darling Downs. Every four years, when the Bunya
nuts ripened, the tribes laid aside their old feuds
and met in a sort of truce. They feasted fat on the
nuts, and, according to Russell, included human flesh,
usually that of old and infirm people, in the menu.
In the North, of course, the practice was general.
Late Chief Inspector Douglas, of the Native Police,
had many a yarn about the cannibal propensities
of the Palmer blacks. But I doubt if any Aus-
tralian abo. ever ate his brother man in the fashion
of the Papuan cannibal, who was (and is) accus-
tomed to look on his brother as kai first and man
afterwards.

“Merrigal”: The Too demolishers cited by
“45/90” (B. 3/l/’18) were evidently hot pots, but
Frank Pressy, a Riverina product, and his wife and
two others named Bannerman composed a gang
that had little to learn. Of 22,000 longtails accounted
for at Buttabone station (Warren, N.S.W.) in one
year in the ’eighties, by yarding and shooting, more
than 12,000 answered to their guns. They used
treble F black powder, behind BB shot. They drove
their quarry into a corner of any paddock and
downed them as they broke back. At stripping the
jackets Pressy was a blitherer. He could average
one a minute all day, and once he knocked the boss
of the station bandy by removing a blue flier’s pelt
in 16 seconds. He was a topper with any sort of
shooting-stick, and several times have I seen him
shy two cartridge-cases skywards and well apart, fall
on his back and get one with each barrel. The
boss mentioned was also handy with a musket. I
saw his eight consecutive bullets stop an octave
of grunters ambling across a plain—that was the
day he and Barry, of Benah station, Canonbar
(N.S.W.), shot 212 ’roos with the rifle. By the way,
who knows a better one-day kangaroo drive than
the 9000 got at Trinkey, Warialda district (N.S.W.),
some 30 years ago ?
" » » »
“Snell”: I went to Pibrock for my
Christmas debauch of fresh air, rest and recuperation,
and stayed with the Canty family. In the course of
social peregrinations I fell in with the Holts, a mild
little man and a mild little wife, and was told
later that no one could be a friend of the Holts who
abided with the Canties. “We’re not speakin’ nor
doalin’,” said old man Canty. “When the Holts
got married and came here they set to work ’n’
poisoned two of the best cow dogs man ever had.
They said the dogs was savage, ’n’ swore in court
that the dogs ’d worry their dear little baby.” “And
did the dogs ever offer to touch their baby?” I
asked. “That’s 20 years ago,” Canty replied, “ ’n’
they haven’t had a baby yet.”
**• * *
“J.G.”: Not all circus riders are such
mugs at riding buckjumpers as “S.S.” (B. 3/l/’18)
tries to make out. At a horse-sale at Ballarat about
25 years ago a fine-looking bay prad was led into
the ring, and in a few minutes he proved to be the
primest bucker ever I have seen in action. All the
hard-case riders about Ballarat were sent for rocket-
flights ; but he found his boss in a little foreign
circus-rider, who stuck to him long after the saddle
had gone. Then the showman made the outlaw fly
the gate, took him for a trip round two or three of
the centre blocks of the city, hopped him over the
gate again and re-entered the ring.
* * * *
“Te Whare”: A boss destroyer of the
pond-snail (host of the liver-fluke, abhorred of
mutton merchants) is the straw-necked ibis, which
, occurs about inland marshes and surface
pools of the south and west of N. S.
Wales in immense flocks. Of course, the
white ibis and the glossy ibis help, but
both these birds are rare, the glossy
variety especially so. The three species
are nomadic in habits, appearing occasion-
ally in unexpected quarters. Writer has
only once seen the white ibis on
the Monaro (N.S.W.) plains, but
the straw-necked species comes
ocasionally in wet seasons. The
solemn birds stand in small com-
panies around springs on the
black-soil plains, probing the soft
mud with their long sickle bills
in quest of yabbies and
water insects, or hunting
around in damp grass for
snails. They never breed
on the Monaro highlands,
but out west the untidy
stick nests, built on swamp
gums and other small tim-
ber, occur literally in
“rookeries,” and the beau-
tiful greenish-blue eggs—
very elongated and rounded
at the ends —may be gath-
ered by the bushel. Pass-
ing coastwards over the
ranges the straw-necked ibis sometimes
reaches the flats in the Bega district in
fair-sized flocks, abides there perhaps for
two or three months, and again wings its
way westward. The ibis travelling formation is
peculiar. The birds fly head to head in one long,
wavering line, extending for many yards across the
sky; black swans, on the other hand, fly in two
lines in angle, the apex of which is the leading swan.
* * * *
“Kila” : Which is the largest sheep-station
in Australia —I mean the one that shears the greatest
number of sheep ? Away back in the ’eighties
Brookong tN.S.W.) had a board of 104 at shearing
time, and as about three men are required to each
shearer this meant a little township. I know that
amongst the fraternity Brookong was reckoned to
be the boss shed out back. I fancy that famous
Kilfera, near Clare, ran it a good second,' with 100
shearers. But the bunny has put the kybosh on the
old-time glory. I know of one station near the
Murray that had 80,000 sheep before he appeared;
now it is down to 30,000. Besides the rabbit, over-
stocking did a lot of harm. In many places the
saltbush, that famous old standby, has vanished.
Some paddocks that were masses of cotton bush and
other edible scrub are now as bare as the N.S.W.
Treasury. I don’t think they will ever recover, either.
A FOUNDATION OF LAW.
EXCITED OLD PARTY: “Help! Help! Call the police! Where’s the police?"
BYSTANDER: “Get out, per ole goat—the police is underneath!"
“Edward Barbor” : We’ve had it out about
strange places for birds to camp; now what about
the queerest place for bees to nest? At the Balook
(Vic.) post office a swarm lately took possession of
the letter-box, and are now busy preparing comb.
The postmistress intimates that anyone may have
the lot as a going concern on application.
* * * *
“S.L.E.W.”: Passing through Linville
(Q.) I dropped into a pub. On a table were two
deer-heads with full-grown antlers. These antlers
were so firmly interlocked that to separate them
without breaking them was impossible. They told
their own tale. The stags had fought for a bride,
and, their main weapons becoming linked, the antago-
nists lay down together and gave up the ghost. Thus
they were found by a bullpcky in the scrub. Deer
are very plentiful among the hills of the Brisbane
River Valley.
“Hoplocephalus” : Without desiring to east
any slur on Arizona (B. 3/l/’18), a hair rope, not
a tarred one, was the orthodox snake-repeller in
Texas in the good old days of no fences and cheap
cartridges, celebrated in the pictures. The rattler,
provided that "he was a real rattler and not a pro-
duct of boot-leg whisky, was supposed to be too
annoyed by the hairs getting under his belly scales
as he crossed to recollect his intention of biting the
sleeper.
* * * *
“Hard-up, Esq.” : Squatters by destroying
timber near water are helping to increase the
blowfly pest. No tree should be allowed to be cut
or ringbarked within three miles of the bank of any
stream, because in hot weather if there is green tim-
ber near water the flies collect upon it during the day
and don’t trouble the sheep. The jumbucks, as a rule,
keep right away from the trees during the heat.
Planting belts of shade trees will further help to
defeat the fly curse. It was a terrible mistake to
whack down the big gums near the Murray. Here’s
another thing. Why isn’t proper investigation given
to the tiger-fly ? It is a deadly enemy to other flies,
including the “blow,” which is making such big
trouble for the squatter.
“Fanuela”: A farmer argued into my
attentive ear that sheep generally feed with their
rear-ends to the breeze and that cattle generally face
it. Always understood that the reverse was correct.
Anyway, whichever it is, why is it?
* * * *
“H.V.E.” : It is through no fault of any
wild bird that its species shows a declining birth-
rate. If the first clutch of eggs or nestlings comes
to grief the pair lose no time in building another
nest, and the female produces the nucleus of another
brood. A pair of white-shafted fantails .(“cranky
fan”) plastered their ethereal wineglass-shaped nest
this season on the low twig of a laurel bush. Chicks
arrived in due course, but a laughing-jack (I think)
scoffed the lot. The fans promptly built again in
another laurel bush a few yards distant from the
first. Three eggs hatched out safely, and the chicks
qualified for the flying corps. But the morning fol-
lowing my last inspection I found two lying dead
beneath the nest, one with a crushed skull and the
other with a broken neck. The third chick escaped
injury and flew about with its parents. Still these
fantails are not satisfied. They are determined to
deliver the full quota of goods, and have built a
third time, in the same bush. (The second nest, by
the way, swarmed with lice.) The same thing hap-
pens if the nest and eggs are destroyed in exception-
ally heavy rains, or the nestlings drowned. The
yellow-throated scrub-wren (“devil-bird”) hangs its
enormous nest from the twigs of sassafras and wild-
willow about secluded coastal gullies- If heavy rain
comes the nest gets soaked through, breaks its cob-
web attachment and falls to the ground. But the
devils either build a new or renovate an old nest —
the latter a habit of theirs. I have found the nest of
this bird with eggs as late in the season as the
middle of May.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Cape Wollomai” : The mutton-bird (sooty
petrel) in November becomes Australia’s
champion egg-layer, and is responsible for
quite a flourishing industry in parts of
coastal Victoria and the Barrier and Fur-
neaux Groups in Bass Straits. The egg—
usually one per bird —is laid in a burrow in
the sandy soil, which extends for a good
many feet underground in some cases. The
birds arrive in immense flocks with the first
of the equinoctial gales—known locally as
“mutton-bird gales”—and proceed to clean
out the holes with great vigor. The mutton-
birder then takes his faithful hook, made
from a piece of doubled fencing-wire lashed
to a stick, and burgles the home. Usually he
gets the egg alone; sometimes the egg and an
indignant mother mutton-bird; sometimes
one or both of these and a snake; sometimes
the snake only; sometimes nothing at all.
The eggs at ordinary times are worth a bob
a dozen, and are largely bought by pastry-
cooks. There is a tale of an enthusiastic
poultry-fancier who bought a very cheap lot
of duck-eggs in November and incubated
out a fine family of sooty petrels. The
mutton-bird of commerce, beloved by the
more robust variety of late drunk, is the
young bird taken from the burrow, fried in
its own oil and packed in barrels. The
Straits Islander gets his light, his bedding
and most of his living from the mutton-bird ;
but the industry is being destroyed by pastur-
ing sheep on the rookeries.
“ivlurkah”: Some of P.M.G. Webster’s
telegraph regulations urgently require revi-
sion. For instance, why prohibit the use of
figures in the text of private messages? They
are permitted and largely used in M.L. and
U.S.A.: also they are used in railway tele-
grams, where correctness is very necessary.
Further, the convenience of figures is per-
mitted in Government telegrams. From the
public’s point of view the regulations re-
garding liability are outrageously unfair.
The addressee of a telegram, doubting the
correctness of the message, can get a repe-
tition thereof by paying the cost of two tele-
grams—one the official advice requesting the
repetitipn and the other the repetition itself.
Should, however, events prove that both the
original and the repeated copy were incorrect,
Article 34(5 of the current Postal Guide pro-
vides that “no responsibility shall be under-
taken nor any claim for compensation enter-
tained, for any errors or mistakes in trans-
mission or delay in delivery of telegrams,
whether repeated or unrepeated.... from
whatever cause” such hash-up may have re-
sulted. This reservation, be it noted, also
refers to the charges for transmission, though
a footnote to this article states that if,
through Departmental blunder, a message
has been rendered useless, “the P.M.G. may,
as an act of grace and not in consequence of
any legal liability, refund the amount paid
for transmission.”
“Tropical Druid”: Somebody was lately
holding forth on the goodness of tinned rab-
bit, and demanding that more of it be sent
to our troops in France. They can have it
all if it’s the same as is supplied to the
Pacific forces. Experience has shown that
if there is any hard yakker to be done rabbit
is no good unless helped out by a meat ration.
Many a time I have seen tins of it left on
the roadside by disillusioned patrol parties.
Even niggers sometimes refuse it. Brown
Brother calls it “meat belong pussy,” liis
only knowledge of bunny being got from the
labels.
* * * *
“Fax”: It is seemingly impossible to get
the Vic. State War Council to move in the
matter of a certain deficiency in the
accounts of the former sec. of one of the
soldiers’ comforts funds. From the outset
the excuse for hushing it up has been that
the shortage was only £5O, and that a prose-
cution would do the Cause more than £5O
worth of injury. 'This compounding of a
felony—if it was a felony—might have been
sound diplomacy had everybody kept silent.
But there seems to have been so much shrug-
ging of shoulders that the scandal has been
magnified; and, in consequence, action would
probably be taken now if it weren’t that
other people’s carelessness would be exposed.
“Carburetted Hydrogen” : The big Sydney
gas co. pleads that in the month when 22
tests out of 53 showed that it wasn’t giving
its customers full measure it was using-up
unsatisfactory strike-coal. No doubt the
Government will be bluffed; but it shouldn’t
be, because all the time there was in N. S.
Wales, and handy to Sydney, the finest gas-
enriching shale in the world. Why was it
not used? Scores of thousands of tons have
been exported to enrich the gas in German
towns, which paid more than four times the
price per ton that our gas pirates pay for
best gas-coal.
* * * . *
“F.C.” : The fact that an ejectment order
was lately granted in Melbourne against a
soldier’s wife who owed 20 weeks’ rent and
firmly declined to either pay or vacate the
premises, reminds me. Outside the suburb
of Oakleigh a citizen built a cottage on an
allotment he owned, lie bad got it all fin-
ished, and was tidying up one evening pre-
paratory to putting up a ‘‘To-let’’ notice,
when a casual person with a horse and cart
and a few duds occurred and asked if he
might camp there for a day or so—he was
travelling to GippslancT and his horse was
knocked up. The kindly cit. consented. A
week later he came along and found the
casual person comfortably installed, with a
wife, a daughter and two upstanding sons;
also a discourteous dog and a dozen fowls.
The cit. inquired about rent, but the other
failed to understand him. The casual person
had been allowed to occupy the house, and
nothing had been said about rent, and he
proposed to dwell there permanently. He
was a hefty man, with two hefty sons, so the
kindly citizen went to the law, and was
advised to remove the roof. He removed the
roof, luckily of iron, and the stranger
departed. Then the owner put the roof on
again. But nobody gets into that house
again without paying the first week’s rent in
advance.
* # * *
“Barcoo”: Last year when Clermont
(C.Q.) had been nearly blotted out by a
flood the survivors woke up. After years of
neglected warnings they started to shift
back from the death-trap on the lagoon to
the near-by high and dry ridge. The build-
ings that would stand the strain were re-
moved intact on rollers. In this way
Peterson’s pub, the Grand, wended its way
to its new resting place, taking a fortnight
to do the 880 yards, business being con-
tinued as usual right through the operation.
“A.M.J.” : Now that the Broken Hill Prop,
is installing rolls in the Newcastle Steel
Works for making steel plates, Australia has
a chance to start on building steel ships.
Even yet we don’t seem to have grasped the
fact that the earning power of a steel vessel
for several years after the war will more
than compensate for its present high cost.
While we were hanging round the pawnshop
door Japan was getting even busier than
usual in the ship-building line. In addition
to using the output of his own rolling mills,
Nippon was paying as much as 6d. per lb.
for Yankee plates delivered in Japan. Even
at this price his ship-plate imports increased
from 3275 tons in 1914 to 138,565 tons in the
first 10 months of 1917. Nippon is not keen
on importing, as the low figures for 1914
show, but he was sufficiently seized with the
wisdom of getting his ships on the water and
in among the big freights to buy what his
ever-increasing blast-furnaces couldn’t turn
out. What Australia has done in the same
time will not bear thinking about; but if
we gagged the politicians and got to work,
we might even yet have something more to
live on than Billjim’s reputation and the
blessing of Cohen.
“Copabella”: A Victorian metropolitan
shire notorious for its neglect of ordinary
sanitation was suddenly bitten with a desire
to administer the Pure Foods Act. It
therefore hauled to court a couple of very
petty traders who had been guilty of selling
cream of tartar that transgressed to the
extent of one part in 322500! Then it
rammed the lesson home by charging in its
“costs” Is. 9d. for the sample it had bought,
and which, after analysis, it did not return .
Nothing, of course, was done to the whole-
saler who supplied the widow. He might
have fought the case.
* * * *
“E..T.K.”: If F. W. Ledger, of Yarra-
wonga (Vic.), isn’t a good entry for the best
Australian odd-job man I’m content to lose
my deposit. Firstly, he is a hairdresser, and
is able to use the blade with either hand. He
makes a specialty of massage. He is a
chiropodist. He is a gardener. He can milk
cows with anyone in the business. In his
spare time lie goes in for pig-jobbing and
cattle-dealing. He is a tent and tarpaulin
maker, and supplies the local demand for
water-bags. All the waggon hammocks in
these parts and for miles around are manu-
factured by him. Itope-splicing is one of bis
fine arts. He runs the only jewellery estab-
lishment in the town, and mends watches
and clocks. As a judge of poultry and bees
his services are always in demand. He con-
trols a musical-instruments business, and, of
course, runs the usual tobacco emporium.
Finally, he is a strong temperance advocate.
• * * *
“Wandi” : I was in a store in Caboolture
one day when an aborigine, who was em-
ployed by a timber-getter, came in with a
slab of pine, three-feet long by a foot wide,
which lie dumped on the counter with the
explanation that it was a letter. On one
side of it was written in charcoal: “Please
send me one pound of tobacco and a tin of
golden syrup. Money enclosed. Yours truly,
Robert Breeze.” “Where’s the money ? ’ the
bearer was asked. “No money,” was the
answer. “Your boss says ‘money enclosed
here,” said the storekeeper tapping the char-
coaled lines. Muni repeated that no money
had sent; nothing, in fact, bad been
said to him about money. Surmising that
the boss had not trusted his boy, I care-
fully examined the slab, and in one end dis-
covered a plugged auger-hole. The slab was
split with an axe, and the enclosed cash, in
shillings, was revealed, to the amazement ot
the blackfellow who had brought it.
* * * *
“Snowy” : If Egypt is a “gift of tbe Nile, ’
the 30,000 acres of fat alluvial land about
Orbost (Vic.), which produces more maize,
pigs and pumpkins than any other equal area
i,. the world, is certainly a Christmas-box
from the Snowy River. The washings from
a hundred different kinds of soil have been
laid down in the old estuary to a depth of
no one knows how much, and the process is
still going on. There are places where three
fences, one on top of the other, have been
built in the last 30 years. The river has
buried the first in mud and then repeated the
process with the second, and an extra-big
flood or two have started to put a blanket
over the third.
INEXPLICABLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Foul eggs my giocer sent to me,
A pound of execrable tea.
(A man of good repute is he.)
The butcher to my home convoyed
A pound of steak, and I enjoyed
A hunk of changeless ruberoid.
(My butcher has a foremost perch
Among respectables at church ;
No rumors his good name besmirch.)
A dozen peaches next I had,
And they were dangerously bad.
(The fruiterer’s a worthy lad.)
1 paid good coin wool socks to get.
They're cotton. (To my great regret
The draper is looked up to yet.)
To each, some little thing to buy,
I gave a bad half-crown. Now I
In gaol for one whole year must lie!
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.

LOSING YOUR NERVE.
Everybody has some store of nervous
energy. When work or worry without suffi-
cient rest exhausts this store, a condition
results that medical men call neurasthenia.
It is commonly met with in those who
have cared for sick relatives, and business-
men who worrv over their affairs and neg-
lect to take proper rest. Women who work
beyond their strength, anyone who has too
much excitement and too little rest may
show the symptoms.
The signs are clear. Your complexion
becomes pale, you imagine unpleasant
things, your brain insists on working when
you want to go to sleep. Sometimes you are
melancholy. Things that used to please you
no longei; do so. Constipation is usually
present. You worry about yourself and your
work and cannot forget your anxieties.
No doctor • can cure neurasthenia; you
have to do it yourself. First, worry less and
don’t overtax your strength ; then begin Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for pale people, for
they supply new blood to feed your worn-out
nerves and so steadily build up your health.
Never accept any substitute at shops.
The Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Box
594 G.P.0., Sydney, has published a free
book on nervous disorders that contains a
chapter on neurasthenia, in which the symp-
toms are fully described and the correct
treatment given.— Advt.
