REMARKABLE HAIR GROWTH
FOLLOWS USE OF SIMPLE
FORMULA.
During the last few years a simple hair-
growing formula has appeared on several
occasions in leading publications, and has
aroused a great deal of interest, not only on
account of its simplicity and inexpensive-
ness, but also because of the remarkable
results so uniformly reported from its use.
Increased growths of from one to three
inches a month, disappearance of dandruff
in one to three days and the stopping of fall-
ing hair in the same time, making harsh,
dry, brittle hair soft, luxuriant and glossy,
etc., etc., are quite frequently reported. The
tonic is prepared from the following
formula : 3oz. bay rum, i drachm menthol
crystals, and loz. Lavona de Composee, and
we understand that the ingredients can be
obtained at small cost from practically all
chemists, many of whom have also arranged
to hold a stock of the complete preparation.
This is supplied in convenient sprinkler-
topped bottles, under the name of Lavona
Hair Tonic, and doubtless many readers will
prefer to obtain the hair tonic in this com-
plete form. — Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The object of the Censorship is to keep
information from the enemy. The enemy is
any person who would vote against you if
lie knew the truth.
Last Commonwealth Parliament had a
majority in favor of Hughes going to the
Cold Country. This Parliament seems to
have a majority in favor of Hughes going to
the hottest place you can think of.
There are things like the report of the
commission of business men which was
appointed to inquire into the Defence i e-
partment administration that the Censorship
can’t very well deal with. But, thank
Heaven, in the interests of Muddle and
Inefficiency, there are still some pigeonholes
in the Department.
The Argus, realising that if a majority in
the Federal Legislature cast a dinkum vote
the Hughes Ministry would be tossed into
the garbage tin, calls upon the alleged
Nationalists to do nothing rash, lest the War
Precautions Act be not administered with
prudent restraint and raids be made on
Wealth. Ah, note we’re getting it. Hughes’s
job, then, is to Win the War for the Fat
Man ?
While we are demanding that German
colonies in the Pacific shall never go back
to the Hun, we might spare a yell or two
hi demand decent treatment of fhe terri-
tory we have in those parts. We are sup-
posed to be developing the tropical indus-
tries of Papua; yet, though Papua forms
part of the Commonwealth, the other part
exacts Customs duty on goods imported
from Papua. It is a weird way of giving
help—more like a big brother stealing the
baby’s pennies than anything else.
THE NEW ARRIVALS.
BILL AND JIM, JUN.: “KAMERAD!
KAMERAD!!”
Colonel Repington, London Times’ mili-
tary correspondent, nas left the paper be-
cause, he says, Northcliffe is “attacking”
some more generals. The impudence of!
those military people is sublime. They
muddle; they waste; they fail. But there
must me no criticism of their precious
actions. On the trumpery pretext that it
is bad for discipline, or that it will be
giving away something to the enemy, they
frame regulations making it an offence for
their employers, whose lives are in their
hands, to adversely comment on their admin-
istration. Seeing what the failure of in-
competents means to him, John Bull might
well thank God every morning that he has
got a Northcliffe big enough to attack this
cheekiest confidence trick of all times.
Putting the Bendigo pledge out of the
question, is there one single reason why
Australia should hang on to the Hughes
Government? Its most important job was
to get reinforcements. It has failed so
miserably that the country is disgraced;
and nearly every speaker at the conference
of recruiting agents in Sydney last week
declared that the failure was due to wretched
Defence Department administration. Its
handling of the finances has been shocking.
Its one piece of war-finance legislation is
the War-time Profits measure; and that is
HIS CHANCE—AND HERS.
HE : “Haw, with — er—my money and influ-
ence, y’ know, you could have all that
you desired.”
SHE: “Oh, really! Could it get you into the
army ?”
a weird jest, and is bound to be repealed.
Its handling of the wheat business lias been
a scandal; and, though there has been less
trouble over wool, every man in the busi-
ness knows that there has been muddling
there, too. It has simply piffled with the
subject of shipbuilding: who doesn’t believe
that if the whole thing had been handed
over to the Broken Hill Prop., for instance,
50 times more would have been done? Its
one definite performance has been the
appointment of a crowd of superfluous
Commonwealth police at. great expense when
Australia can’t afford to waste a penny,
and all because a hobbledehoy threw an egg'
at a politician.
Something which shows he can “think in
continents,” if necessary, is being done by
Vic.’s new Works Minister, McWhae. Most
Vic. politicians regard the
East Gippsland-Ma State
border as being at the back
o’ Beyond, and leave it at
that. However, Monaro is
only a few miles over the
dividing line, so McWhae
has agreed with N. S. Wales
to join up with Vic. by a
new 14-mile track from the
village over the border, con-
necting with the main East
Gippsland road, and capable
of carrying any sort of
traffic. Mac finds himself
in progressive company in
these negotiations, since J. D. Fitzgerald,
Minister for Local Government, is at the
N.S.W. end of the arrangement.
It was bound to come. The Argus calls
upon the country to loyally support Hughes
because he “undertook to reassume the bur-
den of administration at the request of the
Governor-General.” Also let no man, least
of all Tiie Argus, pass unfriendly remarks
about Ryan, of Queensland, for he. also
undertook the burden of administration at
the Governor’s request.
It transpires, almost as a matter of
course, that the recommendations which
Professor I.efroy, imported at considerable
expense, made re the murder of the weevil
in wheat : (he made them after three weeks’
study of local conditions) were almost
exactly the -same as those made by local
scientists more than a year ago. These
weren’t acted on, as a matter of course. It
is common enough for a prophet to have no
honor in his own country, but in Australia
the locally-grown prophet is lucky not to
get six months without the option.
Ryan, Nationalist M.L.A. for Essendon
(Vic.), who took Laborite Blackburn’s seat
at the State elections, nas not lost his con-
fidence in Ryan, Nationalist M.L.A. for
Essendon. Speaking at a Drowsy Sabbath
Afternoon in Wesley Church, he showed Aus-
tralia the way out of her industrial troubles.
It is quite easy. First you catch your
organised boss or employee.
Then, having proved him
guilty of a lock-out or a strike,
you proceed to improve him by
imposing penalties to meet the
case. These include disfran-
chisement, Federal, State and
municipal; also deregistration
for five years under the Com-
panies Act in the case of the
boss, and in the case of the
toiler exclusion from the bene-
fits of the Workmen’s Compen-
sation Act. Ryan seems deter-
mined to save his old Labor
pals from themselves, no mat-
ter what it costs them. He is
shortly to introduce an Indus-
trial Peace Bill on these lines,
without asking the permission
of Premier Bowser or anybody
else; and then we shall see —
another fly crushed in the party
machine.
“Country Ped”: What’s the
game—is the returned soldier
to be merely used as a pretext
for buying out the big squatter?
Anyway, the Vic. Board has
just bought £40,000 worth of
Purrumbete land at £l7 per
acre from a Western squatto-
crat. Yet three months ago a
Billjim, who was a practical
farmer, begged the Board to buy
for him a 60-acre irrigation
farm. A start there could be
made at once. The place is
well subdivided, with irrigation
channels and drains all through
it. There are 26 acres of
lucerne and a good six-roomed
house with all necessary out-
buildings ; in fact, everything
for a cow-farm bar cows and plant, and
only four miles from one of the largest
northern towns. The owner ‘ offered it at
£l6 an acre. But the Board wouldn’t
budge, and the soldier has gone on to
Bananaland.
It might be a good idea for Prime Minister
Hughes to hire a disinterested political friend
at £2 a week —just that he might have a
political friend.
An Australian who last year couldn’t net
4s. for his wheat has just got this letter
from his brother in Saskatchewan, Canada:
Crop good ; round here we are averaging 40
bushels to the acre and the fixed price works out
10s. 2Jkl. a bushel. So a farmer with 500 acres
under wheat gets over £lO,OOO for his little lot.
It won’t all be lifted this winter, but the mills are
going ding-dong night and day turning the grain into
(lour. Every farmer in the country now owns an
auto., and out on the trails on Sunday a horse
looks strange, for the farmer for once in his strenu-
ous life is. making big money and spending it.
The Australian, by the way, bought a sulky
on time-payment last year, and has just
written to the maker telling him to renew
the bill for another 12 months.
The Minister for Defence has repeated the
old prattle that wherever possible returned
men are being employed for preference in
his Department. Well, for a start, what
about the Staff Officer for Invalids and Re-
turned Soldiers in S. Australia? This posi-
tion is held by Capt. Campbell, who is also
starter for various racing clubs. There is
a good salary attached to the turf job; yet
many returned men, including officers, can-
not get one position, let alone two. More-
over, this is essentially a job for a returned
man; yet Campbell is not even an old
S’African soldier.
“The Ratcatcher’s Daughter”
The habit of saying “Arrest that man!” seems to
be catching. At a Voluntary Workers’ Assoc, meet-
ing in Sydney during the week George Fitzpatrick,
organising secretary, declared that the Federal
Government had blocked that little crowd of toilers
in a disheartening manner. “Mr. J. C. Watson had
even threatened to arrest Dr. Arthur and himself if
they went into the country to open branches.” Now,
how does ,T. C. Watson come to this goda’mightyship?
Incidentally, just to ascertain how much voluntary
work this son of the proletariat does, I move for a
return of the moneys paid from the Commonwealth
Treasury by way of salary, expenses, commissions,
etc., to J. C. Watson.

At present Uncle Sam is talking hopefully
about a couple of million enlistments. Aus-
tralia will, of course, have to maintain rein-
forcements for the men it has sent; but if
its Government had been better advised it
would have been content to make its army
less. Then, at any rate, Billjim wouldn’t
have been shaken up out of hospital to go
back to his trench job.
The “malcontents” of the Federal National
party are to be invited “to say whether they
are followers of the Hughes Government or
not.” That is all right in theory; but if
followers are elected on a teetotal basis and
the leaders are always going into the pub,
following becomes difficult.
“If I am in the way of union,” Hughes
told the warring parties in the House of
Reps., “I will stand down.” Whether he is
in the way of successful recruiting appar-
ently doesn’t matter.
The Phewsion must have learned its busi-
ness from Mark Twain. “In statesmanship,”
said Mark, “get the foi’malities right; never
mind about the moralities.”

THE MAN: “Well, as long as you only
spend hours—go ahead.”
NOW OR HEREAFTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The chief difficulty of the Germans, in the event
of their gaining a world-wide victory, would be with
those embers of freedom which would be constantly
liable to break forth in flame.” —English magazine.
Would ye rebel before or after?
Here is the choice that ye all may see!
Where were the harvest of love and laughter
If never a meadow or wood were free?
If these should triumph with flame and thunder
j And waste the homes that ye love so well,
Would ye be silent that black rule under -
And drudge as slaves in a man-made hell?
No, by the beard of a pirate father!
No, by the souls of the outlaw dead!
If their flag flew here ye would turn you rather
To slay and be slain than to eat their bread.
Then were ye rebels to law and order,
Hissing your scorn on their purple pride,
Raiding the farms on their scattered border,
Robbing the ships on their guarded tide.
Now, while the scales of the doom are falling,
Now may the weight of your blow, be known,
Now,- while the guns- guns are calling
And a tempest ,of death is on all winds blown.
Would ye be rebels before or after?
Here is the choice that ye all may make.
Strike; for the harvest of love and laughter
Truth.from the steel of their hope may take!
x.s.w.
GEORGE STREET.

WORMS.
ONE NINE INCHES LONG AND ANOTHER FOUR
INCHES PASSED BY CHILD AFTER TAKING
COMSTOCK’S “DEAD SHOT” WORM PELLETS.
Mrs. A. E. Bade, of 203 McKillop Street,
East Geelong, Vic., writes : —“I have great
pleasure in writing to you to let you know
the satisfactory results I had in using your
‘Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets for my young son,
aged 14 years. He had been ailing for some
time and complained of pain in the spine and
a very sore stomach, and often in the night
would start vomiting without any warning
whatever. He also had several fainting
turns. I became alarmed and took him to
the doctor, but the treatment did him no
good. I then thought I would try your
Worm Tablets, as to me he had every
appearance of worms. I procured a bottle
of them, together with a bottle of Indian
Root Pills, and commenced the treatment.
The second day after taking them he passed
a stomach worm nine inches long, and the
next day one four inches long. Since then
he has picked up wonderfully and is twice
the child he was. If he shows any signs
again I will not hesitate to try them, as I
was so pleased with the results. I am most
grateful to the proprietors of these tablets,
as my husband is away on Active Service,
and the worry of our boy being so ill nearly
drove me mad. Thanking you again.”
STOMACH WORMS.
A MENACE TO CHILDREN.
Stomach worms are undoubtedly the most
invidious kind of all. They inhabit the
stomach and inflict great havoc upon their
victim. They deprive the victim of half the
nutriment he consumes, and when the
stomach is empty gnaw the sides of the
stomach, causing such terrible sensations as
to often cause faintness. Aperient medi-
cines have no effect upon them, for when
any purgative is taken they fasten them-
selves to the walls of the stomach by a
groove running down their side. The best
method of dislodging stomach worms is to
starve the system and take Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets. The worms are
weakened by the want of food, and are
induced to take whatever is taken into the
stomach. Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm
Pellets contain the properties which sicken
and disable the worms, and a dose of opening
medicine will clear them out of the system.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are a purely vegetable medicinal preparation
for the eradication of worms in children and
adults. For sale by all chemists and store-
keepers, price 3s. 6d. per- packet, or will be
sent post paid at the same price by The
W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd., 23 Lang Street,
Church Hill, Sydney; or Farish Street,
Wellington, N.Z.

BAKER v. “TRUTH” NEWSPAPER.
APOLOGY.
'V' HE action brought by Mr. R. D- Ba^ r - 'J h
1 im me is prominent in Sporting Uncles as
“Snowy” Baker, in respect of articles appearing last
rear in Truth, which dealt with the visit
America of the late boxing champion Les Darcy, has
been amicably settled.
AV'e have been afforded by Mr. W. IT. Drew, Mi.
Baker’s Solicitor, a perusal of all Mr. Baker s corres-
pondence and records dealing with the matteis
which were criticised in Truth and some of its
American contributors, and have made exhaustive
independent inquiries in the United States. We are
now convinced that the articles and criticisms ol
which Mr. Baker complains were based upon utterly
misleading information and wholly unmerited, and
that he acted throughout not merely in accordance
with the highest standards of ethics and honor, but
with signal generosity towards the late Champion
Boxer.
W'e, therefore, unreservedly withdraw, and apolo-
gise to Mr. Baker for, the publications complained
of, and agree to pay his legal expenses as between
Solicitor and client.
For and on behalf of the Proprietors of Truth and
Sydney Sportsman, the Executors of the late John
Norton.
11. J. McCLINTOCIv,
General Manager.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The good thing comes off sometimes, and
there was no mistake about the A.J.C. Anniversary
Handicap. When Kctinaqualiair won the January, a
mile and a quarter, with Bst. 21b., and did it in
2min. sJsec., it seemed reasonable to expect that he
would be able to seriously argue the point over
another quarter-mile with 1111). less on top. But
weight and distance were not the only points ot
difference between the two events. In the longer
race the colt had to meet a better-class field than lie
beat on the shorter journey. Among them '' eie
Hitachi, who ran second to Poitrel in Tatt. s Cup,
Mehi King, Ulva’s Isle, the ancient Cisco and Chante-
merle,- though as the last of these arrived on. the
course with a leg that was not much use io him lit
didn’t matter very much. In all probability only
the tact of Dempsey having been brought across
from Melbourne to ride Kennaqualiair kept the colt
at the top of the betting, for there was solid course
backing for Hitachi, whose connections were nughty
confident. At the death—9 to 4 the favorite, 9 to
2 Hitachi, 10 to 1 Mehi King, the rest drifting away
from 12's to 100 to 2 for anyone who wanted a bit
on old Scoutmaster.
It was a race with only one in it. Hitachi, as
usual, slipped into the lead and stayed there 101 in
the journey, with Cliantemerle and Lady Liege, the
favorite making a leisurely trip in seventh place. At.
this stage Dempsey appeared to think the time was
ripe to start jogging along, and, drawing his whip,
persuaded the eolt .to hurry up. Within a furloii,,
he was up with the loaders, and Hitachi was done
with. Shaking off Ghantemcrle and Lady Liege
round the turn, lie landed clear into the stiaight,
and then Dempsey set out for the line. Biero was
nothing to seriously trouble him afterwards, though
had a waiting race been ridden serious trouble would
certainly have been provided by Wedding I)a\. She
came from a long way back, and with Ulva s Isle
was doing best at the finish. Mehi King got into
second place, but was beginning to falter towards
the end. Chantemerle was well up, but without a
punch. The winner stayed it out splendidly, and mil
be heard of again. But lie seems to want a lot ot
riding. * ■ *
With Bst. 71b. aboard, Bonnie Plume, get-
ting advantage of an early break on the field and a
mix-up behind her, ran out an easy winner of the
A.J.C. Challenge Stakes. For the Flying on the
second day she went up to 9st. 01b. and ' l,( ln t get
a break or anything else. The race went to Eulacre,
a useful Linacre mare in Booths stable, who thus
won twice in three starts. Hem, winner of the
Christmas Mile, who 1 had not been sighted _m the
Challenge, was next best, and the Rung Rosedell
was again beaten out of place. Rosedell is an
except ionallv fast animal, but seems cursed with a
great capacity for dropping the festive punter into
the soup. # »
Expectations of a set-to between the hurd-
ling champions Jack Rice and Greek Fire under in-
creased tonnage at Randwiek last week wei e like
unto those of peace. They didn’t come off Cieek
Fire being sore. But Jack Rice had a go with 13st.
41b., and, though not at his best, w-as making the
rest look as cheap as 2d. over the last bit ot the
two miles. Basil K, who has won three times out
of four starts, just staggered in with lOst. 101 b. a
neck ahead of the top weight.
* * *
One of the imported horses running at
Svdney has not won as often as he might if he took
ail interest in the proceedings a little earlier. This
is Rebus, a four-year-old by Radium. He isn t much
to look at, but at the business end of a mile can get
over the ground at a rare pace. The trouble with
him is that he begins as slowly as an elephant.
After winning at Rosehill in March Rebus was spelled
until last month, when he ran unplaced at Canter-
bury Park. At the A.J.C. Anniversary meeting lie
was given two runs, each over a mile, arriving third
and second respectively. Each time he was firmly
backed, persisted in viewing the proceedings from
away behind, and then, suddenly waking up, streaked
for the pole—arid showed everyone that he had com-
menced a fraction of a sec. too late. Perhaps
having been born in the Cold Country he wants to
be warmed up a bit to begin with.
* * * *
After n long sequence of seconds the
Charlemagne colt Woorawa got out of the Maiden
two-year-old class at Randwiek on Saturday by win-
ning the Phillip Stakes with 9st. 41b. from a smart
field in which he was giving away a heap of weight.
Earlier in the season Woorawa was being counted
on as likely to grow into a three-year-old above the
ordinary. He looks good enough still. But the next
thing to find out will be how much good all the
galloping he has had has done him. Having had a
win he ought to be entitled to arrest. *
Sport was so much curtailed in Sydney on
Saturday that there was only a meeting at Randwiek
with seven events and one over the road for ponies
at Kensington with 14 scampers on the programme.
As the seven events were over first there was natu-
rally a rush in the direction of the other seven.
Totalisators —at Randwiek, £19,000; at Kensington
£7OOO. The mania for “curtailing” everything but
Israel seems more rampant than ever.
* * * *
ilesert Cold dropped into Sydney at the
beginning of the week. The lady had a good trip
and was quite well, thank you. After resting a day
or two she goes to Melbourne to get ready for next
month. She was stripped for a walk-about at Rand-
wick on Tuesday, and what the little crowd saw was
a stronglv-quartered mare, of good height,
clean, firm-looking legs. There nothing te.mnme
about this nag. She is very dociie. Davis, Desert
Gold’s trainer, was at one time leading jockei fo

“Harrison O.” : I wish to warn Bulletin
readers, so that the blow will not fall too suddenly,
that they may miss my clever verses and para-
graphs in the future, as I am thinking of abandoning
the Inky Wav for the Primrose Path that awaits the
punter in the only Australian State to which the
totalisator has not penetrated. Ihe other day I
invested £2 in four bets at s.p. in connection with
the Aspendale meeting, backing Marehand both
ways for the first race, and Dhuldul, also win or
place, for the second. Marcliand won at 7 to £ oil
and Dhuldul came second at 3 to 1. As a result ot
Marchand’s victory I separated MacShortodds from
3s 6d., of which sum 9d. represented my dividend
from the place bet. By running into a place
Dhuldul enriched me to the extent of 7s. 6(1., and
by failing to win lost me 10s. So that the net
result of my four wagers—three of which I won —
was to tear from Ike.v Mo the sum of one shilling
sterling, which amount I propose to utilise as the
nucleus of a Fund for the Purchase of Champagne
for Impoverished Bookmakers.
“Cherokee”: “Let’s go round to Paddy
Reynolds’s,” said a returned Billjim of my acquaint-
ance ; and I could have sworn I saw a softening of
his face when I told him that Paddy had gone west.
He used to run the Cathedral Hotel. Melbourne, and
was a great pal of Jack Wren, lorn Bent and other
notabilities. “Bint?” he would say when anyone
disparaged that bluff bale of beef. “I’ve known Bint
sin’ be was a h’v, an’ him as phwhite as the dhrivel-
lin’ snow!” Paddv married one of his barmaids, and
at the wedding breakfast be rose to respond. “Oi
t ank you, gintlemen,” he warbled, in his quaint
falsetto. “It shows phwhat a young woman can do
f’r hersilf in Australia. Lasht week she was a
per gur-rl behind me ba-ar: an’ now she’s til’ wife
iv tlie prisident iv th’ Licensed Victuallers’ Associa-
tion!” (Thunders of applause.) He and Jack Wren
were interested in a horse of some celebrity, Solu-
tion with which thev intended to capture the Flying
Stakes at the 1906 Cup meeting. Paddy wanted
Jack to lav £7OOO to £2OOO on. “ ’Tis pickin’ up
the money 1” said he. Jack wasn’t having any, and
the horse, starting at 3 to 1 on, ran fourth in a
field of eight. Paddy saw black and blue. He lost
no time in finding Solution’s jock, and the question
he put to him has hardly been equalled, even at
Flemington. Outwardly calm, but with his grey
eyes flashing fire, he looked at the miserable rider,
and in a polite squeak, like the preliminary warning
of the break-up of an ice-floe, he asked, “And phwhat
detained ju ?” Besides following the turf, he was
a patron of boxing, and one noted pug, whom he
backed heavily, stayed at “the Cathedral” prior to
his match, it came off at the Richmond racecourse
and ended disastrously. At two the next morning
came a knock at the door of the pub. Paddy put
his grey head out of a first-floor window.
“Who’s dhere?” he asked. “It’s me, Mr. Reynolds
—Fitzbiff.” “An’ who’s Fitzbiff?” “Why, you
know me, Mr. Reynolds—Fitzbiff, the boxer. I’m
staying here.” “You did. You’re not now.’ And
down went the window.
“Jacko” : Someone in a recent Bulletin,
writing of John Skillicorn’s horseback trip from
Bathurst to Sydney in the early days, said that it
was done within 24 hours. The actual time was less
than 20 hours. Here are the facts. In September,
1860, Job Manning, a well-known Bathurst publican,
laid Skillicorn (who was a butcher) £l5O to £5O
that he couldn’t cover the distance on his horse,
Poor Man’s Friend, under 20 hours. Skillicorn set
out amid much excitement from Bathurst at 1 a.m.,
and wasn’t heard of till he had reached Hartley at
9.20. Odds were then freely laid against his success,
but Skillicorn bobbed up at Parramatta before 3 p.m.
and finally landed in Sydney at 8.50 pan., thus hav-
ing 10 minutes to spare. Despite the drizzle, crowds
gathered on the road at the points mentioned.
Skillicorn, who went nearly 12st., reckoned that he
exceeded the distance by 16 miles through taking
the wrong track and having to double back. The
horse stood the test well; Parkes’s paper, Empire,
said the animal looked as though it had come only
from Parramatta. In'the settlement Manning took
the point that Skillicorn had broken the terms by
not riding the whole distance, as he had walked the
prad a distance of 20 yards at Penrith for a drink
before mounting! He won on the protest, but his
pub was boycotted.
Bob Herring, who lias got into khaki,
holds the aggregate runs record (1987) in Melbourne
schools cricket. X. O. Dean (1706) and brother E.
F. Herring (1496) are next on the slate. Bob’s
biggest innings was 238—his contribution to the
schools’ record score (961) put up by Melbourne C.
of E. Grammar :V. Geelong College in 1915. In
that match J. C. Sharp notched the schools’ indivi-
dual innings record of 506. Behind the sticks
Herring is as quick as a cat. •

The Inter-State v. International two-day
cricket match, played on Sydney Cricket Ground
last week in aid of the War Chest, showed how
little interest is left in the game. The “gate”
panned out at £94 Bs. The Inter-Statesmen began
well by knocking up 413 (T. J. E. Andrews, 100;
A. Punch, 88 ; W. Berry, 82 ; A. Ratcliffe, 77 ; and
J. D. Scott, 82). The best the Internationals could
do was 276 (Noble, 106; S. E. Gregory, 58; R. B.
Minnett, 35 ; B. J. Folkard, 22 ; and Iredale, 19.
Burdsley was an early failure with 1). To use up
the balance of Saturday afternoon, the Inter-States-
mep went in again and added 114 runs to their
lead of 137 (E. L. Waddy, 4'l ; and E. A. Dwyer,
40 not out). Hordern with 6 for 117 did best
bowling for the Internationals; Mailey (4 for 77)
was arch-destroyer for the Inter-State team. The
least-dull minutes were the Xoble-Gregory and
Noble-Minnett partnerships. The dear old English
game (it is a better sedative than bowls) really
belongs to the leisurely age that closed with the first
cannon shot of 1914. ’ The pulse of the 1918 crowd
beats fast and the old-style torpid barracker who
would growl if any batsman took a sporting risk is
extinct. The Noble-Gregory interlude was a fine
exhibition of carpet driving and elegant cutting,
with the scoring brisk as a careful partnership
could make it. But the turn that roused the
crowd was Minnett’s livelier 35, made mostly by
ruluting out and lifting ’em. If the game is to
live on, someone will have to devise a way ot get-
ting the batsmen to do it often.
In the International-Interstate match in
Sydney wicket-keeper old Jim Kelly gave away only
two byes in a total of 413, and in the second innings
stumped Berry in great style. Kelly was wicket-
keeper in the .’96, *99, *O2 and 05 Austiftliiin XI. s.

Governor Galway, an old soldier who frets
at not being allowed an active part in this war, and
keeps himself fit by playing all kinds of games,
has toid a meeting of Adelaide sportsmen that it is
“absurdly stupid and wicked to sit down and grow
fat, simply because circumstances will not allow a
man to take an active part in the war.”
* * *
V. R. Bradbury, a Methodist chaplain who
has been handed the Military Cross, was at one
time a well-known oarsman in Vic. He stroked
Wesley College eight and afterwards the first win-
ning crew at Queen's College. lie also found a
place in the University crew. Bradbury had been
away from water for some time previous to going
to the war, and this led him into the bad habit
of breaking motor-cycle records along the roads in
the interior of the Speck.
• * * *
H A. Parsons, the Vic. motor crack, fled
up a’section of Wheeler’s Hill (Vic.) the other day
at 61 miles an hour. The stretch of road measures
1158 yards, or a trifle over six-tenths of a mile, and
has a grade varying from 1 in 5 to 1 in 7 over
every inch of the journey. The youngster scattered
J. Booth’s previous record for the same declivity on a
7-li.p. Indian bike shod with Barnet Glass Boomerang
tyres. # *
South Aus. Education Department has a
school-swimming scheme going at full pressure.
Besides following the lines of the Eastern States,
the Department proposes to erect concrete baths
in districts where there are neither swimming en-
closures nor handy creeks.

The intended-to-be-annual swim from
Henley to the Grange, one mile 350 yards from
jettv to jetty along the Adelaide beaches, was spout
bv the over-handicapping which seems bound to be
a feature of any long swim nowadays. The first
batch were all men with from six to eight minutes
start and F. Bradley came home alone. The scratch
man,’ Spencer Major, from Wallaroo, took 28min.
24sec. to cover the distance, and as Bradley did it in
30rain. 38sec., and had a generous six minutes start,
the best man (and those near him) had not much
hope. You don’t find anybody in a fiye-mile race on
land given over a mile start, let alone real good
performers who would be only two minutes behind
the scratch man in a half-hour run.
Some of the amateur athletes of \ ictoria
are kicking against the controlling body because it
refuses to allow war eligibles to compete at meetings
or in events sanctioned by the Council unless they
show a passable excuse for not taking a sprint to
the trenches. Grumblings from the kickers have
been heard since St. Patrick's sports a year ago, unu
as St. Pat’s have another celebration due in March
the clash has commenced again. Sparks were begin-
ning to fly last week, and there was wild talk about
forming an amateur athletic union—a scheme ot
neither use nor ornament to the amateur, since it
would debar him from competing in Inter-State anil
Australasian championships, as well as the \ic.
championships. But it would seem a fair thing toi
the Vie. Association to come down the ladder a pit
and submit the question to the Australasian Jmon
(which is principally Dick Coombes, of Sydney), an
get at least a neutral award. The Associations 1
X.S.W. and Queensland, by the way, have not cealt
out a similar stringent regulation.

when to take risks, banks on his
superior nerve to pull him through.
And if by daily habit and by simple
precautions he has kept his body nt
and vigorous always, you may be sure
his nerve will not fail him in any crisis.
Such men succeed where others dare
not even try to succeed.

ful of Kruschen Salts
in a tumbler of hot
water every morning
before rising clears the
head, invigorates and
cleanses the system

THE thing that the people should have
been allowed to realise from the outset
is that WE MAY GET A LICKING. We
still may get a licking. Are you going to
let the Ravisher of JJelyiur *» win without
lifting a hand?
A Warrak (Vic.) pub, The Live and Let
Live, has been delicensed. What s in a
name, after that?
Talk about rotten luck! W. Bahl. a \ ic-
torian loco.-driver, after spending 40 years
at his whizzing job without getting so much
as a scratch, was inveigled lately into a
motor spin. He had his doubts about it;
but he stuffed them away, settled himself
down comfortably—and then found liimselt
in bed nursing a few broken ribs and other
injuries which have since driven him to
resign.
Ormonde Birch, chaplain in the Indian
Army, who got the M.C. in Mesopotamia
lately after being twice mentioned in
despatches, is a Tasmanian, and took a hand
with a contingent from the Speck in the
S’African scrimmage 18, years ago. For
some time he was an Anglican parson, but
got a chaplaincy in India before the war.
He went to Mesopotamia with the first
Indian expeditionary force, and has been
there ever since.
Ivyabram, where the Reform fowl that
laid the still-born eggs came from, has been
making a better name for itself lately. Its
Methody parson has two sons, Capt. J. S.
and Captain J. 1). Rogers. Both have risen ,
from private, and both hold the Military
Cross. J.D. used to pull a good oar in Mel-
bourne Uni. boat, and was Ormond College's
fourth M.C. He has been in the trouble
since the Landing. J.S. was a science
teacher at Ivyneton High School, and has
not been off duty since he enlisted.
Eric T. Roberts, formerly of the,Austra-
lian Flying Corps in Palestine, was; changed
at Hawthorn (Vic.) with whizzing too fast
on a motor-cycle. He played his hand thus:
First card, he was not driving at 35 miles
an hour, as alleged ; second, his speed was
25 miles; third, he was a good judge of such
matters; fourth, as an aviator he' could
naturally control a motor-cycle better than
a groundling could do it; and fifth and
highest trump—he was the first Australian
aviator to fly over Jerusalem. The Bench
threw in its cards.
“O.”: The home-made motor-car built by
the late Geoff. Wall, author of “Songs of an
Airman,” was made out of four kerosene-
tins, six kerosene-cases and various odds and
ends of machinery. But the young Aus-
tralian made it go. His partner in the ven-
ture was a Wesley College chum, Harry
Rigby, now of the It.F.C. The pair actu-
ally motored from Malvern to Mount Martha
(about 35 miles), and felt that fame was
theirs when a pompous Hop bailed them up
for their license. , Last yeay they met in
France, and it was Harry Rigby who took
Geoff for his first flight.
Old John Morley, in the House of Com-
mons for 25 years, and Secretary of State in
turn for Ireland and for India, in his new
book of recollections gives away the whole
show of Party politics :
Much of Parliamentary debate is dispute between
men who in truth and at bottom agree, but invent
arguments to disguise agreement and contrive a
difference. It is artificial, but serves a purpose in
justifying two lobbies and a party idivision.... I am
sometimes amazed, and a trifle horrified, when I
contrast the loose, free-and-easy way in which poli-
ticians form their judgments, with the strict stan-
dards observed by every conscientious critic or his-
torian. So little evidence goes such a long way
when once your mind is made up.
Dookie (Vic.) Agricultural College is
hoeing its row very well. Over 300 students
have enlisted, and 46 have been killed. Be-
sides sending old boys to the Front, it takes
new ones when they return, and Lieut. L.
Searrott, who went from the trenches to the
furrows, speaks highly of the training.
Among, star pupils are (or were) Lance-
Corporal F. G. Hamilton, a Port Mel-
bourne boy who won the M.M. at
Bulleeourt and a Bar later on; and
Lance-Cofporal P. L. Nihill, who has
both-H>.CyAI• and M.M., and has been three
times iiVdespatches; Nihill was one of three
who rushed 30 iiuus and harvested them.
Then there is Capt W. J. T. Guilfoyle, M.C.,
of the R.F.C., son :of Botanic Gardens
Guilfoyle. He has brooded over Jerusalem,
outed "a Fokker in Egypt and rescued a
stranded airman whom he found in the
desert. Others are Capt. J. A. Lambden,
M.C;; one of four brothers on service, and
a plucky trench raider; M. F. Cockburn,
MM.; Trooper H. F. Pillow, M.M., of the
Light Horse; and L.-Cpl. R. C. G. Greig,
M.M., of the machine-gunners, killed in
action a few months ago. Greig’s brother
went west as a lieutenant in Gallipoli.
“Bauragi”: “John McL.’s” yarn (B.
3/(p318) about Papuan churches recallsjone
of winch I was senior-warden, architect,
master-builder, verger and general custodian.
That was on the Mambare, when Le Hurt to
was “Garbament big fella.” The Anglicans
had there two white missionaries, a hospital,
two white nurses and a string of South ,f)ea
sundries. Something special was called ;p>r.
and a stately tabernacle arose with walls of
split nipa and a real iron roof. There was
also a chancel with a raised floor and an
altar with reredos complete, made chiefly of
whisky-cases from Billy Hancock’s store.
There was also a belfrey outside with a
ship’s bell hung thereon. It was the only
chapel in a Government compound in Papua,
and the moral tone of the field rose notice-
ably. Soon afterwards the mission shifted
further south, and the house of prayer was
forgotten. Then one evening the old Bulldog
came up the river with the Bishop himself on
board. In spite of my attempts at dissuasion
lie insisted on inspecting the church at once, as
he had to start away early next morning. We
went down through the inky night, escorted
by two police boys carrying lanterns. As
we reached the door his Gaiters tripped over
the belfrey, which had fallen across the
porch. Uttering a benediction, he fell
through the front wall of the building, and
when I followed to the rescue I was felled
anu the pair of us were trodden into the mud
by a flight of evil-smelling fiends who pursued
the terrified police into the blackness with
dreadful noises. I explained to the Bishop
that an" unregenerate henchman must have
been using the sacred edifice as a shelter for
my herd of goats. He took the explanation
very badly, and the Mission put me in the
same class as my live stock. That was
umpteen years ago, and when I bowed to
the Bishop—now an ex-bishop—the other
day his acknowledgment was still frosty.

Announcement.
, The Goodyear Tyre and Rubber Co. of Australasia Limited are
pleased to intimate that arrangements have been concluded with that widely-
known Australian Company —Messrs. Elder Smith and Company Limited -
whereby the latter, on and after the ist February, 1918, will be the sole
distributors of Goodyear Mechanical Rubber Goods throughout Australia.
* "The whole of the large and varied stocks of Belting, Hose, Packing,
Valves and other Mechanical necessities held by Goodyear in Brisbane, Sydney,
Melbourne and Adelaide are being transferred to the stores of Messrs. Elder Smith
and Company Limited in those cities, from which immediate delivery can be given
of practically every mechanical rubber equipment necessary in Australian industry.
In addition to large shipments already on the way to Australia, further
extensive orders have been placed with the factory for all Mechanical
Goods, so that ample supplies will be arriving regularly to replace sales and
augment reserves.
The Mechanical Experts on the staff of Goodyear in Australia have
joined the sales force of Messrs. Elder Smith and Company Limited, and their
advice will, as heretofore, always be available on all matters connected with Mill,
Mine, Factory and Power Plouse equipment.
Before concluding this announcement we desire to assure present and
prospective users of Goodyear Mechanial Goods that the high quality standard
of these productions will be inevitably maintained —and probably enhanced by
ceaseless research and experiment.
Goodyear Mechanical Goods may cost more, but they are invariably
worth more.
The same selling prices which have been in operation for a considerable
period will continue until such time as it may be found imperative to make
alterations on account of market fluctuations.
' If through increased cost of raw materials it is ever necessary to raise
prices we will certainly do so rather than lower, by even the smallest fraction,
the Goodyear quality standard now 7 established and acknowledged throughout
the world.
ME GOODYEAR TYRE and RUBBER COMPANY OF AUSTRALASIA LIMITED.
ead Office: 231 Clarence Street, Sydney, N.S.W. ; and at Brisbane, fleibourne, Adelaide, Perth & Hobart.

There was a time when the then mostly-
imported person didn’t reckon this country
could produce even its own lawyers. A Syd-
ney politician’s diary of October, 1840. con-
tains this entry:—
George Robert Nichols, the Colonial Attorney,
has been petitioning the Council to introduce a
clause into the Supreme Court Bill authorising the
Judges to make rules for the admission to the Bar
of Colonial youths; but the proposition has been
knocked on the head.
Matthew -uiry Stephen, the first “colonial
youth” admitted after the rash experiment
was made, is still living.
Thus the Government’s imported stipen-
diary weevil-killer, Professor Lefroy :—
It was even feared that a large portion of the
French army would have to be withdrawn to culti-
vate land for wheat. Every bushel of wheat that
Australia could grow would ultimately have a very
high value. .. . Just what the value would be at
the end of the war no one could say, but it would
be sound policy for Australia to accumulate the
greatest possible quantity of wheat.
Quite so —provided the Government can
borrow the gigantic sums required to enable
it to buy up and store the wheat, and pro-
vided the tear ends the right way. But if it
ends the wrong way the learned bug-smasher
should note that Germany will probably get
this country, and take the wheat and the
bugs for nothing. It may even soundly flog
the farmer for not keeping the wheat clean
till Fritz could come and steal it.
If Conscription is a political matter, then
they lie who tell the public that there is no
political censorship: for returned men are
not allowed to say in print whv thev voted
“No.”

STRENGTH —AND HOW TO GAIN IT.
INSTEAD OF EXERCISES, PATENT
FOODS AND MEDICINES, TAKE
PHOSPHATE WITH YOUR MEALS.
The demand to day is for men and women
who are strong in every sense of the word —
possessing the physical strength necessary
to endure hardships and fatigue; the mental
strength to grapple with difficult problems ;
the nervous force which endows the body
with vigor and vitality; the will-power to
triumph over adversity and turn defeat into
victory. But such glorious strength is
impossible so long as your nerves are weak
and exhausted, and therefore if you would be
really strong you must first care for your
nerves. Weak, exhausted nerves need food,
and it has been proved in numberless cases
that the only food element they can or will
absorb readily and naturally is phosphate in
the well-known form of bitro-phosphate.
which most chemists stock in 5gr. compressed
tablets, so that if you feel your strength is
failing from any cause, you > should get a
supply of these bitro-phosphate tablets and
take one with every meal. Practically all
of the minor ailments afflicting mankind, as
well as many of the more serious maladies,
can be traced to nervous exhaustion and
lowered vitality, and probably this explains
why such a remarkable improvement in tin-
general health is invariably noticeable when
bitro-phosphate is taken as directed, and the
nerves are thereby revitalised and made
strong.—Advt.

“C. Ockney”: The London Punch con-
tributor widely known as “F. Anstey” may,
as “F.N.T.” affirms (B. 24/l/’18), be a
cousin of Frank Anstey of the Reps. But
the “F. Anstey” of the books and plays
happens to be a pen-name. His other name
is Guthrie —F. Anstey Guthrie.
This appeared on the programme of a
patriotic concert at a N.S.W. Northern
Rivers town last week: “God savee the
King.” We piously hope so.
“B. J.” ; A decorative Australian who has
made a good start in the U.S.A. is Margaret
Linden. Here she was in the chorus of the
CHARLIE CHAP-
LIN, as he looks
when he isn’t earn-
ing his living.
Clarke and Mey-
nell comic opera
co. and scored an
ornamental success
as a postillion
attendant upon
Marquis Tom
Walls in “The
Belle of Brittany,”
incidentally pulling
off an ad. during
the Melbourne run
of the piece by
fainting one night
in the centre of the
stage. Upon arriv-
ing in the Wilson
republic Margaret
stepped almost at
once into a part in
“Anthony in Won-
derland,” on tour,
and was booked to
play the same
character in
N’Yark at the beginning of this year.
“8.J.” : George Treloar, who was playing
with Oscar Asche in London when he en-
listed with the Artists’ Rifles f subsequently
he was transferred to the Coldstream
Guards), has both the D.S.O. and the Mili-
tary Cross. George was a St. Pat.’s Col-
lege boy—he came from Ballarat and
acted with Julius Knight and later with
the Bailey-Grant co. He was playing the
younger Carey in “On Our Selection” when
he received an offer from Asche. Now he
is Captain Treloar.
This is a troublesome world for the Musi-
cal Soul. While he was rhapsodising
amongst the high C’s and drinking sonatas
with the gods, the time for furnishing com-
mon, earthy income-tax returns expired
like the Lost Chord. Then the Soul of
Albert Goossens, Belgian singer-, had to be
hauled down and fined £5 with 6s. costs m
Sydney Water Police Court. The outrage
happened last week.
“The Exile”: Londoners assume in their
patronising way that Australians are theat-
rically starved, and it was with much joy
that I was able to casually remark to very
prominent first-nighters in the Big Smoke
that “High Jinks”
—a current craze —
bubbled in Sydney
long before it
achieved its Lou-
don success. Few
Londoners know
that people like
Oscar Asche,
Madge Titheradge,
Rosina Buckman,
and the more re-
cent arrivals Fred
Leslie and Ivy
Shilling learnt
their trade “down
under.” About six
months ago, by the
way, I saw Sey-
mour Hicks trying
to be an American
in “Broadway
Jones”; but I had
had the delight of
hearing Niblo and
Hamilton in Aus-
tralia, and the com-
"The Lifeguardsman,”
at Melbourne
Princess’s.
The Plotting Prime
Minister
(H. W. Varna)
parison was not
nice. About one theatre-goer in 10 thousand
over here has ever heard of Niblo or Hamil-
ton.
“William Devine”: The Pointer who sup-
plied the original cast of “The Silver King
at Melbourne Theatre Royal was wrong in
one particular. He gave the iname or
Blanche Stammers as playing the part of
Olive Skinner, the Spider’s wife. \ oo j
Blanche (Mrs. Arthur Garner) was dead
and buried before the production. The part
was played by Jenny Watt Tanner, a re-
markably fine actress with a striking per-
sonality. Jenny was a well-known figure
on the Australian stage in the late ’7o’s and
’Bo’s, graduating from the ballet at the
Theatre Royal under Harwood, Stewart,
Hennings and Coppin. She became leading
lady to George Rignold in ’7B-9, her two
most notable successes being Chorus in
“Henry V.” and Lady Clancarty in Tom
Taylor’s “Clancarty,” while with William-
son, Garner and Musgrove she created the
part of Mrs. Beresford in the first perform-
ance of the delightful comedy, “Impulse.”
She was Mrs. Posket in the original Aus-
tralian production of “The Magistrate.”
Afterwards she was with Brough and Bouci-
cault, and was the Original wicked Countess
in “A Village Priest.” By this time she had
become somewhat mature, and was in great
request for “grand dame” parts. Later, she
dropped out of sight, and was understood to
have gone to London to push the fortunes of
a daughter who had displayed some signs of
talent. Two or three years ago she was
playing Mrs. Braey’s old part in a London
production of “The Geisha.”
“Arjacee”: Re “Nosey’s” wail (B.
17/1/T8) about the poor manager and the
sordid musician. To make ends meet quite
a lot of first-class artists have to endure a
day’s ill-paid teaching drudgery, and finish
up at the theatre in the vicinity of midnight.
Some of them have family responsibilities.
(My own niob are responsible for 20-odd.)
Four of my lot in pre-war times invested
in homes. Four years.ago the Musicians’
award said £3 per week. It is still saying
so. Any man who undertook responsibilities
before the war and hasn’t had his pay in-
creased is doing some hard thinking nowa-
days. Breakages often reduce the earnings.
A case within my knowledge cost the unfor-
tunate in one week 50s.' for new drum heads.
This man was an artist on his instrument,
too. (Yes, there are artistic drummers.)
String instruments suffer worse. This is
especially the case at waterside picture-
houses, where recently one basso had, after
paying fares, etc., a whole ss. to hand his
missus for his week’s work. The weird
noises complained of by “Nosey” are more
often than not introduced at the request of
alleged managers. Only a few weeks since
one of these asses bobbed his head over the
rail and suggested, “Play some Manly boat
stuff; there’s some people from there in the
house to-night, and they’d like it.”
“Jwosp”: Don’t call Guy Bates Post a
“new” American actor. He’s a renewal,
rather. Starting with Brown Potter and
Bellew in 1895, he got to New York in 1901.
After being first support for a few years
he reached the leading role in “The Heir of
the Hoorah” in 1905, was principal man to
Mrs. Fiske in 1907-8, and in 1909 became a
star in “The Bridge” and scored heavily
with the public straight away. For a few
years he fell off; but he seems to be
scintillating brightly again in “The Mas-
querader.”
One of Guy Bates Post's biggest Yankee
successes was scored in a dramatic version
of Omar Khayyam ; but there is no hint of
Australia seeing this.
The burning of the Town Hall at
Wallaroo (S.A.) has brought up again the
question of the picture-show run by that go-
ahead mining town. Private Enterprise in
the picture business has made all sorts of
handsome offers to the Municipal Corpora-
tion ; but the answer is just that other
arrangements have been made. The last
meeting of the Council had to consider a
letter from an Adelaide lawyer, giving the
opinion that running pictures is no part of
a Corporation’s functions, and threatening
proceedings on behalf of a rival showman.
But the letter was only “formally received.”
“Peter”: The amount of daring credited
to little Fayette Perry, who is one of the
minor features of “Aladdin” in Meloourne,
would make the late air-crack, Guynemer,
seem like an organiser for the Curates’
Union. According to communiques issued
from the King’s Theatre, Fayette has already
defied Death in balloons and aeroplanes and
once dived into a fire-brigade net from a sky-
scraper window. Also, she was once sus-
pended on a wire as a fairy in a panto,
when, suddenly losing her means of support,
she got a reserved seat in a stout man’s lap.
Fayette’s proximity to fat men at critical
moments is rather remarkable, by the way.
Iler modest biographer mentions that she
recently grabbed a capitalist by the scruff of
the ear and lifted him out of the way of a
tram. Tiny Fayette, who can make the
scale shudder up to 7st. aud a bit over, is
evidently a reckless character who is con-
tinuall- encountering dangers that are not
her size. One of these days we shall get the
truth about her, and it will be something
anent her falling into the inkwell through
sliding down the pen.
Bryceson Treharne, piano-teacher at the
Adelaide Couservatorium and founder of the
Adelaide Literary Theatre, has emerged as
a song composer of the first rank. It was
all due to the Hun. Treharne was at Berlin
when the war came, and for 18 months
resided at Ituhleben Prison Camp. There he
composed 200 songs, several orchestral pieces
and the score of a Japanese opera. War
The Strollers , at
Sydney Playhouse.
Madeline Rossiter as
a member of a bur-
lesque minstrel troupe.
bread containing
chopped straw and
sand naturally in-
spired him. (The
straw was added
for bulk, and the
sand was necessary
for weight—where-
by the Hun ingeni-
ously complied with
the requirements of
international law.)
Treharne remarks:
“One becomes very
active mentally on
a limited diet. It
really seems to act
as a spur; one’s
head becomes clear,
and the amount of
mental labor which
can be performed
under, such condi-
tions is quite sur-
prising.” How-
ever, 18 months of
it knocked Tre-
harne out, and he
was exchanged and
allowed to take his
script with him.
On his recovery he
went to Boston.
U.S.A. Musical
America promptly
boomed him as “one of the most
individual voices among contemporary song-
composers” ; and the Boston Music Co. has
published 21 of his “art songs.”

Ilis. name was John Rogers, but he added
the Greville so as not to be confounded
with the other comedian. The Land Boom
broke Harwood and Stewart, equally saving

crest of the wave.

AN INVITATION TO BE MUSHY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It asks big questions: it tells vital truths. Every
adult should see it. A story that stains your cheeks
with tears. —Sob film advt.
Come Hearts of Oak, and Hearts of Stone, and
Hearts of Plain Boiled Tripe!
Ho! get your little hankies out! Unfold! Pre-
pare to wipe!
However dean you’ve kept your face, we’re going
to stain it now !
We’re going to make you think and weep, and
make a dismal row.
You’ve got to snivel with the crowd —to snivel
and to pay—
And choke in stuffy darkness in the proper picture
way.
You never heard a vital truth or had a soul, you
know,
Until we came along with our infernal picture
show ! ■
You never ,realised (Shut up 4) the things you ought
to do!
We taught t .you how to think and weep, we learned
you how to chew!
In intellectual darkness you’ve remained for years
and years ;
But now we’re right here with our show to stain
your cheeks with tears.
We’re out, kid, to improve your mind, and make
you think and sob,
And, maybe, pull your leg a bit. But that’s an
easy job !

CYRUS SEEHERE (U.S.A.)
THE FAIR DECEPTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Melbourne, as a friend asserts,
Rill saw a merry play,
Where dairy-maids in three-inch skirts,
With hearts for ever gay,
Danced in and out the whole day Lng,
And burst repeatedly in song.
One of these girls appealed to Bill.
He liked her figure neat,
And so he spread himself to kill,
And soon was at her fret.
Bill asked her did she think she could.
The girl replied she thought she wo ' '
Bill’s cocky dad at Yam was told
About the wedding day.
Said he: “I bet that boy’s bin sold.
I heard a feller say
That offen dairymaids in plays
Don’t milk a blessed cow for days!”
VICTORIA.

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
Why Have Grey Hair?
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes. A
very old, home-made remedy will turn the
hair back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
two ounces of concentrate of tammalite and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this simple lotion to the hair for a few nights
with a small sponge and you will soon have
the pleasure of seeing the greyness disappear.
This recipe is perfectly harmless, is neither
sticky nor greasy and has given perfect satis-
faction for many generations to those in
possession of the secret.
Blackheads, Oily Pores, etc.
• The new sparkling face-bath treatment rids
the skin of blackheads, oiliness and enlarged
pores almost instantly. It is perfectly harm-
less, pleasant and immediately effective. All
you have to do is to drop a stymol tablet,
obtained from the chemist, in a glass of hot
water, and after the resulting effervescence
has subsided dab the affected portions of the
face freely with the liquid. When you dry
the face you will find that the blackheads
come right off on the towel, the large pores
contract and efface themselves and the greasi-
ness is all gone, leaving the skin smooth, soft
and cool. This treatment should be repeated
a few times at intervals of several days in
order to make sure that the result shall be
permanent.
The Magnetism of Beautiful Hair.
Beautiful hair adds immensely to the per-
sonal magnetism of both men and women.
Actresses and smart women are ever on the
look out for any harmless thing that will in-
ci’ease the natural beauty of their hair. The
latest method is to use pure stallax as a sham-
poo on account of the peculiar glossy, fluffy
and wavy effect which it leaves. As stallax
has never been used much for this purpose it
comes to the chemist only in Jib. sealed origi-
nal packages, enough for 25 or 30 shampoos.
A teaspoonful of the fragrant stallax gran-
ules, dissolved in a cup of hot water, is more
than sufficient for each shampoo. It is very
beneficial and stimulating to the hair apart
from its beautifying effect.
How to Remove a Bad Complexion.
Cosmetics can never really help a poor com-
plexion; often they are positively harmful.
The sensible, rational way is to actually re-
move the thin veil of stifling, half-dead scarf-
skin from the face, and give the fresh, vigor-
ous and beautiful young skin underneath a
chance to show itself and to breathe.
This is best done in a very simple way, by
merely applying inercolised wax at night, like
cold cream, and washing it off in the morning.
Good mercolized wax can be obtained from
any well-stocked chemist. It absorbs the dis-
figuring cuticle gradually and harmlessly,
leaving a brilliant natural complexion. Of
course, this also takes with it all such facial
blemises as red blotches, tan, moth patches,
sallowness, liver spots, pimples, etc. As a
freckle remover and general complexion beau-
tifler this old-fashioned remedy is unequalled.
The Powder Puff Passes.
Every normal woman is always interested
in any plan by which she can quickly enhance
her beauty, provided the plan is perfectly safe
and simple. Ever since the discovery that
ordinary cleminite, such as any chemist can
supply, is an instantaneous and harmless
beautifler, there has been a growing demand
for it from smart women everywhere. They
usually get about an ounce and add just
enough water to dissolve it. A little of this
perfectly harmless lotion gives a beautiful
complexion to any face instantly. It renders
face powder quite unnecessary. Moreover, its
use cannot be detected. The skin is instantly
beautified but appears perfectly natural
under the closest scrutiny. [3

It isn’t only the lads who have a fancy
for the Flying Corps. A recently-accepted
candidate was John Henry McLochlan, and
he served in the S’African war under Gen-
eral Munro, the man who planned and con-
ducted the mysterious Evacuation from
Gallipoli.
“Murray” : There’s a pretty fair collection
of old “tokens” in the Albury Museum, of
all places in the world. There are penny
and half-penny bits issued by business houses
in Melbourne, Sydney, Ballarat, Goulburn,
Hobart and elsewhere and in divers towns
in Maoriland. The habit of issuing tokens
was kept up late in M.L.; there’s one issued
as recently as 1874 by an Auckland pub.
“Araminta”: Another thing Australia is
able to make better and cheaper than the
imported blessing; and that’s sand-soap.
Most of the overseas soap seems „o be made
on the principle that the sooner each block
is used up the better for trade. The Aus-
tralian-built product is hard and lasts twice
as long as the crumbly imported stuff, while
having all its advantages. Yet there is hardly
a grocer who will supply the local goods
unless they are specially asked for.
The 12th Battalion has been keeping the
Speck’s end up. At a dinner in France on
October 20 to celebrate the third anniver-
sary of the day on which the battalion sailed
from Hobart the following list of honors
won by members was reeled off: Two V.C.’s
(Captain J. E. Newland and Sergeant J. W.
Whittle); one C. 8.; one C.M.G.; four
D.S.O.’s (one of which belongs to the com-
manding officer, Lieut.-Col. C. H. Elliot, the
first Australian to get that award in this
rumpus—he has a bar to it now) ; 13 Mili-
tary Crosses; 14 D.C.M.’s; 98 Military
Medals; one Croix de Guerre; one Cross of
Karageorge (Serbian) and a Serbian silver
medal.
J. M. Lamborne, just appointed boss of
Sydney fire-brigades, started life as a com-
positor in Northern Vic.; he passed out of
the Barrier Miner office 20 years ago to
take charge of the Broken Hill brigades,
and has had that job ever since. A fine
organiser, with the knack of handling men,
he would probably have been out of Broken
Hill long ago if he had known less about the
special business of dealing with mine fires —a
more ticklish job than handling an outbreak
in a 10-storey building because of the deadly
gases that smouldering timber generates
underground. There was one fire in the
Broken Hill Prop, that beat off every ad-
vance upon it and lasted for ' close-on 20
years.
George Meudell’s yarn :
The utterly uncalled-for misuse of the Honorable
Randolph Bedford's name by the Warwick (Q.) bell-
man recalls an incident of one of my many attempts
to serve my country for three hundred a year and a
golden railway pass. At Queenscliff (Vic.) I tackled
the late Jonas Felix Levien, who had been re-elected
without a contest for 28 years. The local campanolo-
gist was a fisherman by profession, and carried on
campanology as a sideline. For half a crown an
hour this is how he announced my first election
meeting in the Town Hall: “To-night, to-night, at
hate o’clock, the right honnerible Mr. Bluebell will
address the helectors, and to-morrow mornin’ at nine
I’ll be round with fresh barrowcooter.” I’ve since
heard Swiss goatherds yodelling to their flocks, but
the Queenscliff bellringer excelled them with his
long-drawn-out “cooter.”
“Bad Australian” ; Passing the toniest fur-
rier’s shop in Buenos Aires 1 saw a muff and
collarette which reminded me of old bare-
footed days when I shooed cockatoos and
crows off the wheat and chased the nimble
Opossum out of the corn patch. I went in
and asked the price of the set, which was
made of seven silver-greys—not the big,
black-pointed silver-grey of the blue-gum and
messmate forests; just the ordinary little
cuss of the white box and apple-tree country.
The effusively-polite son of Old Castile behind
the counter informed me that he was sacri-
ficing that line of chinchilla at $l2O (about
10 guineas) ; and when I pointed out that I
had pursued the chinchilla in his native
haunts in Bolivia and Upper Peru, and that
these furs didn’t seem to belong to those
wily beasts, he explained that these were not
that kind of chinchilla, but Chinchillas Aus-
tralianos, which, although not quite so fine
as the Chinchilla Sud Americano, was about
20,000 per cent, more durable; that, as a
matter of fact, you simply couldn’t wear the
nap off the tough-fibred Australiano. Then
I thought of the thousands of Chinchillas
Australianos I had recklessly wasted in
those old days, and it occurred to me that if
I had only put those pelts in cold storage or
safe deposit, instead of trading them off to
Sam Lee, the Pong hawker, for 2s. 6d. per
dozen, I would now be known and respected
as the Chinchilla King.
“Jacko”: The present training of such
special units as the A.S.C. in the Citizen
Forces is a painful :nd expensive farce.
During the past few months the members of
the absurdly-inadequate eight companies
that are sprinkled over N. S. Wales have
been dragged into Liverpool camp for eight
days at a time. But in actual fact the
training period has been reduced to about
five days—three are spent in getting fixed up,
a Sunday is lost, and the breaking-up day is
negligible. Even the ridiculously brief time
is not employed in real training. The
trainees, who should be instructed in the
technique of the game (and Heaven knows
there’s enough of it to learn!) are given the
highly-specialised job of carting wood to the
circular-saw that the Department has in-
stalled at Liverpool, after discovering that
thousands of pounds had been paid away
unnecessarily for sawn fuel. As a relief
from this monotony, these specialist trainees
are put on to cleaning stables. The nearest
they ever get to A.S.C. work is carting sup-
plies from the station to the camp and thence
to “Germany,” otherwise the Holdsworthy
Concentration Camp. The wretched sham
costs about £5 per head. We still have uni-
versal service for home defence. A blind
man can, however, see that an attack upon
it is coming; and if it is turned down, to
the awful peril of Australia, it will be
because thousands of busy men, like myself,
are convinced the present scheme gives hardly
any value in training for a great deal of
money.

The Inky Way.
“Copy Boy”: A writer who left a news-
paper in which he had for some years run a
column under a pen-name, recently obtained
an injunction in the Philadelphia Court of
Common Pleas to restrain the rag from con-
tinuing to publish stuff under the nom-de-
plume. Seems to me it might be well worth
the while of an inky-wayfarer who has won
a reputation pseudonymously to register his
brand under the Trade Marks Act. In Aus-
tralian journalism it is customary for news-
papers to appropriate pen-names which have
acquired a reputation; writers come and go,
but the same old pen-name goes on for ever.
A weekly topical column still appears in
Melbourne over a nom-de-plume that was
used by Marcus Clarke and his half-dozen
successors. “Oriel” in Ihe Saturday Argus
has covered a score of identities, and for a
good many years the column has loafed on
the reputation given to it by Johnny Sandes.
Died at Sydney, Mrs. Josephine Bennet,
relict of B. J. Bennet, aforetime proprietor
of the Burrangong (N.S.W.) Argus. She
was born in Dublin in 1840, and came to
Australia in 1562. That Henry Flood who
was so prominent a member of "the old Irish
Parliament before the Union (1801) was her
grandfather; and her father was Chief Clerk
of the Court of Chancery in Dublin.
The ARGUS was in a frivolous mood on
the 14th inst. when she printed, over an item
about the meat-shortage in England, “The
Meat Pinch” ; and, underneath that, “Mayor
Seizes Mutton.”
* * * *
Eighty this month, Robert Chaffer, of
Adelaide, who came from London to Mel-
bourne in 1852 and put in nearly 30 years
as Argus printer. The family meant to go
to Sydney, but all the sailors deserted in
Melbourne to pick up nuggets, and the ship
Deborah became a prison-hulk. Since 1882
Chaffer has been a S. Australian ; he built
up the Modern Printing Co., now run by his
son.
* * * *
Fred Cutlack, who has been appointed
assistant war-correspondent under Bean, is
only 3L He was a Renmark boy with a
literary father, and took various honors,
chiefly literary, during his education in Adel-
aide.' Then he went on to the Register, as
secretary to editor Sowden, but presently
took a fancy to try his luck in London. The
luck was various, and he spent much of his
time in reading for the Bar. He enlisted
early, and has been wounded twice. Some
of his articles have been widely published
already, for he was allowed to come out on
the Australia on her first voyage and write
an account of it.
* * * *
“75”: Some time ago, in connection with
the Hughes-Watson libel action, it was
announced in the Melbourne press that the
matter had been settled and that Hughes
had paid the costs of both sides. A para-
graph in London Sunday Siironicle, to
which Hughes recently contributed some
windy articles, reads: “Mr. Watson has
withdrawn the charges, and will pay the
costs of both sides.” Anybody know who is
the Chronicle’s xYustralian correspondent?
Perth Press Club was hesitatingly allowed
to go into voluntary liquidation the other
day. The sworn declaration of the officials
that it was unable to pay its debts passed
muster all right; but what puzzled Justice
Burnside was how a club with a wine, beer
and spirit license came to be incorporated
under an Act which was only intended for
chapels, churches, pious bodies and associa-
tions which do not have a bar or sell booze
and never want to go . into liquidation.
Then counsel stated that some of those who
originally formed the club had religious
leanings and quoted Scripture between
drinks, and his Honor granted the applica-
tion.
* * * *
About a friend of the family:—
He nodded pleasantly as he came abreast, and 1
invited him to beer and a sandwich. He waxed
poetical on the view, and reeled off yards of Words-
worth. I was interested. Had he seen better
days? He sighed and admitted it. The drink was
his trouble. He had disgraced his family and was
sent out to Melbourne, where he took a job
as dramatic and literary critic for the Argus to
augment his “miserable pittance.” (He hotly denied
having ever written any of its leaders.) The" respect-
able journal could not stand his constant backslidings
and passed him out. He referred to his own “little
things” for The Bulletin, and produced clip-
pings of some of the most delightful verse of Mabel
Forrest. I looked the query, and he modestly ad-
mitted to being “M, Forrest, of The Bulletin.”
Then I brained him with the empty beer bottle and
shoved his carcase in a hollow log.
THE LITTLE WOODEN BELL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the weary day is over and the high white
stars look down
On the maze of lights and shadows that compose the
sleeping town,
When the pen can go no further and the hot eyes
seem to swell,
Comes the sweet and cheerful chiming of the Little
Wooden Bell.
“Time to douse the light and chuck it!” sings the
Little Wooden Bell.
There are vesper bells for Christians, there are
temple bells for lnd,
Sending out their soothing message on the soft and
scented wind ;
But for me there is a summons of a less pretentious
type—•
Just the hurried clink-clonk-clitter of my neighbor’s
final pipe,
And it tells its message clearly does that little
tapping pipe.
“Praise your God for good tobacco that will see you
through the day,
Till it’s time to spill the ashes and put your pipe
away.
It is sleep and good tobacco that makes Heaven out
of Hell—
Good-night, all; see you to-morrow,” says the Little
Wooden Bell,
Says the singing and the ringing of the Little
Wooden Bell.
N.S.W.
