When the Eels Gome Down
the River.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DREAM pools and whimpering willows,
and sweet beside the bridge
The prattling of the rapids over
many a shallow ridge,
Bright Avators gaily swinging down betAveen
the quiet shores,
All golden-voiced like radiant girls in muslin
pinafores;
Like little laughing girls who romp adoAvn
some oaken hall,
The rapids gleam like muslined girls, the
river voices call.
The AvilloAV Avands are musical; the reeds
sing weird and low ;
The breeze takes up the harp of Earth ; the
river draws her bow,
And from the platform of the hill, the stage
of slope and glen,
There rings the wonder-message for the ears
of simple men ;
For simple men who hear the hymn of
Beauty chanted loud
By flowers and pearl-grey butterflies and
tinted wisps of cloud.
And now the warm earth slowly tilts its
fretted edge and spills
A deep day-draught of mellow sun behind the
lifting hills,
And, tilting, dips Avith thirsty rim within the
fount of Night,
Brimming Avith stars and mystery before the
charmM sight;
AVhile lisping ’round the pebble-place the
silvern ripples run,
And the eels come down the river at the
setting of the sun.
The eels come from the reeded beds and
every shadowed place ;
Like fleeting thoughts of sombre things,
they cross the Avater’s face.
The eels come from the willow-shades when
all the Avorld is still,
And here you’ll see a coil of green, and there
a yellow gill,
And here you’ll find a gleaming eye, and
there a shining fin ;
For the eels come doAvn the river when the
night is drawing in.
Grim regiments of forest-trees are standing
rank on rank;
The beeches gleam like nickel rods upon the
further bank.
But every tune the river plays now holds a
sorrow-note;
A tui wakes a wistful song which dies within
her throat;
For joy walks with the twilight out to Avhere
the sun has gone ;
For the eels lead down the rh T er and the
darkness follows on.
And the river, oh the river-tide is harsh
upon its bars!
The gaunt eels leave the haunted shades to
hunt beneath the stars ;
Dark shadows deep within the deep, they’re
passing, passing by,
Like echoes from the moaning trees or flecks
of wintry sky;
And the Night is stealing, stealing o’er the
weary heart o’ me,
While coil and coil the river Avrithes in
darkness towards the sea.
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
“Predestination, Faith and
Works.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CAPTAIN ARCHIBALD McALISTER,
master and owner of the schooner
Bird of Paradise, set sail from the
Trobriands for Samarai. He had traded
long in the Solomons, and his crew were
mostly bad men of Malaita—doubly bad
because they had been the last returned
Kanakas from Queensland under the White
Australia Act, and had a grievance
against white men; trebly bad because they
had been half-Christianised. That which the
missionary begins with adult Kanakas the
Devil finishes.
These deportees had returned from Queens-
land to Malaita with much “trade,” and the
trade ivas not hymn-books. Rather was it
extraordinary loud lingerie for dark ladies
previously clothed in a suit of cocoanut-oil,
and these ladies kicked through the filmies.
the madapolam, the Swiss embroidery and
the torchon in two hours by a fast clock.
The trade, furthermore, included accordions,
mouth-organs, walking-sticks, spectacles for
eyes that could look at the sun by noonday;
white suits that became as cheese-cloth with
the first rain; tobacco, and tobacco, and
again tobacco; canned goods and things of
which the Australian Government made ex-
port penal—shotguns, rifles, revolvers,
ammunition and dynamite. Yet, there are
always mean whites to supply the black,
brown or any other sort of enemy with
weapons for the killing of white men or any
other sort of man ; for as a nail sticketh on
the joints of the floor, so doth roguery stick
between buying and selling.
They had fired off all their ammunition
on Malaita, had pawned the last gun for a
little food and advertisement, had exploded
their dynamite to the damage of many fish
and one village. And at long last they had
been signed on before the Government Resi-
dent by McAlister, and driven to sea as
white men and better men than they have
been by the whips of poverty on the land.
Off watch they gambled all day and sang
hymns at night, dolorous howls of Moody
and Sankey replacing the harmony of the
songs of their villages; sailing on and under
and through beauty they could not see; San
Cristoval and Guadalcanal-; up the splendid
channel to Nedrai, where grass-tree, jungle,
lawyer-vine, palm and creeper grow from sea
edge to the blue black mass of forest on the
foothills. They threaded little straits between
patches of palm-covered sand, with surf
breaking lazily in long sluggish curves;
black-green islands came to them and went
astern; black canoes, graceful as the gon-
dolas of Venice, floated up to golden beaches
as if they had volition and intelligence; and
the long-tailed bosun-bird flew over all.
Past all these islands went the Bird of Para-
dise —a sulky ship. Convolvuli, croton and
celandine charmed them not, nor the sight of
electric-blue fish on pink and grey coral. The
loveliness and the divine air called for drift-
ing ; the pigeons in the island woods cooed
drowsiness; yet the Bird of Paradise sailed
through it all, Gavutu to Gizo and the Short-
lands and on to the Trobriands.
Such of the crew as were returned boys,
and they were many, seduced the unsophisti-
cated ; lectured them against giving a fail-
deal to the master; told them stories of the
wages they had known in Queensland plan-
tations before deportation—thirty shillings
a week and food and lodging; sneered at the
standard island rate of ten shillings a month
as a half-loaf, worse than no bread. They
found the native labor content and taught
them, in the manner of the travelled, the
damnable doctrine of “Ca’ canny”; for to
Australia and the Coral Sea agitators are
not indigenous, and have to be imported.
Glibly they told the untravelled native of
the missionary’s promise: “Missionary boy
be go longa top side; white man he go down
alonga fire altogether too much.”
Atta, the ringleader and bush-lawyer of
them all, a big, ugly, black .and brown man,
slippery with cocoanut-oil, his skin smelling
as if his daily diet were shark long deceased,
and made more brutal of appearance by
reason of the triangular piece cut out of his
upper lip. was almost openly mutinous. Once,
receiving an order from McAlister with open
contempt, McAlister, for the sake of disci-
pline and the safety of the ship, at the
moment practically in irons, knocked Atta
down and booted him up again. Atta was
afraid to show open resentment; he merely
went to his duty muttering: “White man he
no savvy alonga Malaita man; Malaita man
he savvv everything too much.”
McAlister did not care to follow up the
insolence, being a man who knew well how
to let sleeping dogs lie. He but said aloud to
himself: “An ugly countenance yon, but I
ken they’d a’ run a dead-heat for the ugly
stakes.”
So up to the Trobriands went the Bird of
Paradise', a sulky ship; and Archie McAlis-
ter was glad to find another schooner, the
Crest of the Wave, his friend Foulmouth
Binns master, ready to be his consort south
to Samarai.
“’Tis a feckless crew I got yon, Foul-
mouth,” said McAlister, “an’ I’m verra glad
o’ a consorrt; forbye ma black sons o hell
are deporrtees fra Bund.vberg,. an’ the dam’
mishinries ha’ leffit them their ain devilry
an’ taught them the white man’s, tew. Mon,
there’s a great muddrer leaden’ ’em —a g’rilla
of a mon wi’ a scollop earmarked _oot o his
tope lup an’ twa big buck teeth itchin’ for
bluid on them. Foulmouth Binns, mon.
fower times atween Gizo an’ Kulamadau I
see him grinnin’ at me ahint the mast; an’
fower times I quelled him wi’ the carrm glint
o' a Gallowgate eye. But when I turrn ma
back I can feel his devil’s een tryin’ to burrn
a hole atween ma shoulther-blades; an’ I
ken his fingers itch to slip a wee knife atween
ma rubs. So it’s gude to hae a consorrt,
though he mightna be able to help me; it’s
comfortin’ ta think there’s a frien’.on the
high seas wi’ ye, even tho’ ye see him on’y
whiles on the tope o’ a sea to wind’ard. ’
Mainly in adjectives did Foulmouth Binns
state his solution of the difficulty, of Atta.
It included such exercises as putting a fist
down Atta’s throat and turning him inside
out, then leaving him in that darkness and
discomfort to turn his own skin to the out-
side again. Further, Foulmouth Binns
offered for two bottles of squareface to force
Atta’s feet into his own mouth and make him
eat himself from the toe upwards. But
McAlister rejected these extreme methods
for the settlement of disputes between Labor
and Capital; he inclined to Arbitration
(with a handspike) and Conciliation (with
a rope’s end and a heavy square-toed
Cossack boot administered with great force
to Atta’s ribs). He did not wish to damage
him too much, he said, nor lose him, as Atta
when in the humor was a fine worker.
Foulmouth considered McAlister soft-
hearted, and stated his belief in slang as
vivid as it was obscene. But when they left
Dobu, Foulmouth kept the Crest of the
Wave close on the course of the Bird of
Paradise, and McAlister was comforted by
his nearness; and. always kindly to his
crew except in the defence of discipline and
his own skin, he was that day more than
usually so.
All day they sailed together steadily
south; and ><:en the thick violet moonless
night closed in on them, McAlister saw the
masthead-light of the Crest of the Wave, and
it seemed even more friendly than a con-
sort’s light at sea. Foulmouth Binns saw
the stern lantern of the heavily-laden Bird
of Paradise gleaming as a wave lifted her
stern, and waited for its reappearance when
it sank as the ship’s counter thundered the
seas in the night. The night became filled
with stars; planet on planet, galaxy and
constellation, light without haste, heat or
passion falling softly to the sea like dew
upon the land. Fore and after masts cut it
with two spearpoints of black ; the full sails
blotted it like clouds. The immensity of it
all, the ruffled liquid plane of blackness torn
to phosphorescence by the rushing forefoot,
uplifted the heart of McAlister, still with
the religion of his ancestry—deep down, but
there. He stood by the wheel and watched
the wavering light of his consort; turned
and saw ahead the forefoot of the Bird of
Paradise rise like a rearing horse as if to
paw the sky, and the schooner slide down
again with her dirty weed-grown bottom
out of the water and her old stern lifting
for a look at the stars.
Something of childhood came back to
him as old and seemingly forgotten happi-
ness comes to the miserable when most
weary of life—which is when a disillusioned
man finds himself so strong that he almost
despairs of dying. He found himself mut-
tering the words of an old hymn as if he
were back in Sunday-school at Parkhead
or on the Kelvin side. He began very
reverently:—
“Code imives in a masteereeus weey
His wunners to perform.

Tut, tut, I clean forgote the line—-
An’ rides upon the storrm.

He walked the deck of his ill-balanced
empire until the four bells of ten o’clock
sounded; then he put Sualefatoi to the

aim keep a star —the lower pointer of
le , Cross dead ahead and to hand the star
and the wheel over to his reliefs; and then
he went to the little cabin, got into very
tun pyjamas and turned in and slept the
sleep of that good digestion which produces
an easy conscience.
He awoke an hour or more after midnight
leeling that all was not well. A mist had
come up on the sea and flattened it; the
yellow smoke curled forward. McAlister
took a glance astern for his consort and saw
not the masthead light of the Crest of the
u av ?j ! nit Heta Centauri astern when it

curtain of the mist dropped behind the ship
at aVi last star was smudged out.
McAlister went to the wheel and found it
pendulismg to the pull of the rudder now
helpless to the sea—two spokes to port, two
spokes to starboard, and back again. By
the sick glimmer of the binnacle light he
saw a black mass at the foot of the wheel
stanchions—Atta, the relieving steersman,

ii.'to sensibility; then demanded why the

And leave the star asturrn and turn the

Why you do that one?”
Sail one way all right—sail ’nother way
at a i 1 , re Pli e d Atta insolently, and
McAlister drove at the grinning face with
both fists and dropped Atta to the deck.
Ihen he turned and leaned over the stern-
rail trying to guess position.
The warm fog shrouded the world and
made him seem the ghost of himself; the
lsird of Paradise became a shadow ship,
made of black lace and crape; the schooner
had no way but that of the sluggish cur-
rent, the very ghost of a vagrant tide;
there was no motion of air or sea; the fog
was soft _ and opaque as silk—the world in
euthanasia, in an air like that of the room
ot a man a-dying!
As McAlister leaned over Atta moved
sat up, remembered and crawled silently to
MnAr? 1 ' i S '! (ldon,y he arose, lifted

McAlister, in his haste to cry out to the

f.f tlie 1 5 ,0ra l k ea - As he came to the sur-
nnff’ „f he ? 1 f ht ’ as if to annoy him, sent a
s?® S f wind—short and irregular as light-

show him for a moment the Bird of Para-
dise, her sails hanging limply and blackly,
drawing away from her master, borne in the
hands of the hidden tide. McAlister called
for help, and saw Atta’s arm raised as if to
strike him down to the sea-floor. The Bird
of Paradise drew away as if the current that
bore ship and man sped the ship faster than
the man could drift. Then the yellow cur-
tains of the fog closed again, and McAlister
was alone, a white-bearded, bald old man
made of steel wire and whipcord, and so far
from fear that all he could feel was a blind
rage against Atta, not for attempting to kill
him so much as for the insolence of jettison-
ing him from his own ship as if he were a
rotten bunch of bananas. Mechanically he
had been swimming after the Bird of Para-
dise now vanished in the fog, and, feeling
that he must husband his strength for the
unknown number of hours that were to come
before- death or rescue, he turned over on
his back and floated on the almost imper-
ceptible rising and falling of the sea.
The fog came within less than a foot of
the water, so that it seemed to rest an inch
or two above his face; he could see nothing
but the deep mist covering him lightly and
warmly as a quilt of eiderdown. Its near-
ness became oppressive; as h e stared up at
the fog it seemed to settle closer to him ; he
turned on his breast again, glad that the
fog was on the back of his head and not so
immediately before his eyes. He felt no
danger of cramping, for the tropic sea was
thick and warm; but suddenly he thought
of sharks, turned in the water to meet the
treacherous, sneaking enemy face to face,
and suddenly remembered that in the deep
there is neither back nor front, nor east nor
west, but only an element producing an
offence more mobile than the eagle in the
air. The hair on his neck bristled ivith
horror. He had been overboard often in
shark waters, but never without an ark of
refuge to swim to; and now his ark had
drawn into the fog and he was hopelessly
alone. lie thought of praying and seeking
to end the suspense; welcoming death, as
the man who sees it inevitable and fears it
less than the torture that must come first.
I hen he put prayer from him as cowardice,
seeing the ways his life had known, and. in
that mind, began to fight again for life,
swimming as if in a race and calling loudly
to the mist.
Something brushed past him in the water.
He resigned himself to death, and swooned.
* * * *
Foulmouth Binns listened to McAlister*s
story and grinned. Atta, with a bleeding
back from the rope’s-end wielded by Capt.
McAlister, had been cast off from his com-
pulsory embrace of the fore-mast and now
lay in the fo’c’s’le of the Bird of Paradise,
his jellied flesh still twitching to the pun-
ishment that had passed.
T™ as earl y trainin’,” said Captain
McAlister, that was nigh makin’ me a wee
cock-angel, an’ no man. I swooned at the
ouch o drowned seaweed in the wather;
but twas no’ for long. I came to mysel’ an’
iound ma bones hadna been broacht
by any sharrk, an’ the weariness o’ the mist
was on me; an’ ma lungs seemed to breathe
the foggy air as thick as wather. Ah, Foul-
mouth! Foulmouth Binns! Had ye been
theyah, Gode w’u’d ha’ washed yeer wee
words clean an’ stoppit yeer damnin’ an’
blastin’ an’ bulgruddery in the clean hearrt
he had pit in yeer breast wi’ the fear of the
sharks cornin’ to tak’ a three-pund piece oot
o’ y’.”
Said Foulmouth Binns: “Get on with the
tale.”
“I trembled there before the Lorrd in the
fog; the breath came oot o’ the mist an’
clammy it was an’ the wind o’ death in it;
an’ strange uncanny movings in the wather
below as if the blind de’ils o’ the slime o’
the sea-floor reachet up to tak’ me by the
heels. I couldna’ swim—could only" float
there like a dynamited fish, belly up, alive
an’ swowning. In my half-daft thochts I
thocht o Sun’ay-schule an’ me mither mould-
erin’ in the kirkyard by Castel Cary. I
thocht o’ ma kirk an’ the preacher’s worrds
Predestination, faith and worrks.’ I
hadna ony faith, an’ ma worrks before the
Lord haena been anything to brag aboot,
an’ I wudna like tae see mony o’ them in
the Scotsman or the Glaisgie Herald; an’
for what I was predestined on’y Gode an’
the sharrks might ken.
Then I tried to say a wee bit prayer.
Mon, I couldna be humble! I could on’y
think of the gran’ idea o’ Gode; I could
on y remember : ‘Praise Him all ye hills,
and piaise Him all things that live; praise
Him ye seas an’ all that therein are’ (in-
cludin, o’ course, the dom sharrks) ; ‘praise
Him ye mountains an’ all the threasures o’
the snow.’ Whiles I said to maseP :
(lode muves in a masteereeus weev
His wunners to perforrm!
An’ then I forgot the third wee line; but
maybe the Lorrd doesna’ know any better
liimseP, and the fourth line is ‘An’ rides
u P° n storrm.’ Then the wather was
couldther, the bluid left my feet; I felt I
was slippen oot o’ life like dead wrrack
gangin’ to sea in a spring tide. So I said
to masel : Airchie McAlister, I wilna bear
anither pang; I’ll just say ma wee bit
prayer and pit ma hands ower ma heid, an’
gang doon an’ suffer nae mair.’
“I couldna’ think o’ a prayer, but Gode
cam to me in the wather and brought me
shame. An’ ’stead o’ prayin’ I said to
mysel, oot loud, speakin’ to the fog that was
now toimentit by little winds that stayed
but a minute and were gone: ‘Airchie
McAlister! Ye’re sixty-fower year old. an’
for fifty-eight o’ them y’ haena thocht o’
Bode. Yeve walked yeer ain wild way, an’
had yeer fun an’ yeer wee drammie, an’ yeer
by-drinkin s, an’ never a flower o’ spring-
time passed ye by. Ye’ve walked the worrld
an made the South Seas obscene wi’
screechin an square-face gin; an’ ’twasna’
yeer fault, but a good mither, that made ye
no so bad as Foulmouth Binns.’
- !” said Foulmouth.
“b oulmouth, but cleanheart—an’ Gode
will foigie yeer swearin’ becos ye never
turrned awa’ from a friend. An’ I said:
‘Airchie McAlister, shame to live as ye ha’
done, an’ then greet an’ whimper as soon’s
ye’re, in a wee bit trouble. Die if ye wull to
save torrment, Airchie McAlister, but dinna
think ye’re a marthyr. Dinna pray; Gode
has forgotten the soond o’ yeer voice. Swim
an’ fecht as ye ha’ done all yeer life. Ye
canna pray! Fecht an’’ dinna whimper!’
Mon! wi’ that my hearrrt tightened up its
strings, an’ ma’ sinews steeled, an’ the bluid
ran sweetly to ma feet. Gode knew that
every crupple has his ain way o’ walkin’, an’
that bletherin’ a lot o’ prayer isna’ ma way.
The fog foldet before ma eyes; all the little
gauzies o’ the mist lifted like the eurrtains
o’ the play-actors; an’ a big gowd eye in
the sky to ma left showed me the east. A’
the mists sneaket awa’ before the honest
sunrise; and there was yeer ship, Foul-
mouth Binns, searrchin’ for yeer consort an’
found her masther in the wather.
‘‘Before eight bells we had ma ship again,
an’ Atta has had the flesh cut off his back to
make him a gude bairn. An’ that was
betther than prayer for the resignation to
sink wi’. Better than dein’ ’twas to see the
gowden sunrise, an’ ma mate Foulmouth
Binns; an’ praise God whiles I was cuttin’
to pieces wi’ a rope’s-end the dirrthy
mudderin’ black carcase yon !”
“ !” said Foulmouth Binns.
“ ’Twasna’ that! Faith I had in mysel;
worrks was in my swimmin’ till the dawn ;
an’ predestination brot me back to ma own
ship,. praisin’ God an’ listenin’ to yeer
damnin’ an’ blastin’ and bulgruddery.”
“I’d ha’ gone crook if I well
hadn’t picked you up, Archie,” said Foul-
mouth Binns.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
King.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN one glorious, gleaming hour,
I pluckt Life, the passion-flower
In a musk-rose scented bower.
Prest it to my naked soul,
Felt its sepals round me roll
Like a seraph's aureole.
llow I scorn the world’s derision,
I, who’ve seen Love’s splendid vision!
Gazing in her veiled eyes
I beheld the twilight rise,
Drank the truth beyond the skies !
Holding her dear hand in mine
Humbly knelt at Godhead’s shrine,
Found the Trust and Faith divine.
One with the eternities,
When upon my lips her kiss
Made me free of all that is!
I have drawn from Death the sting ;
I have lost Time’s reckoning;
Love has crowned me. made me King!
N.S.W.
JACK ELIOT.
